MARBHRANN DO UISDEAN A’ BHAILE SHEAR

MO thruaighe bristeadh Shìol Chuinn,
Mo chreach bhur dìobhail ’s bhur sgath,
Sgeul duilich as mór am beud,
Ri fhulang gur geur an gath.

Gur geur an gath gath an aoig,				2695
’N ar taobh a dh’fhalaich e ghuin
’N uair dh’fhuadaicheadh uainn na suinn,
Sgeul as cruaidh ri innse ’n diugh.

’N uair thugadh ar ceann prìseil uainn
Gur mìofhortan cruaidh a bh’ ann				2700
Càch a bhith teàrnadh cho cas
’S a theàrnadh a’ chlach leis a’ ghleann:

Mun tàinig lethbhliadhna slàn
Chaill sinn Fear Bhàlaidh ’s a mhac,
Ar sàr chaiptean Fear an Uird,				2705
Gill-easbuig, gun dùil ri theachd.

Tha fir ’s a’ chaibeal ’n an tàmh,
Iain ’s am Bàillidh air thùs:
Bu deagh chreideas iad air tìr
G’a sheasamh an sìth ’s an cliù.				2710

Nach mór am bristeadh oirnn sin
Cuide ri ’r bristeadh an diugh,
Na h-uiread bharanta mór?—
De bhrollach Chlann Domhnaill na fir.

B’iad sin na fir bu mhór meas					2715
’N ionad nach do sheas an cas,
Air an cluinnte ’n t-iomradh còir
’N àite nach robh ’n eòlas ac’.

[TD 187]

[Beurla]

[TD 188]

Cuideachd is móran dh’an dìth—
Faodaidh iad bhith cruinn air cnoc;				2720
Nach cianail an nochd a’ chuirm—
Ogh’ Iarla Dhùn Tuilm fo bhrot.

Am fìor ghaisgeach dol fo lic
Mu ’n iargaineach iomadh neach,
Sàr churaidh a chaisgeadh tòir,				2725
’S nach cuireadh ob-òb air n-ais,

Nan tigeadh trioblaid ’n ar caraibh
Sheasadh fearail air ar ceann,
Gun mur-a-bhitheadh ri fhaotainn
’N cridhe no ’n gaoirdean no ’n lann.				2730

’N duine bu duin’ air gach càs,
Ionadail càirdeil gun phléid,
Ceannard sluaigh gun tuar bhith sgàthach,
Guala làidir anns an t-streup.

Duine sgairteil tapaidh teòma,				2735
Duine saidhbhir anns gach beairt,
Labhradh an fhìrinn le grunnd,
’S cha b’i ’n teanga leam is leat.

Gur mise chunnaic an uair
’M b’urramach do shnuadh ’s do dhreach,			2740
Fear fearail bu fhlathail gnùis,
Amharc nan sùl nach robh tais.

Calpa foinnidh, sliasaid mheanmnach,
Colainn dhealbhach bu mhór neart;
’M faicinn ’s an gaisge ’s an gnìomh				2745
Bu dìomhal iarla do mhac.

Ceannard dùthcha ’n éirigh chruaidh,
Ceannard sluaigh an éirigh feachd,
Ceann-feadhna nach fuilingeadh spìd,
’S a b’urrainn an t-strìth a chasg.				2750

[TD 189]

[Beurla]

[TD 190]

Duine fearail a dh’fhàs cruaidh,
Duine ’n robh cruadal is smachd,
Duine ’n robh iochd agus truas
Gu fuasgladh air fear ’n a airc;

Duine ’n robh smear agus sgoinn,				2755
Duine nach robh foill ’n a bheachd,
Nach buaileadh a’ bhuille chùil,
’S nach gleidheadh mì-rùn do neach.

An aon mhìle ’s a seachd ciad
Trì fichead bliadhna ’s a naoi					2760
Ghabh Uisdean cridhe chead dìnn;
Trì fichead ’s a trì b’e aois.

Tha sinn a nis air ar leòn
’N fhine mhór bu lìonmhor buaidh;
Ma bha sibh riamh ’n ur cùis fharmaid,			2765
’S an aimsir-sa culaidh thruais.

Craobh a rùisgeadh air gach taobh sinn,
Craobh nach do ghiùlain a mios;
Chan fhan ar luchd aoise beò,
’S cha tig ar daoin’ òga ris.					2770

Seallamaid a nis ri Dia;
’S cinnteach a’ chrìoch dhuinn am bàs;

’S iarramaid Mac Dé mar gheàrd.

FAILTE DO ’N CHLEIR

FÀILTE oirbh!						2785

Fàilte air na bheil ann uile.

’N t-àite ’n cluinnte focal Dhé.				2790

Beannachd Dhia dhuibh far an téid,				2800
Chan fhaod mi fuireach ri mòisean—
’S éiginn dhomhsa dhol ’n a bheinn.

ORAN DO NA FOGARRAICH

Air fonn: Air tuiteam am chadal

Agus cuiribh ur dòchas					2805
Ann an comhnadh an Airdrigh;

Na bhith fòdha mar thràillean;

De bhreacanan càrnaid					2830

[TD 204]

NAIDHEACHDAN

I

AN uair a bha e ’n a ghille òg chaidh MacCodrum a dh’ionnsaigh banais àraid. Tha a h-uile coltas gur h-e a’ bhanais mu ’n do rinn e Duan na Bainnse a bha ann. An latha an deaghaidh na bainnse dh’fhaighneachd athair dheth ciod e a’ bhanais a bha aca an raoir. “Innsidh mise sin dhuibh,” arsa Iain; “beagan arain is móran uime (ime) is pailteas uisge-na-beatha.” Cha do thuig athair ciod e bha e ag ciallachadh, agus thuirt e, “An da, bha banais an sin dha-rìribh.” Dh’fhalbh Iain, agus is ann an uair sin a dh’innis a mhàthair do Fhearchar mar a bha dà thaobh air an rud a thuirt a mhac.


II

Thachair do ’n bhàrd a bhith aon latha aig biadh comhla ri griasaiche agus fear a bhiodh a’ dèanamh chliabh. A réir coltais, cha b’ionann dòigh ithe do ’n bhàrd is do ’n dithis eile—cha robh aige ach forc nan cóig meur; agus is ann a chionn sin a thuirt e:

“Sgian fhada gheur aig a’ ghriasaiche,
’S corc aig fear dhèanamh nan cliabh:
’S mise ’n so le m’ dhà làimh loma,
’S mi ’n comaidh riutha mu ’n bhiadh.”


III

Thàinig Mac Mhaighstir Alasdair a choimhead air MacCodrum an uair a bha e fuireach ann an t-Hoghgearraidh. Thachair e ri Iain beagan shlatan bho ’n dorus, ach cha do dh’aithnich e idir e. Thuirt e ris,

[TD 205]

[Beurla]

[TD 206]

“An aithne dhuit Iain MacCodrum?” “Is aithne glé mhath,” arsa Iain. “Cia mar a gheibh mi an tigh aige?” arsa Alasdair. “Aithnichidh tu e,” arsa Iain, “an uair a chì thu tigh agus tughadh air an dara taobh dheth is gun tughadh air an taobh eile idir; agus an uair a théid thu stigh, chì thu bean a’ fuine air lethlaimh.” “Bheil fhios agad a bheil e fhéin a stigh?” dh’fhaighneachd Alasdair. “Ma ta, bha e stigh an uair a bha mise, is cha do rinn mi ach tighinn a mach.” “Caithidh mi an oidhche mar ris,” arsa Alasdair, “mas àbhaist aoighean a bhith aige.” “Tha mi ag creidsinn,” arsa Iain, “nach bi e falamh dhiubh sin cuideachd ma bhios na cearcan a’ breith.” Mu dheireadh tha e coltach gun do dh’aithnich Alasdair có bha aige.


IV

Bha MacCodrum aon uair air fear de sgiobadh bàta Uibhistich a chaidh gu ruige Tobar-Mhoire. An uair a ràinig iad, thàinig cuid de mhuinntir a’ bhaile a nuas chun na laimrig. Thuirt fear ri MacCodrum, “Co as a thug sibh an t-iomradh?” “Thug as na gàirdeanan,” arsa MacCodrum. “An ann bho thuath a thàinig sibh?” arsa an duine. “Pàirt bho thuath is pàirt bho thighearnan,” arsa am bàrd.


V

Thàinig muc-mhara a bha anabarrach fhéin mór air tìr air a’ chladach, agus bha a h-uile duine riamh a’ dol sìos a choimhead oirre agus nach fhac iad a samhail ri an latha. Chaidh MacCodrum e fhéin sìos g’a coimhead. An uair a bha e a’ tilleadh thachair fear ris a dh’fhaighneachd dheth, “An ann bho ’n mhuic a thàinig tu?” “Thàinig mi,” arsa MacCodrum, “bho mhnaoi a b’fheàrr na do mhàthair, a thrusdair. Co as a thàinig tu fhéin?” “Thàinig mise,” arsa an duine, “as an Uachdar.” “Ma ta,” fhreagair MacCodrum, “is ann mar sin as dòcha na coin ’g ad imlich.”

[TD 207]

[Beurla]

[TD 208]

VI

Choinnich am bàrd latha ri seann duine air an robh e eòlach an uair a bha e òg. Air do ’n bhàrd faighneachd dheth ciamar a bha e—bha an duine, a réir coltais, ’n a gheòcaire—thuirt an duine gu robh e air a stamag a chall. “Ma ta,” arsa Iain, “is math leam nach mi fhuair i!”


VII

Ri linn MhicCodrum bha dà cheisteir an Uibhist a Tuath—Alasdair MacDhomhnaill, am bàrd, ris an abradh iad an Dall Muileach no an Dall Mór, agus an Ceisteir Crotach. Chan ’eil coltas gu robh an dithis miadhail sam bith aig a chéile. Bhiodh an Dall Muileach a’ falbh is gille beag aige g’a threòrachadh. Latha bha siod thachair e ri MacCodrum, agus dé ach is ann a thuirt an gille ris gur h-e an Ceisteir Crotach a bha ann. An sin thuirt an Dall Muileach:

“Fàilt’ ort fhéin a bhràthair ceisteir
Dhèanadh an ceasnachadh mìn;
Cha tig an latha gheibh thu leannan
Leis a’ char a tha ’n ad dhruim;
Mar mheallas am maide ’n cùbair
’N am a bhith lùbadh a chinn,
’S ionann sin ’s mo bhràthair ceisteir,
Toil gun fhreasdal aig a mhnaoi.”

Fhreagair MacCodrum:

“Carson a dh’aoir thu ’n Ceisteir Crotach,			2935
Duine cho bochd ’s tha ’s an dùthaich,
’S ged bhiodh e falbh air a’ bhata,
B’fheàrr e na claigeann gun sùilean;
Chunnaic mi t’athair air bàta—
Chan fhaigheadh e gu h-àrd a stiuireadh;			2940
Dh’itheadh e uiread ri dithis—
Ma-ruibhe! ged theirinn triuir ris.”

[TD 209]

[Beurla]

[TD 210]

VIII

Bha muinntir an Taoibh an Iar, fo riaghladh Alasdair mhic Dhomhnaill, bàillidh na dùthcha, air a bhith leigeadh Loch Hàstain am Paibil. Cha do nochd MacCodrum gus an robh an obair seachad, agus an uair a ràinig e bha buideal uisge-bheatha an déis a tholladh a dh’ùrachadh an luchd-oibreach. Thairg am bàillidh glaine dha: “So, Iain, sin agad glaine de bhùrn Loch Hàstain.” Fhreagair am bàrd agus a’ ghlaine ’n a làimh:

“Gum beannaicheadh Dia bùrn Loch Hàstain—
Ge math fhàileadh ’s fheàrr a bhlas;
’S ma bha e mar so gu léir					2945
Bu mhór am beud a leigeil as.”


IX

An uair a bha MacCodrum a’ fuireach ann an Langais, rinn e deiseil aon latha gu falbh a choimhead air Fear Bhaile Raghnaill agus air na càirdean air taobh an iar na dùthcha. Smaoinich a bhean gun gabhadh i an cothrom an uair a dh’fhalbhadh Iain gu cuirm bheag a dhèanamh do bhoireannaich eile a bha anns a’ choimhearsnachd. Bha amharus aig Iain gu robh rudeiginn air bonn ged nach do leig e dad air, ach an uair a bha e tuaiream mìle no dhà bho ’n tigh thill e. Anns na làithean a bha siod bhiodh daoine a’ rùsgadh nan tighean a h-uile h-earrach agus ag cur an tughaidh mar thodhar air an fhearann. So an dearbh obair a bha aig Iain aig an am, agus bha dara ceann an tighe gun sgrath gun chonnlaich. Dhìrich e an àird air an tobhtaidh agus leig e e fhéin sìos troimh na cabair. Bha na boireannaich cruinn mu ’n bhòrd ag òl mogain agus abair gun do chlisg iad. “Na caraichibh, na caraichibh,” arsa Iain, “cha do rinn mise ach tilleadh a dhèanamh an altachaidh dhuibh.”


X

Bha am bàrd agus a bhean trang as t-earrach a’ feamanadh leis na cléibh. Latha a bha siod thàinig feamainn gu cladach ach chuir Iain dàil ann a bhith dol

[TD 211]

[Beurla]

[TD 212]

g’a h-iarraidh agus mun do ghluais e thàinig an làn agus atharrachadh anns a’ ghaoith agus dh’fhalbh an fheamainn. Cha robh a bhean a’ leigeil bial a Iain ach ag caoidh na feamad—“Dh’fhalbh an fheamainn, dh’fhalbh an fheamainn,” agus móran eile air an aon duan. An uair a fhuair Iain cothrom facal a ràdh, thuirt e, “Ma dh’fhalbh a’ bhuidheag, dh’fhuirich a’ ghleadhrach!”


XI

An uair a bha am bàrd ann an Langais bhiodh e deònach a’ bhliadhna ùr a thoirt a stigh anns an dòigh àbhaisteach comhla ri feadhainn de na nàbuidhean, agus aon uair choinnich e fhéin agus Ruairidh mac Iain agus Iain mac Gille-Chrìosd aig Abhainn Fhiodaidh a dh’òl slàinte a chéile aig toiseach na bliadhna. An uair a bha iad air dol gu math ris a’ bhotul, thoisich Iain mac Gille-Chrìosd ri dhol air mhìothapadh le daoraich. “Chan ’eil sìon a dh’fhios agam,” arsa esan, “ciod e nì mi no their mi.” “Ma ta,” arsa am bàrd, “tha agamsa:

“Na trì chinn a dh’fhàg an t-earchall
Chaidh iad air thearbadh dh’ an mhòintich:
Caillear mise gun fhios dhu’sa,
’S caillear thusa gun fhios dhomhsa.”				2950


XII

Sheinn am bàrd Oran a’ Bhonn-a-sia aon uair ann an tigh àraid, agus an uair a bha e ullamh thug a’ chuideachd gu gàireachdaich is a h-uile duine cho riaraichte a chionn mar a ghread e Ruairidh Bhòrlain. An sin chuir Iain rann eile ris an òran, ag gabhail lethsgeul Ruairidh:

Faire faire dhaoin’ uaisle,
Cuim’ nach gluaiseadh sibh stòlda?—
’S ann tha Ruairidh ’n a bhantraich
Agus clann air tighinn òg air;
’S gum bi dubhar na h-oidhche				2955
Cur nam mìltean gu dórainn,
Cur nan loingeas gu cladach
Far nach faic iad an t-seòlaid.

[TD 213]

[Beurla]

[TD 214]

XIII

Bha am bàrd air chuairt ann an Uibhist a’ Chinn a Deas, agus chaidh e a choimhead air an t-sagart an Staghlaigearraidh. An uair a chaidh e stigh, bha an sagart a’ sgrìobhadh agus ag gabhail pìob thombaca. Chunnaic e MacCodrum ’n a sheasamh anns an dorus, agus thuirt e ris gum feumadh e rann a dhèanamh dha mun suidheadh e. Thuirt Iain:

“An rud sin nach fhaca mi riamh,
’S mi air liathadh ’n déis bhith òg—				2960
Sagart ag gabhail a phìoba
’S e sgrìobhadh le ite geoidh.”


XIV

Thug MacCodrum sgrìob do ’n Eilean Sgitheanach agus thadhail e aig tigh Chinnseborgh. Cha do dh’aithnich na searbhantan e agus cha tug iad na b’fhaide na an citsin-dubh e. Bha fhios aig té dhiubh gur h-ann a Uibhist a thàinig e, agus thuirt i ris, “Nach do chaochail Mac ’ic Ailein?” “Mur do chaochail,” arsa Iain, “rinn iad an eucoir—thiodhlaic iad e.” “Ciod e,” arsa ise, “cho fad ’s a bha e os cionn talmhainn?” “Bha,” arsa Iain, “trì fichead bliadhna ’s a deich.” Bha am bàrd cho leamh riutha agus gun do ghabh na searbhantan gu h-olc e. Leig dithis aca orra gun deach iad far a chéile agus thòisich iad air sgiùrsadh a chéile leis na bataichean; ach bha a h-uile buille a bu truime a’ bualadh—gun fhios daibh nam b’fhìor—air Iain. Có a thàinig a nuas a choimhead ciod e an ùbarraid a bha dol air aghaidh ach Fionnghal fhéin. Thionndaidh i fàd as an teine agus dh’aithnich i MacCodrum. Chaidh an uair sin gabhail ris gu fialaidh. Mun do dh’fhalbh Iain sheinn e dhi fhéin is dh’ a céile an t-òran a rinn e dhaibh, agus thug iad dha cóig puinnd Shasunnach a làimh an urra.


XV

Bha MacCodrum latha ann am Beinn-na-fadhla, agus thadhail e ann an tigh Phàdruig MhicNeacail am Baile nan

[TD 215]

[Beurla]

[TD 216]

Cailleach. Cha d’fhuair e de bhiadh no dheoch ach aon chuach bhrochain; agus is ann an sin a thuirt e ris a’ bhrochan:

“Math thu fhéin a bhrochain
Gun mhudha gun bhrachan,
Math thu fhéin a bhrochain					2965
Gun dhath dubh gun sgealpan,
Math thu fhéin a bhrochain—
Ailean Chlann Mhic Neacail.”


XVI

An uair a bha MacCodrum air leabaidh a’ bhàis chaidh Iomhar, mac a bhràthar (?), a choimhead air. Bha Iomhar ’n a dhuine a bha anabarrach seòlta; dhèanadh Iain an gnothuch air an uair a bhiodh iad aig bearradaireachd, ach far am biodh na cuilbheartan a chum feum is ann aig Iomhar a bhiodh a’ bhuinnig, mar a fhuair am fear eile a mach uair is uair aig an am a bha iad ’n an nàbuidhean an t-Hoghgearraidh. Ach co dhiùbh, sheas Iomhar aig taobh na leapa is thuirt e, “A bheil thu ’g am aithneachadh?” Dh’fhosgail am bàrd a shùilean is choimhead e air. “An da,” arsa esan, “cha do dh’aithnich duine thusa a riamh.” Bha Iomhar an uair sin air son faighinn a mach an robh Iain a’ fàgail móran anns an tiomnadh. “Tha mi,” arsa Iain, “a’ fàgail na chuireas fo ’n talamh mi gu h-onarach.”

[TD 231]

NOTES


<gai>DUAN NA BAINNSE<eng>

The chief interest of this song lies in the fact that it was MacCodrum’s first effort in verse. It may perhaps be dated as early as c. 1712. He and two other youths appeared at a wedding in the Paible district without having received an invitation—a serious breach of convention which was apt to lead to unfortunate consequences for the offenders. The bard and his friends, however, were more or less ignored. As has been pointed out (UB., ix), three divisions were at that time usual at such gatherings. The living-room or “ben” was occupied by the wedding party and near relatives of the bride who had been present at the marriage ceremony <gai>(an luchd comhailteachd),<eng> the schoolmaster, and sometimes the officiating minister. The general body of guests partook of the wedding feast in the “butt,” where the cattle were then usually housed. It often happened that this accommodation was insufficient, and some of the guests, young lads for the most part, had to be content with eating and drinking outside. The uninvited guests on the occasion in question hoped to escape detection among this crowd. But they were recognised by the miller as intruders and not given even a dram. The song, lampooning the wedding, and the bridegroom in particular, was composed in retaliation for this treatment. The author prudently kept his identity undisclosed, but the song itself became well known and caused great offence to the persons against whom it was directed. The discovery by John’s father that the song was his son’s composition, and its consequences, are discussed in the Introduction.

1. <gai>Paibil:<eng> anglicised Paible; a general name for the district between the loch of the same name that adjoins Balranald (made an arm of the sea shortly after MacCodrum’s death by a cutting called <gai>An Dìg Mhór<eng>) and Bayhead <gai>(Ceann a’ Bhàigh).<eng> It is the most populous district in Uist. The pronunciation in the eighteenth century, to judge from the rime with <gai>maduinn<eng> (cf. also l. 617 ff.), differed from that of the present day—<gai>Peabail<eng>—and was the same as the present Lewis pronunciation of the same name (but anglicised Bayble).

9. <gai>an teine:<eng> probably a fire built for the benefit of those guests entertained in the open.

[TD 232]

11. <gai>am muilleir:<eng> the miller was perhaps the father of the bride in whose house the wedding festivities would take place. In 1792 there were three mills in the parish of North Uist (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 304, 313). These were probably the same that are still standing, at Loch nan Geirean, Tigharry, and Dusary, of which the last named is the only one now in working order. Millers do not appear to have been popular in the eighteenth century. Private grinding was prohibited by a law of Alexander II, and millers were empowered to seek out querns and destroy them. A raid for this purpose was carried out in South Uist c. 1750, when a large number of querns were thrown into the sea (Clan Donald, iii, 130).

17. <gai>Domhnall mac Gille-Mhàrtuinn:<eng> He was the bridegroom, who had to supply the whisky, and therefore did not welcome an addition to the original number of guests.

<gai>Gille-Màrtuinn<eng> is here aspirated in the genitive in both its component parts. But the second part was often not aspirated. Cf. l. 2858 which follows S., and l. 2422 which follows M. In the North Uist Rental of 1718 both “Mcillickallum” and “McGillechallum” are to be found. In the same Rental one Annable McEan vic illimartin appears as one of the tenants of Balemore. The only other instance of the name is in Baile Mhic Phàil, where Killmartin Munro is a tenant (Clan Donald, iii, 662).

49ff. Cf. 1199 ff.


<gai>ORAN MU ’N EIDEADH GHÀIDHEALACH<eng>

In T. the title is <gai>“Oran le Iain MacCodrum, agus e bhi diombach air son an eididh Ghaidhealaich thoirt uaithe, ’n deigh bliadhna Thearlach.”<eng> In U. it is called <gai>“Oran a Rinn saighdear a bh’ann sa n, arm dhearg an am do na chasag a bhi aig arm an (airm<eng> written above) <gai>Righ (Deors<eng> written above),” which does not accord with the content of the song nor with the fact that soldiers were not prohibited from wearing the Highland dress. In EP. the heading is simply <gai>“An Aghaidh an Eididh Ghallda Le Iain MacCodrum,”<eng> and the same heading appears in GB. The song seems to have been widely known, and—no doubt as a consequence—the various versions differ considerably. T., the earliest and fullest, is here followed, except where considerations such as metre and assonance and meaning have led to the adoption of readings from the later versions.

[TD 233]

The song was composed when the Highland dress was proscribed after the rebellion of 1745-1746. This was done by an act passed in 1746 called “An Act for the more effectual disarming of the Highlands in Scotland; and for the more effectually securing the Peace of the said Highlands; and for restraining the Use of the Highland Dress … (further provisions of the act).” The clause relating to the Highland dress was to take effect from and after 1st August 1747. The proscription applied only to Scotland and an exception was made in favour of “Officers and Soldiers in his Majesty’s Forces” (19 Geo. 2, c. 17). By an act of the following year, however, the enforcement of this clause, “as to all those who are not landed Men,” was postponed until 1st August 1748 (20 Geo. 2, C. 51). In 1748 a further postponement, “as to the Plaid, Philibeg or Little Kilt” until 25th December 1748, and as regards the rest of the dress, until 1st August 1749 was enacted (21 Geo. 2, c. 34). MacCodrum’s song was composed probably not long after the former date.

The Highland dress was rigidly banned until 1757 (Stewart of Garth’s Sketches of the Highlanders, 123). The people were made to take the following oath that they were obeying the law:

“I, A. B., do swear, and as I shall answer to God at the great day of judgment, I have not, nor shall have in my possession any gun, sword, pistol, or arm whatever, and never use tartan, plaid, or any part of the Highland garb; and if I do so, may I be cursed in my undertakings, family, and property, may I never see my wife and children, father, mother, or relations, may I be killed in battle as a coward, and lie without Christian burial in a strange land, far from the graves of my forefathers and my kindred; may all this come across me if I break my oath” (Ibid., 121).

For other songs on the same subject see list in BGh., 278-279. To that list may be added a song by John Maclean of Inverscadel (Iain mac Theàrlaich Oig), published in G.B., ii, 193.

57. The first four lines of this verse are quoted by John Morrison at the beginning of Am Millennium as follows:—

<gai>Tha mi tursach tinn
Tha mi sgìth dochart,
Ceangal air mo bhuill
Us cha dean mi coiseachd.<eng>
(Henderson’s Dàin Iain Ghobha, ii, 38.)

[TD 234]

63. <gai>beul sìos:<eng> common in the imprecation <gai>Beul sìos ort!<eng> By one authority (UB., 105) connected with “the tendency of the underlip—especially in children—hanging down when a person is in sorrow.” The imprecation, however, is stronger than that, meaning “Mayest thou fall down dead.” The dead in battle would be found usually face downwards and such was the regular posture of death in the old tales; cf. the English phrase “to bite the dust.” Cf. also <gai>An fhìrinn ’sa beoil foipe sìos<eng> (BGh., l. 2342 n.); <gai>Marbhphaisg ort!<eng> (Death-shroud on thee); <gai>Geòlach ort!<eng> (Death-shoulder-bands on thee). It may be noted that according to one account Mary MacLeod gave orders on her death-bed that she should be buried face downwards as a punishment for having forgiven the chief who banished her and for having composed a song in his praise (Henderson’s Dàin Iain Ghobha, 1, xiv). Cf. the death of Airnélach mac Adhmalláin, son of the king of Leinster, as told to St Patrick by Caoilte: <gai>“tuc a aigid re talmain 7 nir’ thócaib a ghnúis nogu bhfuair bás do náire.”<eng> (Stokes and Windisch, Acallam na Senórach, Irische Texte, Fourth Series, i, ll. 1072-1073.) See also l. 1971.

67. <gai>an t-osan geàrr:<eng> Taylor, the water poet, describing the dress of Highlanders in 1618, says, “Their habite is shooes with but one sole a-piece; stockings (which they call short hose) made of a warme stuff of divers colours, which they call tartane” (quoted in Skene’s Highlanders of Scotland, 2nd ed., 148).

86. <gai>deich slatan singillte:<eng> the amount of cloth required for the belted plaid. See BGh., l. 1835 n.

92. The reference is no doubt to King George II.

109. <gai>Breacan air am féile:<eng> part of the tartan was pleated to make the kilt every time the belted plaid was put on. In an old song, <gai>Gillean an Fhéilidh,<eng> occur the lines—

<gai>Tha mo cheist-sa air gallain
Nan cuilbheirean glasa:
Guma slàn thig iad dachaidh
Fo ’m breacan an éileadh.<eng>

For other examples see BGh., l. 1847 n.

112. <gai>Nach éisd iad sradag:<eng> they do not listen to a spark, i.e. they do not misfire.

119. <gai>Còta geàrr:<eng> Lesly, writing in 1578 of the dress worn by Highlanders, has the following—“Reliqua vero vestimenta erant

[TD 235]

brevis ex lana tunicella manicis inferius apertis …” (The rest of their garments consisted of a short woollen jacket with the sleeves open below …) (quoted in Skene’s Highlanders of Scotland, 2nd ed., 144).

120. <gai>sléisdean nochdte:<eng> in 1512 John Major remarks of the Highlanders, “A medio crure ad pedem caligas non habent … frequenter nudis tibiis sub cruribus …” (From the middle of the thigh to the foot they have no covering for the leg … their legs are frequently naked under the thigh …) (quoted in Skene’s Highlanders of Scotland, 2nd ed., 145). Sacheveril, writing in 1688, mentions the same feature, saying that “Their thighs are bear, with brawny muscles” (quoted ibid., 144).

131. <gai>lannan Spàinneach:<eng> frequently mentioned by bards of the clan period. See BGh., l. 1858 n. Swords were also made in the Highlands. The MacDonalds of Sleat had as smiths and armourers a family of MacRurys whose office was hereditary, and who had the smiths’ pennylands of Baile Mhic Gille-riabhaich in Trotternish in return for their services. A branch of the family officiated in the same capacity in North Uist. One of them, who learnt the art of working in iron and of farriery from his father as it has been handed down by the family tradition, is smith in the township of Malacleit at the present time.

159. <gai>Deòrsa:<eng> King George II already mentioned.

162. <gai>am prionnsa òg:<eng> Frederick Louis, Prince of Wales, born 6th January 1707. Flora MacDonald is said to have been set at liberty through his intercession. Died 20th March 1751 as the result of an accident while playing tennis (Dict. Nat. Biog., vii, 675 ff.). A Latin poem on his death was sent by Donald Roy MacDonald of Baleshare to Bishop Forbes (Lyon in Mourning, iii, 89).

163. <gai>Teàrlach:<eng> Prince Charles Edward. The bard forgot, or ignored, the fact that his father, whom Jacobites regarded as King James III, was still alive (he died in 1766), and would become king in the event of his prophecy being fulfilled.

170. <gai>Cóig ceud mìle Frangach:<eng> the Jacobite policy was to overthrow the House of Hanover with the help of the French, who in the eighteenth century were the rivals of Britain on the continent of Europe and more particularly beyond the seas, where a race for colonial power was in progress. It seems probable that it was the absence of French help that chiefly prevented the

[TD 236]

men of North Uist from taking part in what was to be the last Jacobite rising, for without it the Chief of Sleat, Sir Alexander MacDonald, saw no hope of success (cf. Clan Donald, iii, 88 ff.).

176. <gai>A luaidheas an clò Catach:<eng> a pun, which also appears in a song of earlier date by Alexander MacDonald; see BGh., l. 1880 n. Sutherland tweed is specially singled out because the northern clans were Whigs.

177. <gai>a’ mhuc:<eng> members of the Hanoverian dynasty were often compared to German swine. Julia MacDonald of Keppoch says in one of her songs—

<gai>’S e rìgh na muice
’S na Cuigse rìgh Deòrsa;
Tha mi am chadal ’s na dùisgibh mi.
Mun tig oirnn an t-Samhuinn
Bidh amhach ’s na còrdaibh;
Tha mi am chadal ’s na dùisgibh mi.<eng>
(SO., 60.)

See also BGh., l. 1881 n.


<gai>ORAN NA H-AOISE<eng>

<gai>Oran air sean aois,<eng> AMD.; <gai>Diomoladh na sean Aois le Iain mac Codrum, am Bard Uisteach,<eng> S.

Alexander MacDonald published this song along with his own in 1751, thus giving the impression, whether intentionally or not, that he was the author. Alexander Stewart attributes it to MacCodrum. He was in a position to know, for he was for a number of years parish schoolmaster at Tigharry in North Uist, and though he may not have known the bard personally, they must have had many mutual acquaintances. Present tradition on the point is quite clear. The bard, it is said, was one day employed roofing a house in the village of Hosta, when he observed a very old man, staff in hand, walking towards him. It was a stormy day, and MacCodrum noticed what difficulty he had in making headway against the wind, and how, when he reached a small burn in the vicinity called <gai>Abhainn nan Riaghan,<eng> he took some time walking up and down before he found a place where he could cross. He thereupon began the song while at work, standing on the wall <gai>(tobhta)<eng> of the house. There seems to be no reason to doubt this account. A false tradition would probably take the form of assuming, as has been done (UB., xlii), that the theme was

[TD 237]

suggested by the bard’s personal experience of old age, which is disproved by the fact that the song was published twenty-eight years before his death. Oran na h-Aoise also appears in the third edition of MacDonald’s songs (1802), but in the next edition (1834) it was omitted. The omission is remarked upon in the preface: “Two poems which commonly appeared in the old editions, the one on Old Age, and the other on the merits and demerits of Whisky, are ascribed to John MacCodrum, a contemporary bard, whose works deserve to be collected and published by themselves. These two have great merit but they have been omitted here. They are to be found in the old collections published by Ronald Macdonald, and Alex. & Donald Stewart” (quoted from the reprint of 1839).

Dugald Buchanan seems to have known this song and to have derived some ideas from it. See An Geamhradh, particularly ll. 113-128 (The Spiritual Songs of Dugald Buchanan, 1913 ed., 54-55).

185. Cf. the proverb, <gai>Mar chloich a’ ruith le gleann feasgar fann fogharaidh. Am Bard Mucanach,<eng> in his song on the massacre of Glen Coe, says,

<gai>Och! ’s ann thàinig a’ chreach oirnn
Mar gun teàrnadh a’ chlach leis a’ ghleann.<eng>

Cf. also,

<gai>Shaoileadh gach neach a’ dol seachad
Gum bu chlach le gleann e.<eng>
(GB., ii, 20.)

261. <gai>’S e do bhuinnig an tuath ’s an déirce:<eng> John Lanne Buchanan, in his Travels in the Western Hebrides (1793), gives examples of people who, when enfeebled by old age, were despoiled of their possessions, and reduced to living on the charity of their neighbours (pp. 101-102, 173-178).

270. <gai>a’ chùirt:<eng> perhaps the court at which the chief or his baron-baillie administered justice in virtue of his hereditary jurisdiction.


<gai>ORAN NA H-OIGE<eng>

Taken down about sixty years ago by Rev. Dr Archibald MacDonald from Donald Laing, crofter at Howmore. It was published in the Celtic Magazine (viii, 236) in March 1883, and later in UB. It has evidently suffered in the course of a century

[TD 238]

and more of oral transmission, to judge from certain repetitions of phrase (ll. 305, 313; ll. 323, 329), and from instances of construction so loose as not to convey the author’s trend of thought with sufficient clarity (particularly the seventh and tenth verses). At present the song is a good deal confused in traditional renderings with MacDonald’s <gai>Oran a’ Gheamhraidh<eng> because of identity of metre and air. MacCodrum’s song contains two lines (ll. 311-312) which also appear in Oran a’ Gheamhraidh. This may be due to borrowing on the part of one or other of the bards (it cannot be said which song was composed first) or to a lapse of memory on the part of Donald Laing or some earlier traditional singer. It should be observed, however, that current tradition as known to the writer also gives the two lines in question.

It is impossible to assign even an approximate date to this song. It would appear to be a counterpart to <gai>Oran na h-Aoise<eng> and may, like it, belong to the period before 1751.

325. <gai>Mìos grianach:<eng> i.e. April, the fourth month; in Gaelic, <gai>An Giblein.<eng>

333. <gai>Bailc-Bhealltainn:<eng> in a note in UB. the phrase is explained as giving “the idea of the soft, dewy weather so desirable in May, and so productive of the fertility depicted” in the song. Rain is not infrequent at this time of the year, but it is generally light and not unpleasant. In the Instructions of King Cormac the best seasons are defined as follows—

<gai>Gem caín cuisnech,
errach tirim gáethach,
sam tur frossach,
fogmar tromdrúchtach torthech.<eng>

(A fine frosty winter,
a dry windy spring,
a droughty showery summer,
a fruitful autumn with heavy dews.)
(Meyer, Todd Lecture Series, xiv, 50.)

379. <gai>Solamh ’s aig Iòb:<eng> cf. 1 Kings iii, 13; Job i, 3.


<gai>CARAID AGUS NÀMHAID AN UISGE-BHEATHA<eng>

<gai>Comh-radh, mar go b’ann eider caraid agus namhaid an Uisgebheatha,<eng> AMD., E.

This is the other of MacCodrum’s songs which Alexander MacDonald published among his own without acknowledgment

[TD 239]

(1751). That it is MacCodrum’s is the unanimous belief of tradition. (See also notes on Oran na h-Aoise.) Nothing is known of the date of the song (except that it was composed before 1751) or of any special circumstances relating to it.

It will be noticed that the bard avoids the issue of temperance, probably in order to make the dialogue more clear-cut and artistically effective.

389. This line is copied by William Ross at the beginning of Oran an Uisge-bheatha.

394. Cf. the proverb, <gai>Chan fhiach cuirm gun a comhradh.<eng>

445. Rati: according to UB., a famous distiller, mentioned by more than one of the bards. The word “distiller” may be misleading to the present generation, accustomed to the production of whisky on a large scale. Rati was in fact a Uistman who combined with his lawful callings the ownership of one of the illicit stills which did a roaring trade at a time when excise regulations were not of much importance in the remoter parts of Scotland. How he got his nickname (for such it seems to be) is difficult to say. The surname Rati is common in Italy, but whether there was any connexion is doubtful. There was a song about Rati, by MacCodrum or some other bard of the time. The following fragment appears in M. (No. 22) and CC.—

<gai>O hó Rati,
O hì Rati,
O hó Rati
Nan clòithean;
Bu luath Rati
Le chuag chasan
Ri cruaidh shneachda
Gun bhrògan.

Fear mór foinneanta
Cruaidh sgoinneanta,
Cual chonnaidh
’S cruach mhòna;
Bidh siosacota
’S bidh briogais ort,
Agus siosar
Gu t’fheòsaig.<eng>

[TD 240]

569 ff. Refers to a notable characteristic of mercenary soldiers of old.

585 ff. Acquaintance is here shown with the process of distilling, whether from experience or observation. A diagram of a still, with the names of the parts, appears in Dwelly’s Dictionary s. v. <gai>poit-dhubh.<eng>


<gai>SMEÒRACH CHLANN DOMHNAILL<eng>

<gai>Le Eoin MacCodrum o Uist a Chinn a Tuath, alias (no <eng>written above) <gai>Eoin mac Fhearchair mhic Iomhair, An Smeorach,<eng> M. <gai>Smeorich Clann (Chlann<eng> in Index) <gai>Doibhnil, le Iain M‘Codrum o Uist, chinn a Tuath, alias, Iain M‘Fhearchair mhic Iobhair,<eng> E.

This is easily the best known of MacCodrum’s songs. It has hitherto been generally assumed that it was an imitation of Alexander MacDonald’s Smeòrach Chlann Raghnaill, and MacCodrum, apparently on that account, has been accused by Mackenzie of making “servile copies of great originals.” As regards Smeòrach Chlann Domhnaill evidence on the matter from published sources is inconclusive. MacDonald’s song was probably composed after 1751, for it does not appear in the collection of songs which he published in that year. Tradition avers that MacCodrum composed his song while he was living in Paible (cf. l. 617), which would place the date of its composition several years before 1760, and it is generally believed in Uist that MacCodrum’s song was composed first. Some go so far as to say that just as MacDonald “stole” (their word) some of MacCodrum’s songs and put them in his own book, so he imitated this one, not being able to appropriate it as it referred to a branch of the clan other than his own. It may be said that the voice of tradition is corroborated by a musical consideration. The air to which both songs are sung belongs to much older words—the words of one of the old North Uist seal songs quoted in the Introduction. It would seem more appropriate for MacCodrum, himself one of the seal-folk of North Uist, to use this air, and further, MacDonald was not likely to be familiar with the air of a local North Uist ditty until it became better known through the use to which MacCodrum put it. MacDonald perhaps heard Smeòrach Chlann Domhnaill from the lips of the composer during the encounter which they are known to have had at Tigharry some time after 1755.

617. Cf. l. 1 n. This line does not prove that MacCodrum

[TD 241]

was resident in Paible when he composed the song, but it appears from tradition that such was the case.

629a ff. (1) Several friends in Uist insist upon this verse, though they are aware that it is “not in the books.” The sense does not fit in with the rest of the song, wherever the verse is placed. Moreover, its similarity to the last verse of Maol-Ciarain’s lament for his son Fearchar, which MacCodrum knew (cf. l. 2326) is more than coincidence:

<gai>Thig an samhradh s thig an samh
Thig a Ghrian Ghobhlanach Gheal
Thig a mbradan as a bhruaich
Ach as a Nuaidh cho dtig mo mhac.<eng>
(Rel. Celt., ii, 333.)

The following verse is also similar:

<gai>Thig a’ chuthag, thig an t-snag,
Thig a h-uile h-ian g’ a nead,
Thig a’ gheàrr as a’ chuan,
Ach cha tig—mo nuar!—mo bhean.<eng>
(Carmina Gadelica, ii, 284.)

633. <gai>nach caol ri cois na mara:<eng> an expressive phrase which probably refers to the wide belt of machair and arable land on the north and west of North Uist.

639. <gai>ghèadhach:<eng> wild geese are very numerous in Uist. The bards often speak of <gai>Uibhist nan cràdhgheadh<eng> (Uist of the sheldrakes).

641. <gai>chròiceach:<eng> correctly glossed in M. as referring to kelp, which is made by the incineration of sea-ware. The proprietors of North Uist made huge sums from the kelp industry in the eighteenth century. Lord MacDonald alluded to the island on one occasion as his “little Peru.” The removal of the embargo upon Spanish barilla put an end to the boom, and since then the industry has been carried on only on a small scale. An old Uist song mentions the kind of wife the bard is looking for as follows:

<gai>Ged bha mi gòrach
’S ann bha mi ’n tòir
Air té bhuaileadh cròcan
’S a’ chròic gu teann.<eng>

<gai>eòrnach:<eng> Uist is also often termed <gai>Uibhist an eòrna.<eng> In a


(1) See Variant Readings.

[TD 242]

song, said by one authority (MC., xxxvi) to be to a daughter of Maclean of Boreray occur the lines:

<gai>Shiubhlainn leat a dh’ Uibhist
Far am buidhicheadh an t-eòrna.<eng>

and in another to Martin of Bealach:

<gai>Ma théid thu dh’ Uibhist an eòrna
Thoir bean bhòidheach dhachaidh leat.<eng>

652. <gai>òr air chlachan:<eng> another reference to the lucrative kelp industry. See l. 641 n.

653 ff. In describing his birthplace the bard proceeds from the particular to the general, i.e. he was born and brought up at <gai>Cladh Chomhghain<eng> in <gai>Aird an Runnair,<eng> which is a peninsula on the west of North Uist beyond the village of Hougharry, from which it is separated by a beautiful tidal bay called <gai>Tràigh nam Fadhailean.<eng> On the western side of this bay is a rock known as <gai>Sgeir na Circe,<eng> wrongly identified by Beveridge (North Uist, 266) with a huge boulder which stands about the middle of the peninsula, the name of which is <gai>Clach Mhór nam Peighinnean.<eng> At the coast-line about one hundred yards north of Sgeir na Circe is the end of a green ridge of turf called <gai>Gàradh na h-Airde,<eng> which is the remains of a dyke built after the peninsula was taken away from the township of Houghharry (c. 1828?) when Captain Allan Cameron was factor for North Uist. Immediately to the south of this end of Gàradh na h-Airde are the turf-covered remains of MacCodrum’s birthplace, barely visible after the lapse of more than two hundred winters. The house probably faced the south (the doorway cannot be traced) and measured about thirty feet long by fifteen feet broad. It no doubt consisted of the usual butt and ben of the period. Near the north-east corner of the house there appears to have been another structure measuring approximately twenty feet from north to south and twelve feet from east to west. This was probably the barn in which he is said to have rehearsed his first song. It may appear strange that a house should have been situated in a cemetery, but <gai>Cladh Chomhghain<eng> had been disused for centuries before MacCodrum’s time, and the house is described as having stood on the edge of the former burial-ground <gai>(an imeall a’ chlaidh).<eng> A slight mound several yards to the south of the house may be the sight of the small chapel which was situated in the middle of the cemetery. The dedication was to St Comgan. There are two saints of this

[TD 243]

name mentioned in the old Gaelic martyrologies—Comgan Céile Dé (Comgan the Culdee), whose day was 2nd August, and Comgan “of Cluain Connaidh in Cuircne” in Roscommon, whose day was 13th October. The Comgan commemorated on the latter date in the Scottish Calendars was, according to the Aberdeen Breviary, a brother of Kentigerna, daughter of Cellach Cuabann of Leinster, who died in 734 (Celtic Place-Names of Scotland, 281). The pronunciation is now as a rule Cladh Chòthain (denasalised), but the older pronunciation is not unknown. There are several dedications to Comgan in the Isles and on the mainland.

In the vicinity of <gai>Aird an Runnair<eng> there are many names of ecclesiastical origin—<gai>Cille Mhoire, Peighinn Moire, Eilean Drostain, Cille Chalmain, Cille Pheadair.<eng> In the peninsula itself there do not seem to be any ancient remains other than <gai>Cladh Chomhghain,<eng> except perhaps one prominent mound on the high ground to the north which bears the curious name of <gai>Tobhta na Sgàrlaid.<eng> If the place was ever densely populated there is no evidence of that now, for there are no other noticeable sites. The tacksmen to whom it belonged after Captain Allan Cameron’s time may, however, have destroyed such traces of occupation. In order to improve the pasture they removed as many stones from the land as they could. The numerous small cairns which dot the peninsula bear witness to those operations—such, at any rate, is the local belief.

682. Cf. Iain Lom—

<gai>Am fear nach dùraig a h-òl
Gun tuit an t-sùil air a’ bhòrd as.<eng>
(E., 197.)

683. This probably refers to Sir James’s absence from home when he was at school in England.


<gai>AN AGHAIDH FÀILTE NA MÓRTHIR<eng>

This is ascribed in North Uist to MacCodrum. The third verse is the best known; it appears in UB. (p. xxxiv) and is there attributed to him. The third, fourth, and sixth verses were known to Maclean Sinclair, who published them in the Maclean Bards (i, 251), but he attributes them to An t-Aireach Muileach. It would be quite in keeping with what is known of the latter that he should compose a song contradicting Alexander MacDonald: MacDonald did the like to his condemnation of <gai>An Caimbeulach Dubh.<eng> But the first two verses can hardly be

[TD 244]

by a Mull man. It may be that there were originally two songs which have become confused in tradition.

The Uist tradition is that MacDonald’s Fàilte and this was originally an ex tempore dialogue between the two bards which extended to twenty-five verses each, at the end of which MacCodrum had the last word with his pithy characterisation of the men of the Mórthir. The song itself, however, shows that it was, not one side of a dialogue, but a parody on MacDonald’s song, which may have been sung as a joke by MacCodrum during one of his encounters with his brother bard. It is also said that MacDonald added considerably to his song at a later date. There may be some truth in this, for the version of the song in E. has only thirty verses, whereas the MacNicol MS. version has fifty-seven.

More verses of the song given here may still come to light. It may be that the extra verse introduced into <gai>Fàilte na Mórthir<eng> in the 1923 edition of MacDonald’s songs may properly belong to the description of Uist of which the first two verses are part:

<gai>Tìr ’m bi pailteas
Coirc’ is eòrna,
Beoir fo shop is dram innt’.<eng>

The mention of barley suggests Uist rather than the <gai>Mórthir<eng> (cf. l. 641 n.).

691. With this verse compare the last verse of MacDonald’s song in the MacNicol MS. version:

<gai>’S uisge brisg-gheal,
’S e ri briotal,
Ruith throimh chriostal bheanntan.<eng>

694. This probably has reference to MacDonald’s verse (only in E. and SO.; cf. UB., xxxiii):

<gai>A’ choill gu h-uilidh
Fo làn duillich,
’S i ’n a culaidh bhainnse.<eng>

695. <gai>A’ Mhórthir:<eng> in Uist and the Small Isles this was used in the particular sense of the mainland territory of Clan Ranald, i.e. Knoydart, Morar, Arisaig, and Moidart. It can be distinguished in Gaelic from Morar (apart from spelling) by the fact that the latter does not take the article.

696. <gai>calltuinn:<eng> hazel was regarded of old in the Highlands as unlucky and as a useless timber.

[TD 245]

697. Cf. MacDonald (MacNicol MS. and SO.):

<gai>Na minn bheaga
Dol ’s na creagan,
’S iad ri ceigeis ghreannmhoir.<eng>

703. Cf. MacDonald (MacNicol MS.):

<gai>Mnathan aoibhinn
Mòdhar coibhneil,
Làn de loinn ’s de bhaindeachd.<eng>

706. Cf. MacDonald (MacNicol MS. and E.):

<gai>Fir làn sonais,
Saor bho dhonas,
Gun dad conais-dranndain.<eng>


<gai>AOIR DHOMHNAILL FRISEIL<eng>

The subject of this lampoon was Donald Fraser, an alien tacksman who settled in North Uist before 1760. He was probably one of the first tacksmen in the island who did not belong to the old stock. It was about 1770, when there was a rise in rents all over the Highlands, that his type became numerous in Uist and elsewhere. The older tacksmen found that they were unable to pay the higher rents without treating their sub-tenants in a way which was inconsistent with the traditional bonds of kinship and their responsibilities as chieftains of the clan, and they emigrated in large numbers to America. Strangers had no such scruples, and regarded their tacks merely as financial speculations. Observers of the period, like John Lanne Buchanan, remark upon the difference between the lot of the sub-tenants of the older class of tacksmen and that of the unfortunate ones who were dependent on tacksmen of the new land-broking type. MacCodrum makes a similar distinction in the sixth verse. In the circumstances, it is not difficult to believe that Fraser would be unpopular even if his personal qualities were more engaging than they seem to have been.

715. <gai>Céile mhic Shir Seumas:<eng> it has been suggested (UB., xxi, 62) that this was the widow of Ranald, natural son of Sir James MacDonald, second baronet of Sleat, who was known as <gai>Raghnall mac Shir Seumas,<eng> and from whom were descended the MacDonalds of Baleshare. She was Marion, daughter of Donald MacDonald, thirteenth of Clan Ranald, and relict of Allan

[TD 246]

MacDonald, fifth of Morar, and married Ranald MacDonald before 1706. Ranald died in 1742, and if it was she who married Fraser she must have been at least sixty years old when she married for the third time. Another possibility is that Fraser’s wife was the widow of John, son of Sir James MacDonald of Oransay, sixth baronet of Sleat, by his second marriage with Margaret, daughter of John MacDonald of Castleton. He is on record in 1723, but nothing further is known of him (Clan Donald, iii, 536, 474). John Lanne Buchanan mentions that many of the new tacksmen made a point of marrying into the old families, as that made it possible for them to exploit their position with impunity (Travels in the Western Hebrides, 69).

716. <gai>éighreachd Chloinn Mhic Cuinn:<eng> the island of Oransay and adjoining lands on the mainland of North Uist were occupied by a family of MacQueens from an early period. The original tack is said to have been a verbal one; it was to endure—

<gai>Fhad ’s a bhios bainne aig boin duibh
No Cnògaire Mhic Cuinn ’n a bhun.<eng>

(The <gai>Cnògaire<eng> is a hill on their former territories on the mainland of North Uist.) Early in the seventeenth century they received a more modern guarantee of their tenure. Kenneth MacQueen appears under North Uist in the Valuation Roll of Inverness-shire in 1691, when his estate was worth £160 (Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, 1st Series, 56). In 1718 Oransay was held by Kenneth MacQueen for 240 merks Scots and rent in kind to the extent of 8 bolls victuals and 24 st. butter (Clan Donald, iii, 110, 660).

757. <gai>am Bàideanach:<eng> so called, according to one authority (UB., xxi), because he was a native of Badenoch. There are, however, reasons for thinking that the <gai>Bàideanach,<eng> despite his name, was a Uistman. It is to be observed that, in this and the following song, he seems to be introduced, not because he was specially disliked, but because he was unfortunate enough to be a near neighbour of the notorious Fraser and so came into conflict with him oftener than others, especially if, as seems likely, he had a fiery temper. His sobriquet was that of others in Uist as well, and was applied to descendants of <gai>Domhnall Bàideanach<eng> (fl. c. 1510), a son of <gai>Domhnall Hearach,<eng> the progenitor of the MacDonalds of Griminish and Balranald, and a woman from Badenoch (Clan Donald, iii, 482).

[TD 247]

758. <gai>Lì:<eng> this is the name of two hills (<gai>Lì bho Thuath<eng> and <gai>Lì bho Dheas<eng>) on the east side of North Uist between Loch Maddy and Loch Eford. Cf. l. 2546 n.

765-766. The way in which the price of the heifer is given (partly in merks and partly in pounds Scots) is peculiar, and if the reason is metrical, it argues considerable arithmetical ability in a bard who had no school education. The price in pounds Scots would be £11, 13s. 4d., i.e. 19s. 5d. sterling.


<gai>A’ CHOIMHSTRITH<eng>

<gai>Air Connstrith Edir Diais ann Uibhist a Cheinne-tuath Leis an Ian Mac Codrum cheudna,<eng> M., G. <gai>A Chomhstri eadar Domhnull Friseal agus Domhnull Bàideanach,<eng> UB.

The two principals in the quarrel were Donald Fraser, the subject of the foregoing song, and <gai>Am Bàideanach<eng> (? Donald MacDonald), mentioned in the same song. The date of the piece is c. 1758.

773. MacCodrum was probably living in Langash, then a remote part of North Uist, when the song was composed. It is now easily accessible as the road between Clachan nan Cluip and Lochmaddy runs nearby.

774. Why he thought it necessary to go to Harris is not clear.

778. <gai>Leòdach:<eng> MacLeod was perhaps the man mentioned in the seventh verse who brought the news that the old enemies had made peace.

784. Cf. l. 33.

785 ff. The song was composed during the first part of the Seven Years’ War (1756-1763), before matters had begun to go well for Britain. By the Treaty of Versailles (1756) Austria joined her old enemy, France, and, as a result, Britain brought the alliance with Austria to an end, and by the Treaty of Westminster (1756) sided with Prussia. Thus MacCodrum is quite accurate in his enumeration of the enemies of the country—(1) the Hungarians or Magyars, who, on account of the popularity among them of Maria Theresa, had supported Austria since near the beginning of the War of the Austrian Succession (1740-1748); (2) the Germans, by which was meant at that time the Austrians, or Germans in the Empire as distinct from Prussians; (3) the

[TD 248]

French. The statement in l. 787 that neither sea nor land could protect the country from its enemies may have been inspired by the British disasters of 1756-1757. On land the British suffered reverses in America (on the Ohio and the St Lawrence); in India, where the native ruler of Bengal captured Fort William (Calcutta) and massacred most of the English-speaking inhabitants by suffocation; on the continent, where the Duke of Cumberland was defeated by the French and forced to give up Hanover. But what was regarded as of greatest consequence at the time was the loss of the command of the sea. This began with Admiral Byng’s abandonment of Minorca to the French. Panic ensued, and rumours of a French invasion were rife. These rumours reached even as far as the Western Isles, as is shown by this allusion. A sense of security was not restored until Hawke’s victory in Quiberon Bay in 1759.

790 ff. The bard’s forecast was correct.

793. This is a reference to the prohibition of arms in the Highlands after the risings of 1715 and 1745.

797. <gai>an Diùc:<eng> the Duke of Cumberland.

798. <gai>rìgh Phruisia:<eng> Frederick II, king of Prussia, better known as Frederick the Great. Frederick had just distinguished himself by gaining the brilliant victories of Rossbach and Leuthen (1757). His defeat by the Russians at Kunersdorf (1759) had probably not taken place when the song was composed.

814. <gai>Pabaidh:<eng> a large island in the Sound of Harris which supported a considerable population in the eighteenth century. It is now occupied by two shepherds.

844. North Uist—though there are indications that at one time it was well wooded—has been dependent in historic times on outside sources for its supply of timber. Frequent voyages were made to the opposite mainland coasts of Inverness-shire and Argyll and to Mull for cargoes of wood. This was called <gai>dol dh’ an Choille.<eng> It is possible that this source of supply was temporarily closed when the song was composed.

848. <gai>Mac Fhionnlaigh:<eng> he is said to have been a Morrison from Clachan Sand, belonging to a family which originated in the Isle of Pabbay, several of the members of which were smiths, including the religious bard of Harris, John Morrison. His family is still represented in North Uist. His part in the story is difficult to understand, unless it was to supply the combatants with weapons.

[TD 249]

849. <gai>Tha an dà iarann deug ’s an teallach aige<eng> is a saying used to describe a very busy person.

858 ff. This is the best-known verse of the song but its meaning is somewhat cryptic. Maclean Sinclair (GB., ii, 204) explains it as follows: “So horrible were the maledictions hurled against one another by the two who were quarrelling that Uist was disgraced, and that the demons deserted the lower regions to listen to them, whilst Satan was delighted, and at the same time astonished that men who were capable of using such awful language were not leaving this comparatively pure world and going to reside with himself.”

863. MacCuinn: Rev. Donald MacQueen, minister of the parish of North Uist from 1755 (admitted 29th September) to 1770. He was born c. 1700, and was a son of Rev. Angus MacQueen, minister of Sleat, who belonged to a line of clergymen which went back to the beginning of the seventeenth century. He was educated at King’s College, Aberdeen, where he graduated M.A. on 2nd April 1719. He was ordained to the parish of the Small Isles on 9th April 1727, where he remained until translated to North Uist. He married, on 13th October 1736, Marjory, daughter of Lachlan Maclean of Torrestan, cadet of Coll, with issue, Allan, who succeeded him in North Uist, and Edmund, minister of Barra. He believed in second sight and contributed a letter on the subject to Theophilus Insulanus. He died on 28th March 1770 (Fasti Eccl., vii, 191; Miscell. Scot., iii).

864. <gai>an Dotair:<eng> Niel Beaton, the last of a branch of the famous MacBeth family of physicians to hold hereditary office from the house of Sleat in North Uist. He died in 1763 (Clan Donald, iii, 128). His great-grandson, John Beaton <gai>(Iain mac Fhearchair mhic Iain mhic Nèill Dhotair),<eng> a shepherd at Corodale in South Uist, died in 1881 at the age of ninety-two. He had no school education, but had a wonderful knowledge of herbs. He had a son named Farquhar (Carmina Gadelica, ii, 80).

868. <gai>Uisdean:<eng> Hugh MacDonald of Baleshare, for whom see notes on <gai>Marbhrann do Uisdean a’ Bhaile Shear.<eng>

869. <gai>Dh’ Fhear Bhàlaidh:<eng> Ewen MacDonald of Vallay, third son of William, Tutor of MacDonald, and grandson of Sir Donald MacDonald, third baronet of Sleat. He came to Vallay in 1727, and from 1733 to 1740 acted as MacDonald’s factor for North Uist. He was known in Uist as <gai>Eóghan mac an Taoiteir,<eng> and

[TD 250]

is said to have been a true Highland gentleman <gai>(fìor dhuine uasal còir).<eng> He married Mary, daughter of Rev. Lauchlan Maclean, minister of Coll. She is not favourably spoken of by tradition. Ewen was an excellent piper, and was also a composer of pipe music. When Sir James MacDonald was accidentally shot in the foot while on a visit to North Uist and taken to Vallay, the Goodman of Vallay composed a pibroch known as <gai>Cumha na Coise,<eng> with which are associated the following words:

<gai>Mo ghaol, mo ghaol
Do chas threubhach
Dh’ an 	tig an t-osan
’S am féileadh;
Bu leat toiseach
Nan ceudan
’N am féidh
Bhith ’g an ruith.<eng>
(Clan Donald, iii, 542-543.)

He was also, according to tradition, one of the finest swordsmen in the Isles. An encounter took place between him and MacLeod of Bernera at Skellar, in which the latter was worsted. This was the last duel fought in North Uist. Ewen MacDonald built at Vallay one of the first slated houses in the parish (cf. Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 325). After the fashion of the time his and his wife’s initials with the date of their marriage are inscribed on the lintel:

E. M. D.	M. M. L.	1742
(See Beveridge’s North Uist, 99.)

The Clan Donald (iii, 543), arguing from the reference in l. 2704, gives the year of his death as 1769. The meaning of the line, however, taken with its context, seems to be that he died within six months of Sir James MacDonald, and the year of his death is therefore probably 1766 or 1767.

870. <gai>D’ an Bhàillidh:<eng> Alexander MacDonald of Hougharry, for whom see notes on <gai>Marbhrann do Alasdair mac Dhomhnaill.


DIOMOLADH PÌOBA DHOMHNAILL BHÀIN

Dio-moladh Pioba Dhonuill Bhain,<eng> M.

A certain local rimester named John MacPhail composed a song praising Donald MacAulay <gai>(Domhnall Bàn),<eng> who lived in

[TD 251]

Paible, for his excellence as a piper. The praise was so extravagant for a piper whose reputation was purely local that MacCodrum was moved to compose this song wherein MacAulay’s claims to skill in his art are ridiculed.

873. Iain: John MacPhail. See l. 1082 n.

877. <gai>MacCruimein:<eng> Pàdruig Mór MacCrimmon. See l. 890 ff. n.

878. <gai>Con-duiligh:<eng> he was perhaps the best known of the Rankins, who acted as hereditary pipers to the Macleans of Duart. He flourished in the first part of the eighteenth century, and was present with his chief, Sir John Maclean, at the Battle of Sheriffmuir. It is said that when the battle started he gave his pipes to a servant lad to guard and rushed into the thick of the fray. When the right of Mar’s army, in which he was fighting, routed their opponents, he returned to retrieve the pipes, but they were not to be found; the boy had taken fright and run away. A Coll bard, Lachlan Maclean <gai>(Lachlann mac mhic Iain),<eng> composed a song against the pipes in which he makes satirical use of the incident:

<gai>’N Sliabh an t-Siorraim beag dona so shuas—
An cuala sibh à?—
Thug i leum air muin gille bhig ruaidh
Gu teicheadh o ’n bhlàr.<eng>
(Rel. Celt., ii, 341; GB., i, 127.)

Condullie Rankin had a son named Hector. Hector’s son Ewen, an elder in the Church, died in 1783. His son Hector, also an elder, left Mull in 1804 to reside with a sister in Greenock. He was the last of the Rankin pipers in Mull, and his departure was regarded as a great loss to the community, as is shown by a song composed at the time:

<gai>Tha ’m dhìth, tha ’m dhìth,
Tha ’m dhìth gu buileach,
Clann Duiligh ’s a’ phìob
Tha ’m dhìth gu buileach.
’N am gleusadh nam pìob
Bu bhinn an luinneag;
’S iad thogadh am fonn
Neo-throm ’s a’ chuideachd.<eng>

This Hector had a son called Neil who was piper to Maclean of Coll and accompanied <gai>Alasdair Ruadh<eng> in the Earl of Breadalbane’s

[TD 252]

regiment. He married Catherine Maclean <gai>(Catrìona nighean Theàrlaich mhic Eachainn)<eng> and had five children—Hector, Catherine, Ewen, Condullie, and Janet. He gave up piping c. 1806, and died c. 1819. Of the sons, at least one, Condullie, was a piper. He fought in the American War of 1812 and rose to the rank of captain. In 1816 he returned to Scotland, and in the following year married Margaret Maclean, Kengarar, Mull. He and his brother Hector emigrated to Prince Edward Island in 1820.

The family claimed descent from Cù-duiligh mac Raing, who was the great-great-grandfather of Gill-Eathain na Tuaighe, from whom are descended the Clan Maclean. He was the first of the ancestors of the Macleans who lived in Argyll. After him the Rankins were of old called <gai>Clann Chon-duiligh<eng> and later <gai>Clann Duiligh.<eng> Later still they began to call themselves <gai>Clann Mhic Raing<eng> (Rankin), bringing in the earlier name in the genealogy.

When they became pipers to the Macleans is not known, but there is no doubt that by the eighteenth century they had for long possessed a great reputation. Their school of piping at Kilbrennan in Torloisk was attended by young men from all over the Highlands. With the decline of the fortunes of the Macleans of Duart it gradually lost its former glory. In 1760, the year in which it ceased to function, the pupils of <gai>Eóghan mac Eachainn mhic Chon-duiligh<eng> numbered only seventeen.

The Rankins are said to have been a very handsome race of men. This fact is referred to in the following verse:

<gai>Càit’ a bheil anns a’ chruitheachd
Aon mhic mnà thug ort urram
Ann an uaislead ’s an grinnead,
Ann an deirgead ’s an gilead,
Mach bho phearsa Chloinn Duiligh,
Is bho Phàdruig MacCruimein?<eng>
(MT., xii, No. 2.)

<gai>Teàrlach:<eng> Charles MacArthur, the most celebrated of the hereditary pipers of the Sleat family, who was a contemporary of MacCodrum. The MacArthurs occupied the lands of Hunglater in Trotternish in virtue of their office. They were supposed to be a branch of the MacArthurs of Proaig in Islay, who acted as pipers and armourers to the MacDonalds of the Isles. Charles had the advantage of being under the tuition of Pàdruig Og MacCrimmon for eleven years. When Sir Alexander

[TD 253]

MacDonald was a student in St Andrews (1726-1729) Charles MacArthur was in attendance as his piper. He was also piper to the next two chiefs. His salary in 1749 was £66, 13s. 4d. Scots. Among his compositions is a lament for Sir James MacDonald published in General Thomason’s Ceòl Mór. The date of his death is not recorded; he was living in 1774, in which year Pennant visited him. At that time he must have been an old man, and he probably did not survive that decade. Over his grave in Kilmuir, Skye, is a recumbent stone with the following inscription:

HERE LY
THE REMAINS OF
CHARLES MAC
KARTER WHOSE
FAME AS AN HON
EST MAN AND
REMARKABLE PIP
ER WILL SURVIVE
THIS GENERATION
FOR HIS MANNERS
WERE EASY & REG
ULAR AS HIS
MUSIC & THE
THE MELODY OF
HIS FINGERS
WILL

The unfinished state of the inscription is due to the fact that the local mason who executed it was receiving, or was to receive, certain sums in commission from Charles’s son Donald while the work was still in progress. This arrangement fell through when Donald met his death suddenly by drowning while ferrying cattle from Uist to Skye. Charles MacArthur had another son named Alexander who is said to have emigrated to America. Shortly after 1800, he petitioned Lord MacDonald to appoint him his piper, describing himself as the only male representative of the family then living. Better known in North Uist than Charles MacArthur was his brother John (Iain Bàn), who was official piper to Sir Alexander MacDonald there in 1745 with a salary of £33, 6s. 8d. His son Angus was piper to Lord MacDonald and died in London in 1800, the last holder of the office. A second brother, Neil, had a son John, who was instructed in the art of piping by his celebrated uncle. John took up residence in

[TD 254]

Edinburgh, and was piper to the Highland Society of Scotland. Representatives of the MacArthur family are still to be found in Skye (Clan Donald, iii, 85, 126-127; Celtic Monthly, xi, 118).

891. <gai>B’e MacCruimein an rìgh:<eng> it is almost certain that the piper MacCodrum had in mind was Pàdruig Mór MacCrimmon, the most renowned of his line. With the statement that he was king of pipers compare the following from the Wardlaw MS. (pp. 379-380): “All the pipers in the army gave John Macgurmen [MacCrimmon] the van, and acknowledged him for their patron in chiefe. It was pretty in a morning (the King) in parad viewing the regiments and bragads. He saw no less than 80 pipers in a crould bareheaded, and John M’ gyurmen in the midle covered. He asked What society that was? It was told his Majesty: Sir, yow are our King, and yonder old man in the midle is the Prince of Pipers. He cald him by name, and, comeing to the King, kneeling, his Majesty reacht him his hand to kiss; and [? he] instantly played an extemporanian part [i.e. port] Fuoris Pooge i spoge i Rhī [Fuaras pòg a (i.e. de) spòg a(n) Rìgh], I got a kiss of the Kings hand; of which he and they all were vain.” According to the author of the MS. the above incident occurred in May 1651, a few weeks before the battle of Inverkeithing, when Charles II and the royalist forces were stationed at Stirling. The usual tradition, among pipers and in the Isles, has been that the author of the pibroch, <gai>Thug mi pòg do làimh an Rìgh,<eng> was Pàdruig Mór, and that he composed it when he kissed the hand of Charles II while on a visit to London with Roderick MacLeod of Talisker after the Restoration (cf. Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 103). The Wardlaw MS., written by a contemporary, is probably right as regards the occasion and date of the composition; as regards the piper’s first name even a contemporary might err. That there is an error is suggested by the fact that there does not seem to be any further trace of a John MacCrimmon in the family during the seventeenth century. The supremacy of the MacCrimmons among pipers was apparently acknowledged long before 1651, but as MacCodrum seems to be referring to one particular member of the family, his words may possibly refer almost directly to the Stirling incident—one among many instances of his knowledge of Highland history. That it was Pàdruig Mór he had in mind is confirmed by his reference to the <gai>Oinseach<eng> (l. 900), a set of pipes usually associated with Pàdruig. It is true that Alexander MacDonald has a song in praise of “MacCrimmon’s Pipes, called the Idiot,” which would appear

[TD 255]

to indicate that the owner was his contemporary, <gai>Domhnall Bàn.<eng> It is very likely, however, that the same instrument might be used by several generations of pipers.

The MacCrimmons are of Norse origin (cf. MacBain’s Etymological Dictionary, 365). The MacCrimmon country was originally the south of Harris and the islands of the Sound. Such at any rate is the Island tradition recorded in the Bannatyne MS.

A recent book on the piping family of the name is The MacCrimmons of Skye by Mr Fred. T. MacLeod.

903. <gai>A’ bhàirisgeach:<eng> it may be that a proper name is intended here—<gai>A’ Bhàirisgeach<eng>—by which Charles MacArthur’s pipes may have been known, just as MacCrimmon’s pipes were designated <gai>An Oinseach.<eng>

926 ff. The Macleans of Duart were the victims of the scheming of the Earl of Argyle during the minority of Sir John Maclean. In 1681 the estate of Duart became the property of the Crown and after 1688 it was acquired by the Earl of Argyle. That the Macleans were Jacobites worked in favour of the Campbells after the Revolution.

929. <gai>luchd nam beul fiara:<eng> the Campbells. Gaelic <gai>Caimbeul = cam + beul<eng> (wry mouth).

934. See l. 878 n.

935. See l. 869 n.

946. <gai>o-dròchain:<eng> MacCodrum was probably well versed in pipe music, as a listener if not as a player, and, like many others at that time, had some knowledge of <gai>canntaireachd,<eng> the vocal method of rendering pipe tunes. The MacCrimmons and the MacArthurs each had a system of canntaireachd, and there were several other systems. Further examples of MacCodrum’s knowledge of canntaireachd will be seen in ll. 967, 968, 969, 970, 977, 992, 1071.

979. <gai>Tùbal-càin:<eng> see Genesis iv, 22, where he is spoken of as <gai>fear-teagaisg gach uile fhir-ceird’ ann an umha agus ann an iarunn;<eng> perhaps MacCodrum is confusing him with his half-brother Jubal (ibid., 21)—<gai>Iubal: b’esan athair nan uile a laimhsicheas clàrsach agus organ.<eng>

983. Genesis vii, 16.

994. <gai>Maol-Ruanaidh O Dòrnan:<eng> “A wandering Irish piper, whose music the Highlanders could not appreciate” (SO., 149). “A wandering Irish harper whose piping was unacceptable to the Highlanders” (UB., 53). Even as late as the eighteenth

[TD 256]

century Irish harpers used to travel through the Highlands. The celebrated Irish harper Echlin Kane visited Sir Alexander MacDonald at Sleat in 1775, and at the end of a lengthy stay his host presented him with a valuable silver harp-key (Bunting, Ancient Music of Ireland, 44, 79; Gunn, An Historical Enquiry respecting the Performance on the Harp, etc., 48).

997. <gai>MacBheatrais:<eng> several of this name are mentioned in the records of the Privy Council and elsewhere as fighting men in the following of the MacDonalds of Sleat. In the Lord High Treasurer’s Accounts for the year 1503 there is recorded—“Item to Makberty, the clarisha to pass to the Isles, iijb. xs.” Other individuals of this name appear as harpers in Galloway. It may be another form of the one used by MacCodrum. Still another form may be MacBeathaig, which was the name of the Sleat bard, Uilleam MacBeathaig. It is possible that the reference is to him. As a predecessor of MacCodrum, it may be of interest to give what little is known of him and his verse here.

According to the late Rev. Alexander MacGregor, Uilleam MacBeathaig (alias MacArthur, according to MacGregor), piper and bard to MacDonald of Sleat, was present at a gathering of chiefs at Dunvegan with his master, whose daughter was married to MacLeod. There were present also Mackinnon of Strathswordale, Maclean of Duart and Maclean of Lochbuie, MacLeod of Lewis, Mac Gille-Chaluim of Raasay and others, accompanied by their pipers and bards. A badge of honour was offered to the bard making the best rann in praise of his patron on the following day. MacLeod’s lady, who was unhappy in her marriage and had once been sent home after a violent quarrel with her husband only to be immediately returned to Dunvegan, was anxious that her father’s bard should win, and promised him trews of mottled scarlet cloth <gai>(clò breac sgarlaid)<eng> which she had made for her husband, if he made a rann maintaining that she as daughter of the Lord of the Isles was of nobler blood than her husband, a claim which the latter had often strongly contested. For the rest of the day all the bards were busily engaged rehearsing their poetic efforts for the morrow except MacBeathaig. On being questioned about his silence he retorted:

<gai>’S e sinn féin a mholamaid
Mar linne loma-làn;
Na h-uile sruthain as tana
’S iad as àirde gàir.<eng>
(Cf. O’Rahilly, Dánfhocail, No. 51.)

[TD 257]

Next day, having been informed by the lady of the house that breakfast was over, he knocked at the door of the breakfasting-hall, and when it was opened stood there silently after bowing to the assembled guests. <gai>“A ghoistidh, thig air t’adhart,”<eng> said MacLeod; whereupon MacBeathaig delivered his rann:

<gai>Chan fheudar beanailt ri luchd na comhladh
’S ann de m’iongnadh;
Fhuair sibh tigh agus leth Alba
Le neart bhur daoine;
Mac Fhionghuin, Mac Gill-Eathain,
Mac Leoid Leódhuis—
Triuir bha feitheamh dreuchd
An teach Mhic Dhomhnaill;
Fear-ionaid Mhic Leoid a Dùn Bheagain
Dorsair seòmair,
Is bu mhath an inbhe dha
Bhith feitheamh comhlaidh;
Morair Hunndaidh nan each seanga
Dh’ am biodh mórshluagh—
Bhiodh esan am freasdail stioraip
An am teàrnaidh;
Fhuair iad duais mhath a cheann sin—
Bàideanach o cheann gu ceann dith
Aca tha i,
Aca tha i.

“A chon-bhodaich, rinn thu luchd-muinntir uile dhinn,”<eng> said MacLeod. The bard retired, but MacLeod, knowing his effort to be the best, soon followed him with the request, <gai>“Thig an so, a MhicBeathaig, agus gabh do dhuais.”<eng> To which MacBeathaig proudly retorted:

<gai>’S ann a gheibhinn mo dhuais
Ann an talla nan teud
Bho Dhomhnall Gorm,
An t-àrmunn treun,
Bho Dhomhnall Gorm
Bu chomhnard ceum
Fo chomhrag arm,
Bho Dhomhnall Gorm
Nan cliar ’s nan creach,
Mo bhiadh ’s mo dheoch,
M’uisge-beatha
’S m’fhìon gu moch,
’S mo ghrian air loch.<eng>

[TD 258]

MacLeod’s lady was delighted, and gave the bard a suit of the <gai>clò breac sgàrlaid.<eng>

The MacBheatrais alias may perhaps be conceded to MacBeathaig. <gai>Beathag,<eng> usually translated Sophia, is rendered Beatrice by a Uistman, Rev. James MacDonald, in his General View of Agriculture in the Hebrides (p. 705), published in 1811. This may indicate some traditional connexion. The MacArthur alias sounds less probable, but MacBeathaig no doubt played the pipes where his predecessors had played the harp, which had lost its popularity. His skill was not great, to judge from MacCodrum’s allusion. Perhaps the office of piper had not yet become a distinct one in the MacDonald retinue. MacBeathaig’s floruit may perhaps be placed in the beginning of the seventeenth century. The Domhnall Gorm he mentions is evidently the second chief of that name, known as <gai>Domhnall Gorm Mór,<eng> who died without issue in 1617. Tradition has evidently confused the relationship with MacLeod, for it was the reverse of that stated in the story—MacDonald married as his first wife Mary, daughter of Norman MacLeod of Dunvegan, and subsequently repudiated her, in circumstances well known to Island tradition (Celtic Magazine, vii, 21-23; UB., 53; Clan Donald, iii, 124, 470).

999-1000. The last professional harper of Scottish birth was Murdoch MacDonald <gai>(Murchadh Clàrsair),<eng> who learnt his art from Roderick Morrison <gai>(An Clàrsair Dall)<eng> and in Ireland. He was harper to the Macleans of Coll, and retired from office in 1734, from which date until his death he resided at Quinish in Mull (Gunn, An Historical Enquiry respecting the Performance on the Harp, etc., 101).

1001. Balàäm: see Numbers xxii-xxiv.

1005. <gai>Seachd cathan nam Fiantaidh:<eng> the Fian consisted of seven battalions in time of war and three in time of peace. Each battalion numbered three thousand men, as the gloss in M. correctly states. Keating’s account, which is the authority for the above, is as follows: <gai>“Is é gnáthshluagh do bhíodh ar buannacht fá Fhionn trí catha na Gnáithfhéine, agus trí mhíle san chath, an tan fá síodhach fir Eireann ré chéile. Gidheadh an tan do fhásadh easaonta idir aonlucht d’ uaislibh Eireann agus an t-airdrígh, no an tan fá héigean sluagh do chur i nAlbain do neartughadh Dál Riada i n-aghaidh allmhurrach, do bhíodh seacht gcatha ag Fionn, ionnus go mbíodh furthainn sluagh aige ré dul d’ fhurtacht Dál Riada i nAlbain agus ré hEirinn do

[TD 259]

choimhéad ó fhoirneart eachtrann mar an gcéadna”<eng> (Foras Feasa ar Eirinn; Rosg Gàidhlig, 167).

1007. <gai>Dhiarmaid is Ghuill:<eng> Diarmaid O Duibhne, one of the most renowned of the Fian, and Goll mac Mòrna, head of the Clann Mòrna, who was associated with them. For Island tradition regarding them see Gregorson Campbell, The Fians, 49-63.

1009. <gai>Iain Og:<eng> not identified.

1014. <gai>mac Eachainn:<eng> also unidentified; unless it is he who is referred to in the following <gai>port-a-beul—

’N uair a théid thu stigh do ’n tìr,
’S a thadhlas tu ’n Cnoc-an-Lìn,
Seinnidh Cas Odhar a’ phìob,
’S gheibh sibh ruidhle aighearach.

A hao-il a hó-il
Tha dronnag air nighean Eóghain,
A hao-il a hó-il
Cas mhór is cas bheag oirre.

Cas Odhar<eng> was a nickname acquired by the piper as a little boy because when practising on the chanter and keeping time with his foot, his mother used to say, <gai>“Nach seall sibh air a’ chas odhar.” Nighean Eóghain<eng> was a poor woman <gai>(cailleach a bhiodh a’ faoigh)<eng> from the Paible district.

1015. <gai>sliabh Chnoc-an-Lìn:<eng> a village in Paible on the rising ground south of Bayhead. It got its name at a time when flax was grown in the island.

1025. <gai>inneadh air aodach: inneadh<eng> is a lack of thread when there is a web in the loom, and the amount required to make good the deficiency. It is considered unlucky to refuse a request for thread to complete a web. An Island legend tells how the Virgin Mary gave to a poor woman who asked her for inneadh a lock of her hair, which miraculously completed the web.

1042. Cf. <gai>“Seirbhe na ngaphunn a sciamhuill”<eng> in a mock eulogy of the pipes, probably known to MacCodrum (Rel. Celt., ii, 330); <gai>“’S gur a seirbhe e na ’n gabhann r’a iomradh”<eng> (Glenbard Coll., 65). Tadhg Ruadh O Conchobhair, dispraising a bad harper, says:

<gai>tuar tuirse tafann a lámh,
blas na gcrann gafann ’na ngeóin.<eng>
(Measgra Dánta, i, 7.)

[TD 260]

1053. <gai>Còrr<eng> was apparently used as a term of contempt, either by itself or prefixed to another word. Cf. Keating, speaking of Colum Cille’s arrival at the Convention of Druim Ceat, <gai>“adubhairt an ríoghan … gan cádhas do thabhairt don choirr-chléireach ná dhá bhuidhin”<eng> which is explained in O’Donnell’s Betha Coluimb Chille as <gai>samhlughadh re cuirr,<eng> “likening him to a heron.” The “lewd fellows of the baser sort” mentioned in the Acts of the Apostles (xvii, 5) appear in Gaelic as <gai>“droch dhaoine àraidh de na corraibh-margaidh.”<eng> The word also occurs in the Gaelic saying, <gai>Aithris an darna curra air a’ churra eile<eng> (One worthless woman’s reproach of another). The use of the word in such contexts perhaps originated in the fact that the heron was regarded as an ungainly bird (Bergin, Stories from Keating’s History of Ireland, 39, 94; Gregorson Campbell, The Fians, 47). The figures seen by gazing steadily into the fire, of old regarded as spirits, are called <gai>corracha cagailt.<eng> Cf. Neil MacVurich in <gai>Seanchas na Pìoba bho Thùs:

Pìob sgreadain Iain MhicArtuir
Mar eun curra air dol air n-ais<eng>

and:

<gai>Seinnidh corra-ghlùineach a ghafainn
Fuaim trùilich an tafainn sheirbh.<eng>
(G., 292.)

Cf. also l. 1024.

1073. <gai>ochdnar:<eng> the eight fingers used to play the bagpipes.

1076-1077. Cf. Lachlan Maclean in <gai>Diomoladh na Pìoba<eng> already mentioned:

<gai>Gum b’fheàrr i mar chlachbhalg
Chur nan each bhàrr an fheoir
Na bhrosnachadh sluaigh.<eng>
(Rel. Celt., ii, 340; GB., i, 127.)

MacCodrum probably knew this song and the songs for and against the pipes with which it is connected. The connexion appears to be as follows: (1) Neil MacVurich dispraised the pipes (G., 291); (2) Archibald MacDonald of Keppoch, in reply (?), praised them (GB., i, 94); (3) John Maclean <gai>(Iain mac Ailein),<eng> the Mull bard, praised the pipes and Archibald for his effort on their behalf (Rel. Celt., ii, 338; GB., i, 174); (4) Lachlan Maclean composed the song mentioned above as a parody on (3).

1078. <gai>Duan chur as frìthe:<eng> the meaning seems uncertain. Perhaps “a rime (incantation?) for putting out a heath fire,”

[TD 261]

or it may be a continuation of the sense in the previous line—“a rime for chasing away from a heath.”

1082. <gai>MacPhàil:<eng> John MacPhail, the author of the panegyric on Domhnall Bàn, as already mentioned.
