Two persons of this name appear in the Rent Roll of I718, one in Baile Mhic Conain and one in Clachan Sand (Clan Donald, iii, 659). The MacPhails seem to have been a clan of old standing in North Uist, and were at one time numerous in the Sand district. Their ancestor was probably he who gave his name to <gai>Baile Mhic Phàil<eng> and <gai>Loch Mhic Phàil,<eng> both of which are in that district. A Norse chief, Paul Balkeson, still remembered in the tradition of MacCodrum’s time as <gai>Pàl Bacach,<eng> was powerful in this region at the end of the twelfth and the beginning of the thirteenth century, and it is probable that the Uist MacPhails are descended from him. They are said to have been an influential tribe in the island at one time. Neil MacVicar <gai>(Niall Ruadh Mór),<eng> a Uist bard who flourished upwards of a century ago, composed a song in which he made two Uistmen of the time, Roderick MacDonald and Archibald MacPhail, indulge in wordy warfare. Their ancestors are discussed at some length. MacPhail thus refers to his:

<gai>A Ruairidh spàgaich mhic Ailein
Chaill thu thairis do nàire
’N uair a theann thu ri m’aoireadh
’S mi shliochd-dhaoine ro làidir;
Bha mi uair ann an Sannda
Bhiodh do cheann-s’ air dhroch càradh
Air son do chantanais dhomhsa:
Gu robh gu leòr Chlann Mhic Phàil ann
De ghillean smart.<eng>

In later times, with the rise of the MacDonalds and MacLeods, the MacPhails became, like several other clans in the region of Harris and North Uist, a broken clan. In the song quoted above reference is made to the fact that they had no chief:

<gai>’S ged bha iad gun cheann-cinnidh
’S iad nach tilleadh roimh nàmhaid.


ORAN NAM BANTRAICHEAN<eng>

This song to the widows of North Uist was composed some time after the death of the bard’s second wife. He represents himself as very much embarrassed by their attentions. It is said that he had three widows particularly in mind, although they are

[TD 262]

not mentioned by name. One was a MacCuish from Cladach Baleshare, one was a Ferguson from Sand, and the third was from Sollas, but enquiries as to her name have so far been unsuccessful. The first-mentioned was, according to tradition, a near neighbour of the then youthful <gai>Gille na Ciotaig.<eng> John, notwithstanding their determined efforts, did not marry any of them or of the other widows of the island when he took a third wife.

1091. <gai>Shannda:<eng> Sannda is a general name for the district in North Uist to the south of the Sound of Bernera. Before the era of evictions and emigration it supported a large population. In 1718 its five villages <gai>(Baile Mhic Phàil, Baile Mhic Conain, Clachan, Rùmaisgearraidh,<eng> and <gai>Bhàlacuidh)<eng> were divided between thirty-seven tenants (Clan Donald, iii, 659). In 1794 the population was 800 (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 316). The people were removed when Captain Allan Cameron was factor.

<gai>Sholas: Solas<eng> is a general name for the district on the north side of North Uist between Ceann Tràigh Bhàlaidh on the west and Cnoc Mhic Eóghain on the east. It is at present divided into four townships the names of which are in Gaelic (proceeding from west to east), <gai>Malacleit, Ceathramh Meadhonach, Solas,<eng> and <gai>Greunatobht.<eng> In 1718 only the first two of these were occupied by small tenants holding directly from the proprietor. The name Solas was originally applied to an old holding occupying the high ground between the present townships of Sollas and Grenetote south of the main road, and may still be heard applied to that spot. The present township of Sollas, set up by the Congested Districts Board about 1900, and occupying the land between Grenetote and Middlequarter <gai>(Ceathramh Meadhonach),<eng> includes the ancient holdings of Sollas <gai>(Solas),<eng> Havisgarry <gai>(Hàbhaisgearraidh),<eng> Misigarry <gai>(Misegearraidh),<eng> (?) Gerrina-curran <gai>(Gearraidh-nan-curran),<eng> and Doun <gai>(An Dùn)<eng> (see Beveridge’s North Uist, 93-94).

1103. <gai>bàillidh an tighearn:<eng> Alexander MacDonald, for whom see notes on Marbhrann do Alasdair mac Dhomhnaill.

1107. <gai>Fear Ghrìminis:<eng> John MacDonald of Griminish. During his father’s lifetime he occupied Scolpig. He died in 1765 (Clan Donald, iii, 485). The MacDonalds of Griminish were descended from <gai>Domhnall Hearach,<eng> a younger son of Hugh MacDonald of Sleat and a daughter of MacLeod of Harris. He and his descendants occupied Griminish and Scolpig for nearly three hundred years (c. 1500-c. 1790). Towards the end of

[TD 263]

the eighteenth century the main line became extinct and the representation of <gai>Clann Dhomhnaill Hearaich<eng> devolved upon the MacDonalds of Balranald. The most celebrated of the MacDonalds of Griminish in the traditions of North Uist is <gai>Iain mac Uisdein,<eng> fifth of the line, who died in his ninety-fourth year c. 1700 (Martin’s Description, 1934 ed., 146).


<gai>RANN DO ’N LÀIR<eng>

MacCodrum is said to have composed a song to a small Highland mare he had when he was in Langash. The verse given in the text is all that the writer can give of it at the moment. This is one of the few instances where the bard is known to have made a song the words of which are almost entirely lost.


<gai>ORAN A’ BHONN-A-SIA<eng>

The circumstances in which this song was composed were as follows: Roderick MacLeod of Borline, a drover of note in his day, was engaged in shipping cattle, at Loch Eford (acc. to UB.), preparatory to ferrying them across to his own lands on the shores of Loch Eynort in Skye. John MacCodrum, who was probably living in Langash at the time, was engaged to assist in the work of embarkation. Night was falling before the cattle were all aboard, and when the bard received his fee from the drover he mistook the coin in the uncertain light for a guinea—so, at any rate, the song would have us believe. The mistake was not discovered—this, again, according to the song—until the half-penny (for such it was) was presented in payment for a large order at a certain inn. That John was concerned more with the humour of the situation than with the seeming niggardliness of the Skyeman is shown by the verse which he later added to the song excusing MacLeod (see l. 2951 ff.).

1143. <gai>tigh air an laimrig:<eng> the present pier at Loch Eford is on the opposite shore of the loch from Langash, but which <gai>laimrig<eng> is meant here is uncertain. The bard was evidently not returning home that night. He and his seven companions (l. 1203) were staying in the house near the landing-stage until morning.

1145. <gai>Aonghus MacAmhlaidh:<eng> Angus MacAulay was perhaps the owner of the house.

1156. <gai>an clachan:<eng> simply “the hamlet,” and probably not <gai>Clachan nan Cluip,<eng> a village nearly two miles due west from the head of Loch Eford.

[TD 264]

1157. <gai>rathad na Leacaich:<eng> the only Leacach known to the writer is situated on the east side of Loch Obisary and is sometimes more fully referred to as <gai>Leacach Loch Obasairigh.<eng> If this is the place intended in the text then it may be that the ferrying operations were carried out somewhere in the Eaval peninsula—where the old North Uist market stance was in those days—perhaps in the vicinity of <gai>Clachan Biùrabhal<eng> at the mouth of Loch Eford, but where the whisky was to be procured is hard to say. It was evidently not at the inn at <gai>Rudha Eubhadh.<eng> To make the unfortunate Angus undertake the long journey to the inn at Carinish would undoubtedly enhance the effect of the dénouement for which the bard is preparing, and it may be, therefore, that the clachan mentioned in l. 1156 is Carinish. To go from Clachan Biùrabhal to Carinish there are two routes which may be taken on foot, the one by the east of Eaval and the other by the west. These routes are about equal in length, but the latter is the easier and is apparently the one recommended in the song.

1159. <gai>Teàrlach:<eng> the inn-keeper, who is not further identified.

1174. <gai>dealbh na clàrsaich:<eng> this is interesting as showing that the halfpenny was an Irish one—the harp appears only on Irish coins. No Irish money was struck during the reign of Queen Anne, and the inn-keeper’s reference to <gai>cùinneadh na ban-righ<eng> was therefore mistaken. An examination of the various halfpence in circulation after the Revolution leads to the conclusion that the honest Charles failed to recognise the laureated head of George II, which is not unlike that of Queen Anne. A large issue of Irish halfpence, amounting to fifty tons of copper, was made in George II’s reign at various dates between 1736 and 1755. The obverse was inscribed Georgius Rex (the omission of Dei Gratia gave rise to criticism); the reverse, besides the crown and the harp, bore the one word Hibernia and the date. As the conversation between Charles and MacAulay is probably imaginary, the mistake is MacCodrum’s and bears out (though it does not prove) the tradition that he could not read. The tone of Charles’s remarks further indicates that Irish money was well known at the time. Even as late as the eighteenth century there was considerable intercourse with Ireland. Drovers, like Roderick MacLeod, frequently visited there. <gai>Murchadh Ruadh nam Bó,<eng> the author of the original <gai>Hó mo Mhàiri Laghach,<eng> is said to have heard his song sung in an inn in Ireland while there

[TD 265]

on a droving expedition. The Gaelic for “heads and tails” in the Isles to this day is <gai>ceann is clàrsach.<eng>

1180. <gai>Ruairidh an dròbhair:<eng> Roderick MacLeod of Borline, as already mentioned. The MacLeods of Borline were descended from Norman, second son of <gai>Uilleam Cléireach,<eng> fifth Chief of Dunvegan, whose descendants were known as <gai>Clann Mhic ’ic Uilleim.<eng> They possessed Borline for a number of generations. There does not seem to be any genealogical information available regarding this branch of the MacLeods. William MacLeod of Borline, captain in the 73rd Regiment, was perhaps Roderick’s son. Roderick’s children were young when the song was composed c. 1760 (l. 2954). Mackenzie (History of the MacLeods, 273) mentions another member of the “ancient family of Borline,” William Comperus MacLeod, who was born c. 1815. Others of the family emigrated to North Carolina. Mary MacLeod, the poetess, was of the same stock, being descended from Alasdair Ruadh (not to be confused with her father), second son of Norman mentioned above (Bannatyne MS., quoted in Canon R. C. MacLeod’s The MacLeods of Dunvegan, 58).

1183. <gai>Tormod a Uinis:<eng> Norman MacLeod of Unish, eldest son of Donald MacLeod of Bernera. In 1739 he was deeply implicated in the kidnapping of numbers of Islanders for the purpose of transporting them to the American plantations. A ship (known to posterity as <gai>Soitheach nan Daoine<eng>) actually set sail but was wrecked on the coast of Ireland without loss of life. Only a few of its occupants, however, made their way back to the Isles. After this exploit Norman spent some time in hiding in Ireland. When the Rising of 1745 took place he left Ireland and joined the forces of the Government. On the death of his father in 1783 he became tacksman of Bernera, and in the following year succeeded Alexander MacLeod of Muiravonside as representative of the Bernera family (Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 255 ff.).

1184. <gai>Uilleam a Os:<eng> William MacLeod of Ose, who was married to Christian, eighth child of Donald MacLeod of Bernera, and sister of the Goodman of Unish (Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 252). The MacLeods of Ose were descended from Alexander of Minginish, third son of Norman, eleventh Chief of Dunvegan (Canon R. C. MacLeod’s The MacLeods of Dunvegan, 100). Genealogical details do not appear to be extant.

[TD 266]

1185. <gai>Tormod eile:<eng> in all probability MacCodrum means Norman MacLeod, fourth of Greshornish, who succeeded his father, Rev. Donald MacLeod, at one time parish minister of North Uist (1736-1754), on the latter’s death, which took place probably towards the end of 1759 (his death is noted in the Scots Magazine of 12th January 1760). Norman was married to his cousin, Margaret, daughter of Alexander MacLeod of Ullinish <gai>(an Siorram),<eng> whom MacCodrum mentions. Boswell met and admired her in her father’s house. Norman MacLeod of Greshornish was still living in 1798 (Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 268-269).

<gai>an Siorram:<eng> Alexander MacLeod, Sheriff-substitute of Skye, second son of Roderick MacLeod, first of Ullinish, and great-grandson of Sir Roderick Mór, thirteenth Chief of Dunvegan. He entertained Dr Johnson at Ullinish in 1773. Boswell says, “Mr MacLeod of Ulinish, the Sheriff-substitute of the island, was a plain honest gentleman, a good deal like an English justice of peace; not much given to talk, but sufficiently sagacious and somewhat droll” (Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 270, 275 ff.).

1191. <gai>Fear Fearann-an-Léigh:<eng> probably Alexander MacLeod of Ferinlea, who is on record in 1746 (More Culloden Papers, iv, 179). Like MacLeod of Ose, he was married to one of Donald MacLeod of Bernera’s numerous progeny (Mackenzie’s History of the MacLeods, 252). The genealogy of this branch of the MacLeods has apparently still to be traced. Cf. Boswell’s Journal of a Tour to the Hebrides, 1936 ed., 212-213.


<gai>MARBHRANN DO ALASDAIR MAC DHOMHNAILL<eng>

The subject of this elegy was Alexander MacDonald, second of Balranald, and seventh in descent from Domhnall Hearach, younger son of Hugh MacDonald of Sleat. He lost his life while riding across the Kirkibost ford in 1760. It is supposed that he fell off his horse in a fit and was drowned by the incoming tide. He was much admired for his personal qualities as well as for his stature and physical strength. He was probably at least sixty-five years old at the time of his death, for he is on record as Alexander MacDonald “of Hougharie” in 1717. Before 1750 he succeeded Captain John MacDonald of Kirkibost as factor for North Uist, when he also obtained a lease of his predecessor’s

[TD 267]

lands of Balranald and Kirkibost. He was twice married, first to a daughter of Rev. Donald Nicolson of Scorribreck, minister of Kilmuir in Skye, and secondly, to Catherine, daughter of Maclean of Boreray. He was succeeded at Balranald by Donald, the only child of the first marriage. His eldest son from his second wife, Alexander <gai>(Am Bàillidh Breac),<eng> was for many years Clanranald’s factor for South Uist, and founded the family of Peninuran (Clan Donald, iii, 487-488).

1219. <gai>Gill-easbuig:<eng> it is impossible to be certain as to the identity of the persons mentioned in this line and the next. This is perhaps Archibald MacDonald of Griminish, sixth in descent from Domhnall Hearach, who died in 1740 (Clan Donald, iii, 485).

1220. <gai>Eoin:<eng> probably Captain John MacDonald of Kirkibost, fifth son of William, Tutor of MacDonald. In 1735 he had the tack of Kendrom in Trotternish, and acted as bailie for the barony. In 1740 he obtained from Sir Alexander MacDonald a tack of the lands of Kirkibost, Kyles, and Balranald in North Uist. About the same time he was appointed factor for the North Uist estate. He had command of one of the Independent Companies in 1745, but he was no doubt at heart favourably disposed towards the Jacobites. When Ned Burke was in hiding in Eaval in the summer of 1746, a near relative of his, Peter MacDonald, gave information against him to Captain MacDonald, who broke a staff over his head “for telling lies” (Lyon in Mourning, i, 197). Captain MacDonald died before 1750 (Clan Donald, iii, 541).

1263. <gai>do chéile:<eng> Catherine, daughter of Maclean of Boreray, as already mentioned. Commiseration for the widow was a regular feature of elegies by bards both trained and vernacular.

1279 ff. A frequently expressed sentiment. Cf. Mary MacLeod:

<gai>Fasan bu dual
Fantalach buan,
Socrach ri tuaith,<eng> etc.
(J. Carmichael Watson’s Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, 64.)

and:

<gai>Cha b’e ’n cleachdadh bh’ aig càch
So ghlac thu mar ghnàths
Bhith smachdail m’an mhàl air tuaith.<eng>
(E., 102.)

[TD 268]

Cf. also Neil Macnab, in his elegy on Martin of Bealach (died c. 1815):

<gai>Dh’fhalbh thu ’n toiseach do bhlàtha,
’S iomadh neach a bha cràiteach mu t’eug;
Bu tu beannachd na tuatha,
’S tu nach feuchadh riu cruaidh air an fhéill;
’S tu a dhèanadh am fuasgladh
Nam faiceadh tu ’n cruadal no ’m feum;
Tha iad uile ’g ad ionndrainn
Fo mhulad ’s làn tùrsa ’n ad dhéidh.<eng>

Cf. further Songs and Poems of Rob Donn (Morrison’s ed.), 7.

1288. <gai>an Di-ciadaoin mu dheireadh de ’n Mhàrt:<eng> the day on which MacDonald was found drowned. Assuming that he died on the same day on which his body was found, the date of his death was thus 27th March 1760.

1296. <gai>Di-màirt:<eng> evidently the day of the funeral—2nd April 1760.

1297 ff. These lines refer to customs observed at Highland funerals long ago. When the corpse was brought out hired women congregated round it, making loud cries, clapping their hands, and tearing their hair or head-dress. When the procession towards the cemetery began the women followed after the men, showing the same signs of grief and wailing broken ex tempore verses in praise of the dead, that is, the coronach. These funeral customs were still commonly observed at the time of the death of Alexander MacDonald of Balranald, but by the end of the eighteenth century they had fallen into disuse. They survived longest in the island of South Uist.

1301-1302. The idea evidently is that others would have wished that he should have died on the field of battle or at home <gai>(bàs a’ chinn-adhairt).<eng> In the old tales these two kinds of death were contrasted, the one being sometimes spoken of as <gai>bás do rinn<eng> (death from a spear-point) and the other as <gai>éc fri adart<eng> and <gai>bás re hadart<eng> (O’Keeffe, Buile Suibhne, Mediæval and Modern Irish Series, i, 3, 85; Stokes and Windisch, Acallam na Senórach, Irische Texte, Fourth Series, i, l. 3590).


<gai>GEARAN AIR A MHNAOI<eng>

Though John eluded the widows of the district, he was, nevertheless, not altogether happy in his third marriage. One man who used to sing this song declared that John’s favourite

[TD 269]

epithet for his spouse was <gai>sgliùrach,<eng> which would indicate that the domestic atmosphere was not altogether what it might be. As appears from this complaint, her capability as a housewife left something to be desired. This may have been at least partly due to the fact that she suffered from asthma. Her name, according to tradition, was Mary Robertson. She survived her husband.

1303. <gai>lìon:<eng> flax was grown extensively in Uist in MacCodrum’s time, and the industry was encouraged by Lady Margaret MacDonald. Cf. l. 1015 n.

1356. <gai>Bhirgìni:<eng> Virginia, the earliest of the American colonies, founded (after three unsuccessful attempts by Sir Walter Raleigh in the time of Queen Elizabeth) in 1607. MacDonalds and MacLeods, and others from the Isles of Skye and Raasay, settled on the York and Rappahannock Rivers in Virginia during the first half of the eighteenth century, and the reference to Virginia may have been specially topical about the time that the song was composed owing to the fact that at the close of the Seven Years’ War (1763) many soldiers in Highland regiments who were then disbanded or had completed their terms of service received grants of land in Virginia and the Carolinas (Celtic Review, vii, 295). When the great exodus from the Highlands began c. 1770, it was to the Carolinas that most of the emigrants went. Virginia was famous, then as now, for its tobacco, of which the Islanders were fond even as early as 1700, when they were described by Martin.


<gai>AOIR NAN TÀILLEIREAN<eng>

This lampoon is interesting because it led to MacCodrum’s being appointed bard to Sir James MacDonald of Sleat. In those days it was the custom for tailors to do their work in their employer’s house and remain there until it was completed, whereupon they moved on somewhere else where work was awaiting them. In MacCodrum’s time the life of the hunter, the seafarer, and the warrior was still most admired, as the songs of the period show. The sedentary nature of a tailor’s work was in direct contrast, and was often left to those who were physically incapable of enjoying a more active life. It is not surprising, therefore, that the craft should be somewhat despised by a race who still retained something of the primitive belief that physical strength

[TD 270]

and valour were to be desired in a man above all else. This attitude gave rise to such sayings as:

<gai>Cha bu duine tàilleir;
Cha bu duine dhà dhiubh;
Chuireadh am fitheach le creig
Dà fhichead ’s a dhà dhiubh.<eng>

It can be understood that when MacCodrum was, as he thought, treated in somewhat cavalier fashion by the tailors, the impulse to make fun of them was hard to resist. There were twenty-five tailors in North Uist in 1794 (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 313).

The following account of the circumstances connected with the song was written in 1898 by Malcolm Ferguson, Long Beach, Port Morien, Cape Breton, who emigrated from South Uist in 1843 (MT., ii, no. 49). Some additions from UB., x-xi, and current tradition are made in brackets.

<gai>Bha MacCodrum a’ fuireach an Uibhist a’ Chinn a Tuath, an àite ris an abradh iad Taobh Loch Eubhord [i.e. Langais]. Bha an t-àite anabarrach fada bho dhaoine. Cha bhiodh na tàilleirean deònach a dhol a dh’obair ann; cha bhiodh spòrs gu leòr ann, no dannsa, no dad de ’n t-seòrsa sin. Bhiodh a bhean a’ deanamh pìosan clò; ’s chaidh e dh’iarraidh tàilleir uair no dhà ’s cha tigeadh iad. Rinn e an sin òran dhaibh, agus an uair a chuala iad an t-òran thug iad bòid nach fuaighleadh iad greim dha ged a bhiodh e falbh rùisgte.

Bhiodh Morair Chlann Domhnaill a’ tighinn tacan do ’n dùthaich a h-uile samhradh [foghar], ’s bhiodh còisir de dhaoine uaisle eile maille ris. Bhiodh greadhnachas mór ann an latha thigeadh e; bhiodh móran de ’n tuath ag cruinneachadh far am bitheadh e, ’g a mholadh fhéin ’s a’ moladh athar ’s a sheanar. Bha MacCodrum ’s toil aige bhith ’n am measg; bha nàire air a dhol ann cho luideach, ach chaidh e ann is dh’fhuirich e ann an imeall na cuideachd. Thug am Morair an aire dha. Dh’fharraid e dh’ an duine bha ri thaobh có an duine bochd a bha siod. Arsa an duine uasal, “Siod MacCodrum am bàrd!” “Agus ciod e chuir cho luideach e?” arsa am Morair. “Tha,” arsa esan, “nach fhaigh e tàilleir a nì car dha.” “’S cinnteach nach ’eil e ’g am pàigheadh, ma ta,” arsa am Morair. “Tha,” arsa an duine uasal, “ach rinn e òran daibh, ’s cha tig duine aca thuige.” “Abair ris tighinn far a bheil mi,” arsa am Morair. Thàinig MacCodrum a nuas, ’s dh’fhaighneachd am Morair dheth ciod e chuir cho rùisgte siod e. Dh’innis esan nach

[TD 271]

fhaigheadh e tàilleir [a chionn ’s gun do rinn e òran ’g an càineadh. Dh’iarr am Morair air an t-òran a ghabhail, ’s rinn e siod.] Thubhairt am Morair ris an duine uasal, “Thoir thusa dha deise aodaich air mo chosg-sa, ’s bheir mise dha an t-àite ’s a bheil e fuireach saor gun mhàl [mar bhàrd.” Fhuair e a thuilleadh air a sin duais airgid agus cóig bollachan mine ’s a’ bhliadhna. An uair a chuala Iain mu dheighinn na mine, thuirt e, “Gu neartaicheadh Dia sibhse, Thighearn, ’s math an t-aran, ach b’fheàirrd e an t-annlan,” agus chaidh cóig clachan càise a chur ris a’ mhin.]

’S e so pàirt dhe ’n òran a rinn e dha na tàilleirean:—

[Cóig rainn de dh’ Aoir nan Tàilleirean.]<eng>
(MT., vii, 14.)

(MacCodrum was living in North Uist, in a place called Loch Eford Side [i.e. Langash]. The place was exceedingly far from people. The tailors would not be willing to go to work there; there would not be enough fun there, or dancing, or anything of that kind. His wife used to make pieces of tweed; and he went for a tailor once or twice and they would not come. He then composed a song to them, and when they heard the song they made a vow that they would not sew a stitch for him though he were to go naked.

The Chief of Clan Donald used to come for a short time to the district every summer [autumn], and a company of other gentlemen along with him. There used to be a great display the day he came; many of the tenants used to gather where he was, praising himself and praising his father and his grandfather. MacCodrum was desirous to be among them; he was ashamed to go so ragged, but he went and stayed on the fringe of the assembly. The Chief noticed him. He asked the man who was beside him what poor man that was. Said the gentleman, “That is MacCodrum the bard!” “And why is he so ragged?” said the Chief. “Because,” said he, “he cannot find a tailor who will do a hand’s turn for him.” “I suppose he does not pay them, then,” said the Chief. “He does,” said the gentleman, “but he composed a song to them, and none of them will come to him.” “Tell him to come to speak to me,” said the Chief. MacCodrum came forward, and the Chief asked him why he was as bare as that. He explained that he could not get a tailor [because he had composed a song lampooning them. The Chief asked him to sing the song, and he did that.] The Chief said to the gentleman,

[TD 272]

“You give him a suit of clothes at my expense, and I will give him the place where he stays free without rent [as bard.” He received besides a grant of money and five bolls of meal a year. When John heard about the meal, he said, “God strengthen you, my lord, bread is good, but it would be the better for condiment,” and five stones of cheese were added to the meal.]

This is part of the song he composed to the tailors:—

[Five verses of the Satire on the Tailors.])

According to one account (MC., xlvi) the bard had gone to live in Eaval when the song was composed, but current tradition and indications in one or two other songs agree with Ferguson. It can be proved from Sir James’s correspondence that it was not in summer, but in autumn, that he was in the habit of visiting North Uist. The meeting with MacCodrum took place between 6th August and 19th September 1763, probably in the former month (see Introduction). The term <gai>Morair<eng> is perhaps not quite appropriate as Sir James’s successor was the first of the Sleat family to hold a peerage. The statement that MacCodrum’s wife made tweeds is perhaps evidence that there is some exaggeration in his complaint against her <gai>(Gearan air a Mhnaoi)<eng> and in l. 1333.

The descendants of some at least of the tailors in the song can still be identified in Uist.

1376. <gai>na bàghannan:<eng> numerous smaller inlets branch off from the long length of Loch Eford. MacCodrum probably had in mind those near his home at Langash—<gai>Loch Langais, Bàgh Orasaidh, and Ob a’ Ghibhte.<eng>

1385. <gai>nam Mìlidh: mìlidh<eng> = (1) soldier (Latin miles); (2) the name of the legendary leader of the Gael when they conquered Ireland. The poet seems to mean: <gai>na mìlidhean<eng> = the Gaelic warriors with special reference to their descent from Mìlidh. For Mìlidh see BGh., l. 1652 n. The eighteenth-century tradition on the subject, as given by <gai>Iain mac Ailein,<eng> the Mull bard, appears in the Glenbard Collection (76 ff.). In a lament by the same bard for Sir John Maclean of Duart which was known to MacCodrum (see notes on next song) the following lines occur:

<gai>… geugan ro phrìseil
De fhìon-fhuil na Spàinne.
Bha fios aig luchd-leughaidh
’S aig seanchaidhean geur’
Air bhur teachd o Ghatélus
As an Eiphit a thàinig;

[TD 273]

Sliochd mhìlidhean treuna
Fhuair ceannas na h-Eireann
Mar bha Eber na féile
Agus Eremon dàna.<eng>
(GB., i, 187.)

1407. The next four verses appear to have been somewhat confused in tradition. The text of MC. has been followed here except for two lines that did not make as good sense as UB. It is the most complete version available (unless, as seems likely, it is a fusion of two or more shorter versions) and may be as near the original as any other text now to be got. Nevertheless it is in substantial disagreement with the other two published versions and with current tradition as known to the writer. These sources, while disagreeing upon minor points, agree in allocating the various speeches as follows:

<gai>Labhair mi ri Lachlann
Air a’ Chladach ged bha ’n t-Sàbaid ann;
’S ann thuirt e rium gum b’fhaide leis
Mac Raibeirt bhith gun tàilleireachd—
“’S e meud do gheall air òsdairean
A dh’fhàg do phòca fàs agad,
A dh’fhàg an t-aodach tan’ agad
’S cha chuir a’ bhean ’n a àite dhuit.”

Labhair mi ri Mac-a-Phiocair,
“Do ghealladh tric a shàraich mi;
Gheall thu ’m bliadhna, gheall thu ’n uiridh,
Dh’fhuirich thu ’s cha tàinig thu;
Cha dèan mi tuilleadh brìodail riut
Bho ’n tha mi sgìth dhe t’àbhartan:
’S truagh nach d’ rinn iad griasaich dhiot
’S gum biodh na briagan nàdurra.”

Labhair mi ri mac Aonghuis Ghlais,
“An tig thu mach a màireach dhomh?”
Thuirt e, “’S ann as neònach leam,
’S tu eòlach air an fhàilingeadh:
Nach fhaic thu féin bean òg agam
Nach leig dh’ a deoin air fàthchul mi,
’S ged dh’fhalbhainn-sa cha choisichinn,
’S cha bhithinn an nochd an Càirinis.”

[TD 274]

Labhair mi ri Mac-an-t-Saoir,
“Cha b’aon uair bha mo chàirdeas riut:
B’eòl dhomh agus b’aithne dhomh
Thaobh athar agus màthar thu;
’S cha ruig thu leas bhith smaointeachadh
Gur duine faoin an Gàidhlig mi—
Mholainn agus dh’aoirinn thu
Cho math ri aon ’s a’ Ghàidhealtachd.”<eng>

1410. <gai>Mac Raibeirt:<eng> who he was is unknown. Robert Ferguson was a tenant in Clachan in 1718 (Clan Donald, iii, 659). The name Robert is in Uist practically confined to the Fergusons. The tradition is that a Ferguson from the district near Kildrummy Castle accompanied Robert the Bruce to Uist when the latter was a fugitive, received land there, and named his son after the king. But there may be as much knowledge of book history as of tradition in that. Nevertheless it is a fact that there were Fergusons of old in Aberdeenshire.

1415. <gai>Mac-an-t-Saoir:<eng> unless this is a patronymic, the tailor here mentioned was evidently not of old North Uist stock. Tormet McIntyre was a tenant in Baile Mhic Phàil in 1718 (Clan Donald, iii, 659).

1421-1422. Shoemakers had a reputation for lying, apparently because of their habit of not fulfilling promises to have work completed in a certain time. In the waulking-song, <gai>’S a ghaoil a saoil am faigh mi thu,<eng> wherein the maid objects to suitors of various occupations, the shoemaker is mentioned as follows:

<gai>’S cha ghabh mi idir griasaiche,
’S e briagan trian a’ cheàirde.<eng>

Shoemakers had the same reputation in Ireland (cf. O’Rahilly, Dánfhocail, no. 235).

1426. This refers to MacCodrum’s three experiences of marriage.

1430. <gai>Càirinis:<eng> a village in the extreme south of North Uist, facing the North Ford. Whether this was the abode of the son of Angus Glas or a place of halt on the way to MacCodrum’s home in Langash is not clear.

1431. <gai>Mac-a-Phiocair:<eng> the MacVicars were a family of old standing in North Uist. Towards the end of the sixteenth century Donald MacVicar <gai>(Am Piocair Mór)<eng> and his four sons

[TD 275]

occupied some of the best land in the island. Of the sons, Donald had Carinish and Cladach Carinish; Angus (known as <gai>Aonghus Mhannta<eng>) had Baleloch, Balemartin, and Balelone; Hector had Kyles Bernera, Baile Mhic Phàil, and Baile Mhic Conain; and John, the youngest, dwelt with his father, who held Baleshare and Eaval. Their prosperity aroused the envy of <gai>Uisdean mac Gill-easbuig Chléirich,<eng> the villain par excellence of North Uist story, who is on record as ballivus of the island in 1588. He is said to have murdered the four young MacVicars in cold blood; their father was away from his home at the time and escaped. Vengeance for this crime is said by some to have been the cause of Hugh’s downfall. The widow of Hector MacVicar was particularly fierce in her hatred of him. A waulking-song of which snatches may still be heard sung in Uist is attributed to her:

<gai>Him bò-ill ù-ill ò
Hu-o hò-ro éile-na
Him bò-ill ù-ill ò.
Uisdein mhic Gill-easbuig Chléirich
Far an laigh thu slàn na éirich
Do mhionach bhith ’n luib do léine
Sgeul do bhàis do mhnathan Shléite
Is thugam-sa mo chuid fhéin dheth.<eng>

She is said to have helped the party, headed by <gai>Domhnall mac Iain mhic Sheumais,<eng> who arrested Hugh in the vicinity of <gai>Dùn an Sticir,<eng> with encouragement and advice. There are, however, several accounts of the circumstances of Hugh’s capture (Beveridge’s North Uist, 142-144, 287-288).

The lesser of the Carinish temples is called <gai>Teampull Clann a’ Phiocair.<eng> In 1861 there were ninety-eight persons bearing the name in North Uist (Proc. Soc. Antiq. Scot., xi, 505-506).


<gai>ORAN DO SHIR SEUMAS MACDHOMHNAILL

Do Shir S(h)eumus Triath Chlann-do(mh)nuill a dh’eug san Roimh,<eng> M.

All accounts agree that Sir James MacDonald, eighth baronet of Sleat, the subject of this eulogy, was deserving of high praise. His intellectual gifts, his charming personality, and his uprightness of character were admired wherever he went. This knowledge enables us to absolve the bard from the charge of fulsomeness which he might otherwise incur.

Sir James was born in 1741 and was brought up (apparently at Kingsburgh) to speak Gaelic. His father, Sir Alexander

[TD 276]

MacDonald, writing in 1744, says that his “son Jamie is getting more Gallick at Kingsburgh than tongue can tell” (Canon R. C. MacLeod’s The Island Clans, 108). His early removal to Eton prevented him from gaining an extensive knowledge of the language, but he took up its study in his latter years during his sojourns in Skye and North Uist. He went to Oxford in 1759, and there, as at Eton, he acquitted himself with great distinction. On leaving Oxford he made the grand tour of Europe in the company of the Duke of Buccleuch and Adam Smith. In 1761—while perhaps at Oxford—he came north and visited North Uist. While there he was shot in the foot on 3rd August (Correspondence of Sir William Pepys, i, 259) by the accidental discharge of Colonel MacLeod of Talisker’s gun, and was attended at Vallay by Niel Beaton (v. ll. 864 n, 869 n; Clan Donald, iii, 96). He was again in North Uist in the autumn of 1763, having then come of age and undertaken the management of his estates. One of his first acts on arriving in Uist on this occasion was to appoint John MacCodrum his bard (v. notes on previous song and Introduction). It is generally supposed that this eulogy of Sir James was MacCodrum’s first composition in his official capacity.

1447 ff. MacCodrum apparently composed the song to the air of an elegy by the Mull bard, John Maclean <gai>(Iain mac Ailein),<eng> to Sir John Maclean of Duart (d. 1716). At any rate, he had it in mind, as appears from comparing his first verse with Maclean’s second verse:

<gai>Nach iongnadh ri chlaistinn
Gu bheil mis’ o chinn fhada					(cf. l. 1448)
Ann an turracadaich cadail
Agus m’acaid ro chràiteach;
Tha cnoimh air mo ghiùlan					(cf. l. 1451).
’S mi leisg air a dùsgadh
Air eagal le bùrach
Gun ùraich i ’m bàs dhomh.
Gidheadh cha sgeul rùine
Ach sgeul as mór cùram
Sir Iain gun dùsgadh
An dlùthchiste chlàraidh:
B’e so ar fras dhùbailt
Mhill ar n-abhall ’s ar n-ubhlan,
Rinn ar dosgaidh a chrùnadh
Fhrois am flùr bhàrr a’ ghàraidh.<eng>
(E., 126.)

[TD 277]

The first eight lines seem to refer to the death of Sir Alexander MacDonald and to the fact that the clan continued to feel their loss for sixteen years until Sir James came of age.

1463 ff. Apparently a reference to the death of four chiefs between 1718 and 1746.

1467-1468. Cf. the chorus of a song composed in 1715 by <gai>Iain Dubh mac Iain mhic Ailein:

Héi hó!
Tha mulad air m’inntinn
Cho trom ri cloich mhuilinn
Air lunnaibh ’n a sìneadh.<eng>
(Glenbard Coll., 46.)

1473. <gai>ceannard:<eng> Sir Alexander MacDonald, who died in 1746. He is the subject of ll. 1471-1486. Sir Alexander refused to join in the rising of 1745 and consequently incurred the abuse of certain of the Jacobites, whose opinion of him was pithily expressed in the following lines:

<gai>Mac Dhomhnaill gun sgoinn
Chaidh a chomhdach ’s an fhoill,
’N uair bu chòir dha bhith dìleas
’S ann a dhìbir e ghreim.<eng>
(cf. Glenbard Coll., 126.)

Other witnesses, however, bear high testimony to his character. (See also Clan Donald, iii, 85-92, 475.)

1491. Sir James was apparently away from the Isles when the song was composed.

1505. The reference is to some card game, but <gai>cairt dhùbailt<eng> is obscure.

1506. <gai>Ar crùn:<eng> a crowned piece in draughts, also termed <gai>crùnair.<eng>

1510. A common description of a chief.

1513-1514. In the Roman Catholic Church prayers to God for special favours are often accompanied by some particular form of good works such as giving alms to the poor <gai>(nasgadh déirce do na bochdan).<eng> Hence, by association, the present passage practically means “We are praying earnestly to God that thou mayest return home in safety.” <gai>Nasgadh<eng> strictly means pledging or ear-marking in advance what is to be given away as alms.

[TD 278]

1520. Another common description of a chief. Cf. MacCodrum’s predecessor MacBeathaig in note on l. 997.

1546. The usual phrase is <gai>gun taise gun tàire.<eng>

1629. <gai>O Chlach:<eng> Cloch Point, at the mouth of the Clyde opposite Dunoon.

1630. <gai>Port Phàdruig:<eng> Port Patrick in Wigtownshire, facing the Irish coast. MacCodrum’s knowledge of the geography of the south of Scotland may seem surprising. It is quite possible, however, that he was personally acquainted with the Firth of Clyde. In the eighteenth century Islanders frequently went as far as Glasgow in open boats.

The meaning appears to be that the inhabitants of the Ayrshire coast, which practically extends between the two points mentioned, would rise in support of Sir James. The connexion MacCodrum had in mind was that Lady Margaret MacDonald, Sir James’s mother, belonged to the great Ayrshire family of Montgomery; she was a daughter of Alexander, ninth Earl of Eglintoun (Clan Donald, iii, 475).

1631. In Kintyre were the MacAllisters of Loup, descended from Alexander, son of Donald, eponymus of the clan; the MacDonalds of Largie; and the MacDonalds of Sanda, a branch of whom were in North Uist in MacCodrum’s time (Clan Donald, iii, 184, 380, 387).

1639. <gai>na Leòdaich:<eng> Sir Alexander MacDonald, father of Sir James, was a son of Janet, daughter of Alexander MacLeod of Greshornish (Clan Donald, iii, 474). There were also earlier alliances between the Sleat family and the MacLeods, both of Dunvegan and Lewis.

1658. <gai>Sìol Ailein:<eng> the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald, descended from Allan, son of Reginald, son of John Lord of the Isles. The sense would almost seem to imply that the men of Glengarry were among Allan’s descendants; but the MacDonalds of Glengarry trace their descent, not to Allan, but to his younger brother Donald (Clan Donald, iii, 226).

1671. <gai>fir Mhuile:<eng> the Macleans, particularly perhaps the family of Duart. Donald Gormeson, sixth Chief of Sleat <gai>(Domhnall Gorm Sasannach),<eng> married Mary, daughter of Hector Mór Maclean of Duart (Clan Donald, iii, 469). MacCodrum was probably thinking of this ancestress when he claimed the House of Duart as one of Sir James’s allies.

[TD 279]

1679 ff. The Macleans took part in the rising of 1745.

1683. <gai>nan Duibhneach:<eng> the Campbells, whose descent, in the oldest extant pedigrees, is traced to one Duibhne, whom later genealogists wrongly regarded as a son of Diarmaid, one of the leading figures in the Fionn cycle (Skene, Celtic Scotland, iii, 459).

Cf. note on ll. 926 ff.


<gai>TÀLADH IAIN MHÙIDEARTAICH<eng>

This song was taken down by the late Rev. John MacRury, Snizort, from Neil MacDonald, Torlum, Benbecula, over sixty years ago. It was communicated by Mr MacRury to the editor of the Oranaiche, where it was published with the omission (probably inadvertent) of seven lines. The following is Mr MacRury’s account of the occasion of the song (also in the Oranaiche with some changes):

<gai>Air do ’n bhàrd air latha àraidh tighinn a Uibhist mu Thuath do Bhaile nan Cailleach, mhothaich Mac ’ic Ailein dha a nuas o ’n chladh is ghabh e ceum suas ’n a choinnimh is Iain Mùideartach, is gun e an uair sin ach ’n a phàisde beag, air làimh aige. [Suas chun a’ Bhaile Aird (O.)] chuir e bonn òir an làimh Iain Mhùideartaich is dh’iarr e air a dhol an coinnimh a’ bhàird is an t-òr a thoirt da. Rinn am pàisde so. Dh’fhaighnich am bàrd dheth an robh tuilleadh òir no airgid aige, is thuirt e nach robh. “Ma ta,” arsa am bàrd, “chan ’eil oighre fearainn fo ’n ghréin a bheireadh dhomhsa a chuid dh’ an t-saoghal ach thu fhéin.” Thog e am pàisde air a ghualainn is choisich e sìos chun an tighe. ’S e bun a bh’ ann cha ghabhte uaithe an gille mur dèanadh e òran dha. Thuirt e gu feuchadh e ri siod a dhèanamh na faigheadh e a dh’ùine fhad ’s a bhiodh e dol timcheall a’ ghàraidh-chàil. Fhuair e siod. An uair a thàinig e air ais thog e Iain Mùideartach ’n a uchd is sheinn e dha an t-òran.<eng>

(The bard on a certain day having come from North Uist to Nunton, Clanranald noticed him coming along from the cemetery and took a walk over to meet him having John of Moidart, at that time only a little child, by the hand. When approaching Baile Ard he put a gold piece in John of Moidart’s hand, and asked him to go and meet the bard and give him the gold. The child did so. The bard asked him if he had any more gold or silver, and he said that he had not. “Well,” said the bard, “there is not a landed heir under the sun who would give me

[TD 280]

his share of worldly wealth but thyself.” He lifted the child on his shoulder and walked over to the house. The outcome was that the boy would not be taken from him unless he made a song for him. He said he would try to do that if he got as much time as he would take going round the kail-yard. He was granted that. When he came back he lifted John of Moidart in his arms and sang the song to him.)

John of Moidart was the elder son of Ranald MacDonald, eighteenth Chief of Clan Ranald, by his second marriage with Flora, daughter of John Mackinnon, yr. of Mackinnon, which took place in June 1759. The date of the song can therefore hardly be earlier than 1763, when the boy was perhaps three years old. It cannot be later than 1766, in which year his grandfather, with whom he was walking when the bard made his appearance (l. 1688), died. John of Moidart succeeded his father in 1776 and died in Edinburgh in 1794. He was buried at Holyrood (Clan Donald, iii, 235-236).

1689. <gai>iarogha:<eng> John of Moidart’s great-grandfather was Donald MacDonald of Benbecula, on whom devolved the chiefship of Clan Ranald when Ranald, fifteenth Chief, died in 1725. He died in 1730, and was buried in the churchyard mentioned above, <gai>Cladh Mhoire,<eng> Nunton (Clan Donald, iii, 233-234, 279).

1702. John of Moidart actually succeeded to the chiefship in 1776 before MacCodrum’s death. That the bard, who had probably reached the allotted span when the song was composed, should live to see another two chiefs of Clan Ranald would seem most improbable at the time.

1706. <gai>a’ chruit:<eng> harp playing was not much in evidence by the date of this song. Cf. ll. 999-1000 and note.

1708. <gai>do sheanmhar:<eng> John of Moidart’s grandmother was Margaret, daughter of William MacLeod of Bernera, eldest son of Sir Norman MacLeod of Bernera and Katherine, daughter of Sir James MacDonald of Sleat. She married Ranald, seventeenth Chief of Clan Ranald, in 1720, and died at Ormiclate on 20th September 1780. She was buried at Nunton (Clan Donald, iii, 234-235).

1709. MacCodrum here alludes to his ability to trace the genealogy of the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald and perhaps also

[TD 281]

of the MacLeods of Bernera. For the old professional bards a knowledge of genealogy was essential.

1723. <gai>an t-àrmunn Sléiteach:<eng> in the conventional enumeration of allies MacCodrum first mentions his own patron, Sir James MacDonald, whom he no doubt regarded as head of the whole clan.

1729. <gai>Fir Chnòideart:<eng> the MacDonalds of Knoydart were descended from Allan, second Chief of Clan Ranald. They lost the lands of Knoydart about 1610 (Clan Donald, iii, 238-239). It is possible that members of the family may have preserved their identity as tenants of the tacksman class down to MacCodrum’s time.

1737. <gai>Domhnallaich ’s Raghnallaich:<eng> MacDonalds (? of Sleat) and MacDonalds of Clan Ranald.

1757. <gai>Arla:<eng> Harlaw. The battle of Harlaw is usually termed in Gaelic <gai>Cath Gairbheach.<eng> It was fought in 1411 when Donald of the Isles attempted to make good his claim to lands in Aberdeenshire as part of the Earldom of Ross. His opponents consisted of the burgesses of Aberdeen and the men of Angus and the Mearns under the command of the Earl of Mar and the Sheriff of Angus. The battle itself was indecisive. The Islesmen’s losses were much less than those of the opposing army but they advanced no farther and Donald withdrew with his men towards the west.

1758. <gai>’N uair bhrosnaich Lachlann am bàrd iad:<eng> the bard referred to is Lachlan MacVurich. A <gai>Brosnachadh<eng> ascribed to him was published in Ranald MacDonald’s Collection in 1776, where it is described as <gai>“Prosnuchadh-catha roinaidh le Lachun-Moir-Macmhuireach-Albinnich, do Dhomhnil a Iola, Riodh Inshagoill, agus Eairla Rois; la Machrich Cathgariach, Thugidh è June 11, 1411.”<eng> It begins:

<gai>A Chlannaibh Cuinn cuimhnichibh
Cruas an am na h-iorghuille
Gu …<eng> etc., etc.
(E., 5.)

The language is so modern, however, as to throw serious doubts upon its authenticity.

1764. <gai>là blàir Léinidh:<eng> the battle of Kinloch Lochy (1545), in which the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald under their chief,

[TD 282]

John of Moidart, almost annihilated the Frasers (Book of Clan Ranald in Rel. Celt., ii, 170).

1771. Alasdair ’s Mhontròïs: the Highlanders regarded Alexander MacDonald <gai>(Alasdair mac Cholla Chiotaich)<eng> as of greater consequence than Montrose (cf. MacVurich in the Book of Clan Ranald); he is therefore usually mentioned first. MacDonald met his death after the battle of Cnoc na nOs in Cork on 13th November 1647. He is perhaps the chief heroic Gaelic figure of the modern period. (For an account of his life see Clan Donald, iii, 596-609.) There are several biographies of Montrose, including one by Mr John Buchan.

1772. <gai>an Lòchaidh:<eng> the battle of Inverlochy (1645), in which Montrose routed the Covenanting army of the Marquis of Argyll.

1773. <gai>Domhnall:<eng> Donald, later thirteenth Chief of Clan Ranald, was present at the battle of Inverlochy as well as his father, John of Moidart, whom he succeeded in 1670. He died in Canna in 1686 and was buried at Howmore (Book of Clan Ranald in Rel. Celt., ii, 183, 209).

1785. <gai>Coille Chnagaidh:<eng> the battle of Killiecrankie, usually termed in Gaelic <gai>Latha Raoin Ruairidh<eng> (cf. l. 1790), fought between the Highland army under James Graham of Claverhouse, Viscount Dundee, and the forces of the Government, in 1689. The Highlanders won the day, but their leader was killed, and without him the rising soon came to nothing.

1786. <gai>MacAidh:<eng> General Mackay, who was in command of the Government troops. This is an old form of the name still in use in Uist. It is the form used by Aonghus mac Alasdair Ruaidh in his song on the battle of Killiecrankie. Addressing Sir Ewen Cameron, he says:

<gai>’S nam maireadh tu uile
O ’n uiridh mar bha thu,
Gum falbhadh Rìgh Uilleam,
’S cha chumadh MacAidh e.<eng>
(E., 192.)

1792. <gai>Sliabh an t-Siorraim:<eng> the battle of Sheriffmuir, which, though indecisive in itself, resulted in the abandonment by the Earl of Mar and the other Jacobite leaders of the attempt made in 1715 to restore the House of Stuart. The Hanoverian forces were under the command of the Duke of Argyll. The MacDonalds of Clan Ranald fought in the Jacobite army.

[TD 283]

1794. <gai>Ailean:<eng> Allan MacDonald, known as <gai>Ailean Beag,<eng> fourteenth Chief of Clan Ranald, who was mortally wounded in the battle. He was taken to Drummond Castle, where he died on the following day. To judge from the number of elegies, bardic and vernacular, and other songs, composed to him, his popularity in the Highlands was immense. It is still told in the Isles that the news of his fall in the battle was first known in Barra, from whence it reached his own lands in Uist. The story is that a certain old man in the village of Borve who had the gift of <gai>frìth<eng> or divination stood on a knoll all day and described the fight to his neighbours. <gai>“Thuit Mac ’ic Ailein,”<eng> said he. <gai>“An do mharbhadh e?”<eng> he was asked. <gai>“Chan urrainn mi a ràdh, ach thatar ’g a ghiùlan a mach as an àraich is fhuil ’g a dhalladh.”<eng> It will be remembered that Neil MacVurich heard that he was wounded before the news of his death arrived, and composed a song on receiving each piece of news (E., 73, 75).

It is said that before boarding his galley in Loch Eynort Allan had been put under a charm <gai>(sian)<eng> by a woman from Bornish to render him invulnerable. The usual sian of the Clan Ranald chiefs is supposed to have been a piece of club-moss <gai>(garbhag an t-sléibhe)<eng> and a piece of caul <gai>(currac-rath)<eng> which was put into the warrior’s pocket by an unmarried man or woman, an incantation being said the while for each piece. Research has failed to disclose what was said for the caul, but the club-moss was bestowed with the following words:

<gai>Fhir a shiubhlas gu h-eutrom,
Chan eagal dhuit beud no puthar,
Is garbhag an t-sléibhe
Bhith air do shiubhal.<eng>

On this occasion, however, the charm used is said to have been:

<gai>Sian roimh shaighead shìdheach,
Sian roimh ghath shluaghach,
Sian roimh luaidhe shaoghlach,
Sian a’ bheatha bhòrcach bhuan
Roimh shluagh agus roimh bhaoghal.<eng>

But there was another woman from Stoneybridge <gai>(Staoinebrig),<eng> a widow, whose only son Clanranald had taken away against her will and despite her threats. His name was Dubh-sìdh, according to present tradition (perhaps by confusion with the murderer of Maclean of Duart at the battle of <gai>Tràigh Ghruinneard<eng>). “If

[TD 284]

you will take my son from me,” said his mother, “I will make you rue the day you were born. They call you little Allan of the big heart. I call you little Allan of the hard heart.” She baked two bannocks, and asked her son whether he would take the big bannock with her malison or the little bannock with her blessing. The son chose the little bannock with her blessing. “Here are the two,” said she, “the little bannock with the blessing of thy mother for hindering little Allan of the cold heart from ever breaking the heart of the widow again, and the big bannock with thy mother’s malison if thou allowest little Allan of the bold heart ever again to return to Uist. And may thine eye never behold the countenance of the lovely Mary of Grace the Mother of the Christ of the Pasch if thou deceivest thine own mother, my son. Here to thee is a crooked silver sixpence with which to bring down the bold Allan of the proud heart.” This sixpence, seven times cursed, the widow’s son is said to have fired from his musket, wounding Clanranald when the latter was going scatheless in the hottest part of the fight on the field of Sheriffmuir. This is actually a tale or tales of much greater antiquity than would appear from the above, and it is interesting to find such a comparatively modern figure as Allan MacDonald of Clan Ranald associated with it (Carmina Gadelica, ii, 26; Celtic Review, vi, 280-283).

1795. <gai>b’fhearail a chruth:<eng> Clanranald derived the sobriquet <gai>Ailean Beag<eng> from the fact that the breadth of his shoulders made him appear short of stature.


<gai>MOLADH CHLANN DOMHNAILL

Moladh Chlann Do(mh)nuill Leis,<eng> M. <gai>Moladh Chloinn Donaill, leis an Ian MacCodrum cheudna,<eng> G.

The song is well known in Uist under the name of <gai>Cuideachadh Dhomhnaill mhic Fhionnlaigh.<eng> It appears that a young man of this name came to Uist some time before the date of the song and on one occasion expressed his appreciation of the kindness he received from the Uist people. Regretting that he had not the bardic gift to sing their praises, he said:

<gai>’S truagh nach b’aithne dhomh ur moladh
Eadar fhearaibh agus mhnathan
Agus ghillean agus chloinn.<eng>

MacCodrum happened to be in the company, and he came to the speaker’s assistance with the song in praise of Clan Donald.

[TD 285]

There has apparently been some confusion between the story attached to it and the similar story told regarding <gai>Oran do Mhac ’ic Ailein,<eng> which is given in the notes to that song (cf. UB., xxxiii, xxxiv-xxxv).

1799. Who <gai>Domhnall mac Fhionnlaigh<eng> was it is perhaps impossible to say now. All that is known of him has been mentioned supra. It has been noted (BGh., 280) that he had the same patronymic as the person mentioned in the <gai>Coimhstrith;<eng> but the latter was not a stranger to Uist (l. 848 n.).

1807 ff. This verse seems to show that the lines by Domhnall mac Fhionnlaigh which have been quoted are only a fragment.

1816. <gai>pòghad:<eng> this word has been suppressed in G. and all subsequent printed versions, but it may be objected that the alternatives put forward do not give the required rime. In M. (where all English words have been deleted and Gaelic words, usually inferior metrically, written above) poet has, strangely enough, been allowed to stand. The fact that present-day tradition and the earliest version agree on the matter independently is conclusive. The writer once heard a Uistman comment on the word as follows: <gai>Bhiodh m’athair ag gabhail an òrain so leam an uair a bha mi beag, agus an uair a thigeadh e chun an fhacail so stadadh e agus theireadh e, “’S a bheil fhios agad ciod e th’ ann pòbhad (sic)?” Theirinn-sa nach robh. “Tha, ma tha, bàrd.” Agus cha do thuig mi riamh gur e facal Beurla a bh’ ann gu robh mi treis mhath a’ dol dh’ an sgoil.<eng>

1856. <gai>Mac Dhomhnaill:<eng> Sir James MacDonald, for whom see notes on Oran do Shir Seumas and Marbhrann do Shir Seumas.

1863. <gai>Mac ’ic Ailein: Ranald,<eng> seventeenth Chief of Clan Ranald, who died at Nunton on 6th March 1766 (Clan Donald, iii, 234-235). The chiefs of Clan Ranald were known by this patronymic because of their descent from the son of Allan, son of Ranald or Reginald (whence <gai>Clann Raghnaill<eng>), son of John, Lord of the Isles.

1866. Cf. l. 1771 n.

1870. <gai>abhainn Lòchaidh:<eng> cf. l. 1772 n.

1874. Cf. l. 1729 n.

1877-1878. The two places are mentioned apparently as the bounds of Knoydart to north and south. Loch Hourn lies between

[TD 286]

Knoydart and Glenelg, and Loch Nevis between Knoydart and Morar. For <gai>Bun na Stuaighe<eng> compare Alexander Mackinnon, the Morar bard:

<gai>Tighinn gu cala na stuaighe
’N aodann gaillinn ’g a cruaidhead
’S lom a ghearradh tu ’m fuaradh le sròin.<eng>
(Glenbard Coll., 249 (29).)

Cf. also the same bard:

<gai>Tha Clann Mhic Dhubhghaill bho ’n stuaigh
’S an coille dhlùth air a buain;
Bu ghoirt an diùbhail ’s bu chruaidh an sgeul e.<eng>
(Ibid., 253 (33).)

1879. <gai>teaghlach na Ceapaich:<eng> the MacDonalds of Keppoch traced their descent to Alasdair Carrach, fourth son of John, Lord of the Isles. The next line perhaps refers to their losses at Culloden, where Alexander, seventeenth Chief, was killed, or, more probably, to the absence of Ranald, the next chief, in Jamaica, and of his younger brother Alexander in Prince Edward Island. Ranald returned to Keppoch in 1777, and died there in 1788 (Clan Donald, iii, 422-423).

1883. <gai>Mac Iain:<eng> the patronymic of the chief of the MacDonalds of Glen Coe, who were descended from John, son of Angus Og of Isla. It was also the patronymic of the MacDonalds of Ardnamurchan, who were descended from Iain Sprangach, third son of Angus Mór, Lord of the Isles. But they disappeared as a landed family long before the time of the song, and MacCodrum meant the family of Glen Coe (Clan Donald, 210, 212).

1887. <gai>Mac Dhubhghaill a Latharn:<eng> the MacDougalls are generally supposed to be of the same stock as the Clan Donald, but the genealogists disagree as to the nature of the relationship—whether the Dubhghall from whom they take their name was a brother or a son or a grandson of Somerled (Skene, Celtic Scotland, iii, 338, 470; Clan Donald, i, 60 ff., iii, 179-180).

1889. Cf. l. 1631 n.

1890. The MacDonalds had lost their lands in Islay before MacCodrum’s time. Sir James MacDonald, the last representative of the MacDonalds of <gai>Dùn Naomhaig (Clann Iain Mhóir)<eng> died in London in 1626 (Clan Donald, iii, 379).

[TD 287]

1891. <gai>Iarla Aondroma:<eng> at the time of the song the head of the MacDonalds of Antrim was Alexander, fifth Earl (Clan Donald, iii, 415).

1895 ff. This rendering of the verse can be justified on the same grounds as l. 1816—present tradition and the unpublished original rendering in M. are in agreement.

1899 ff. This is a claim frequently made for the MacDonalds.

1903. <gai>baile ’s leth Alba:<gai> this phrase often occurs in Gaelic as a conventional mode of describing the major part of a country. Deirdre uses the phrase in her lament for the sons of Uisneach:

<gai>Bail’ agus leth Albainn féin
Do bhiodh agam, àrd an ceum.<eng>
(Leabhar na Féinne, 23, col. 2.)

Cf. Iain Lom:

<gai>Sliochd nan curaidhnean talmhaidh
Leis ’n do chuireadh Cath Gairbheach;
Fhuair mi uiread dh’ ur seanchas
Gun robh ’n turus ud ainmeil,
Gun robh tigh is leth Alba fo ’r cìs.<eng>
(E., 199.)

So also Archibald Grant:

<gai>Tigh is leth Albainn
Bha siod aig rìgh Fionnghall.<eng>
(Songs and Poems, 56.)

and:

<gai>Sliochd nan Collaidhean garga
Leis na chuireadh Cath Gailbheach,
’S Domhnall Ballach nan garbhchreach
Rinn Tigh nan Teud aig leth Alba ’n a chrìch.<eng>
(Ibid., 94.)

For early references see BGh. (281), where it is stated that <gai>Tigh nan Teud<eng> is “about two miles north of Pitlochry, on the east side of the road just below Bridge of Garry.”

That the phrase means “half of Alba and a little more” is illustrated by the following similar passage:

<gai>Gur càirdeach thu Dhomhnall
Dh’ an robh còrr is leth Alba fo smachd.<eng>
(E., 355.)

[TD 288]

The phrase is not applied exclusively to Scotland; in a song to Sir Hector Maclean, who was killed at the battle of Inverkeithing, the following verse occurs:

<gai>Gun robh tigh is leth Ile
Am bann daingeann dhuit sgrìobhte,
’S bha na fearainn sin strìochdte gu réidh dhuit.<eng>
(GB., i, 56.)

It was also used in Ireland, as in the following lines by Eón Rua Ó Súileabháin:

<gai>Go mb’fhearra liom bé na gcraobhfholt gcíortha gcas
Ar leaba lem thaobh, nú ar aonach taoibh liom seal,
Ná baile is leath Éireann ’s a mbéith le straoille leamh.<eng>
(O’Rahilly, Búrdúin Bheaga, 17.)

Cf. also MacBeathaig in note on l. 997; GSMM., l. 995 ff. n.

1905. <gai>latha Chath Gairbheach:<eng> cf. l. 1757 n.

1909. <gai>Mac Gill-Eathain:<eng> at the battle of Harlaw Hector Maclean of Duart led the right wing of the Highland army at the head of his clan. He and Sir Alexander Irvine of Drum fought in single combat until both fell dead (Clan Donald, i, 156, 162).

1910. <gai>Lachlann cutach Mac-an-Tòisich:<eng> the left wing at the battle of Harlaw was led by Mackintosh (Clan Donald, i, 156). But his name was Malcolm.

1911. Here again, though he has previously mentioned several branches of the clan, the bard seems to regard Sir James MacDonald as representing the whole clan. Cf. l. 1623 and l. 1723 n.

1913. Cf. l. 1890 n.

1914 ff. The clans here mentioned as allies are claimed as such in virtue of having obtained their lands from Sir James’s ancestors. Cf. l. 1902. It would be difficult to substantiate this MacDonald belief. It may be partly explained by the fact that when the Lords of the Isles were also Earls of Ross they were the superiors of some at least of the clans in question.

1917. <gai>Barraich:<eng> cf. Iain Lom:

<gai>Chan ’eil Rothach no Barrach,
Chan ’eil Gallach no Tuathach,
Nach bu dleas da bhith leatsa
’N am caismeachd na h-uaire.<eng>
(Glenbard Coll., 29.)

[TD 289]

It is clear that the bard does not mean the Barra men as he mentions <gai>réisimeid nan Niallach<eng> in l. 1929. See also BGh., 281-282, and ll. 4052, 4078, ibid.

1918. Cf. <gai>Iain Dubh mac Iain mhic Ailein:

Thig Rothaich is Ròsaich
Glé dheònach ’n ar ceann,
Barraich ’n treas seòrsa
Tha ’n comhnaidh measg Ghall.
Clann Donnchaidh cha bhreug so
Gun éireadh ’s gach am;
Mar sin is Clann Reubhair
Nach éisd ach bhith annt’.<eng>
(GB., ii, 10-11.)

<gai>Tàilich:<eng> the form <gai>Sàilich,<eng> which occurs in one source, is also used, though less commonly.

1921 ff. Cf. l. 1671 n.

1928. <gai>réisimeid nan Leòdach:<eng> cf. l. 1639 n.

1929. <gai>réisimeid nan Niallach:<eng> the MacNeils of Barra. Cf. l. 1917 n. They were a notable seafaring clan. It is possible that they also were claimed as allies in virtue of having received their lands from the MacDonalds, this time with some justification, for Gilleonan, son of Roderick, son of Murdoch MacNeil, received a charter of the lands of Barra from Alexander, Earl of Ross and Lord of the Isles, in 1427 (MC., xxxviii). And in an old waulking-board flyting between a Barra woman and a Uist woman the latter tauntingly refers to Barra as:

<gai>Déirc a fhuair sibh
Bhuainn an asgaidh
’N uair a chunnaic
Dia ’n ur n-airc sibh.<eng>
(MC., 235.)

No marriage alliance between the MacNeils and the Sleat family from Hugh onwards is on record. The same applies to the Forbeses and the Frasers.

1932. <gai>Clann Reubhair:<eng> the MacDonalds of Dalness in Glen Etive (BGh., 282). Cf. l. 1918 n. One of the prisoners taken by Captain Ferguson during his progress through Uist in the summer of 1746 was Duncan McRievre, Benbecula, “For having rowed the Pretender’s son from S. Uist to Skye” (Scottish History Society, The Prisoners of the ’Forty-five, iii, 184-185).

[TD 290]

1933. <gai>fir na h-Iubhraich:<eng> i.e. the men of Tomnahurich in Inverness. According to legend, the fairies dwelt there, and MacCodrum may have them in mind. But it is quite probable that he may have been thinking of the Fiann who also, according to one account, were in Tomnahurich (cf. Gregorson Campbell, The Fians, 3-4), and were expected to rise again one day.

1934. <gai>Tòmas:<eng> legend asserted that among the fairy inhabitants of Tomnahurich was Thomas the Rhymer. It was believed that he would reappear among men, and that was to be the signal for a rising in which the true Gaels would crush the power of their enemies, which in the eighteenth century meant the Houses of Hanover and Argyll. For references see BGh., 282-283. Gregorson Campbell in his Superstitions of the Scottish Highlands (271) notes that in the Outer Hebrides <gai>Tòmas Reumhair<eng> or <gai>Tòmas Réim<eng> was said to be in Tomnahurich, and not in Dunbuck Hill <gai>(Dùn Buic)<eng> near Dunbarton: He gives the following version of MacCodrum’s lines:

<gai>Dar thigeadh sluagh Tom na h-Iubhraich
Co dh’éireadh air tùs ach Tòmas?


ORAN FIR GHRÌMINIS<eng>

The traditional title of the song has been retained, although it appears to be a misnomer. The story is that Angus MacDonald of Griminish (eighth in descent from <gai>Domhnall Hearach<eng>), who is said to have been disliked by the neighbouring tacksmen owing to his masterful and overbearing disposition, was the victim of a plot concocted by the latter to get rid of him. Arrangements were made for a wholesale emigration to America, and in order to keep up the deception as long as possible an imposing quantity of baggage was transported to the place of embarkation; but the only baggage which contained anything of value was that of the Goodman of Griminish. The rest contained peats and other articles of a like nature which would give the required bulk and weight. By the time Angus MacDonald discovered what was afoot his preparations were made; he had given up the tenure of Griminish to his brother Archibald, and his pride would not allow him to turn back. He accordingly sailed for America (in 1766) and took up residence at Crane’s Creek, Cumberland Co., North Carolina. His stay there was short, for in 1771 he was back in Uist, though he never regained possession of Griminish. He lived at Balranald on the lands of his kinsman, Donald MacDonald, and died some time after 1785 (Clan Donald, iii, 485-486).

[TD 291]

According to UB. (xxxvii) the tacksmen by the above plot wished to be rid also of their sub-tenants. If this is so MacCodrum gives no indication of the fact.

The song was evidently composed before the pretence of the tacksmen was discovered. It acquired the title <gai>Oran Fir Ghrìminis<eng> retrospectively in course of tradition owing to the fact that the Goodman of Griminish, as already mentioned, was the only one of the tacksmen who fulfilled his declared intention of leaving the country.

1935 ff. Mary MacPherson probably had this song and a version of its air in mind when she composed <gai>’N uair bha mi òg,<eng> which begins:

<gai>Moch ’s mi ’g éirigh
Air bheagan éislein
Air maduinn chéitein
’S mi ann an Os.<eng>

1942. <gai>Innse Gall:<eng> literally Isles of Foreigners, i.e. Norsemen. By this was meant the whole of the Hebrides (Celtic Place-Names of Scotland, 173).

1969. <gai>a’ chuibhle:<eng> the wheel of fortune. Cf. the following in an elegy to Alasdair Dubh of Glengarry:

<gai>Thionndaidh cuibhl’ air Clann Domhnaill
’N treasa conspunn bhith bhuatha.<eng>
(SO., 49.)

So also An Clàrsair Dall:

<gai>Chuaidh a’ chuibhle m’ an cuairt,
Gun do thionndaidh gu fuachd am blàths.<eng>
(E., 103.)

2030. <gai>plang:<eng> the plack was two-thirds of a shilling Scots, or 8/12d sterling. Cf. An Clàrsair Dall:

<gai>Fasan don’ air ’s chan fhiù e plang.<eng>
(GB., i, 153.)

2038. Cf. the proverb, <gai>’N uair as teinne an taod ’s ann as dòcha a bhristeadh.<eng>

2063 ff. The reference here seems uncertain. Perhaps the bard is thinking of the part played by Highlanders in the recent Seven Years’ War (1756-1763). But the number of Islesmen (as distinct from the mainland clans) taking part seems to have been negligible. Another possibility is that he means Highlanders who fought in the French army during the War of the Austrian Succession.

[TD 292]

<gai>ORAN DO CHAIPTEAN AILEAN CHINNSEBORGH<eng>

The subject of the song was Captain Allan MacDonald, then younger of Kingsburgh (he succeeded his father, Alexander MacDonald of Kingsburgh, in 1772). Details regarding him and his wife, Flora MacDonald, are so well known that they need not be given here. An interesting comparison could be drawn between MacCodrum’s description of Captain Allan and that given by Boswell (Journal of a Tour to the Hebrides, 1936 ed., 159).

According to one Uistman who knows the song well, it was sung to Captain Allan and his wife by the bard on the occasion of a visit which he paid to Kingsburgh, still remembered because of his witty remarks to the domestics, who did not know who he was. Finally Flora MacDonald came into the room and recognised the bard. He was hospitably entertained by her and her husband. Before he left he sang the song, and received, in token of their appreciation, five pounds from each of them. If this was sterling, as our informant insists, the gift must have been large even for those munificent days. The mention by one of the domestics of the recent death of Clanranald shows that MacCodrum’s visit took place in the spring of 1766 (Clan Donald, iii, 235). The song was perhaps composed for the occasion. (Cf. Naidheachd XIV.)

2095-2096. Cf. ll. 1375-1376. It would thus appear that MacCodrum was still living at Langash in 1766.

2097. MacCodrum seems to have composed more songs while he lived at Langash than during any other period of his life.

2117. <gai>Clann Mhic Cùibein:<eng> nothing is known of this clan, though the name appears in the records of the Privy Council. They were perhaps notorious for niggardliness.

2122. Cf. the following:

<gai>Gura mis’ tha fo sprochd,
Càch mu t’fhearann a’ trod,
Is nach suidh thu air cnoc g’an réiteach.<eng>
(Glenbard Coll., 137.)

The Gaels held their assemblies in the open on certain knolls used for the purpose from time immemorial. The idea here is the same as that of the word <gai>eireachdail,<eng> which originally signified “fit to be seen in an assembly.” (Cf. Skene, Celtic Scotland, iii,

[TD 293]

390-391; Carmina Gadelica, ii, 247.) There is a similar idea in the Irish proverb, <gai>Dána gach fear go tulaig,<eng> “Every man is bold until he is at a public assembly” (O’Rahilly, A Miscellany of Irish Proverbs, 25).

2143. This was the old Gaelic ideal of manly comeliness. Deirdre wished for a husband whose hair was the colour of the raven, whose cheeks were the colour of blood, and whose skin was the colour of snow. <gai>(Lá n-aon tharla d’ a h-oide laogh do mharbhadh lá sneachta ré proinn dise; agus iar ndortadh fola an laoighe san t-sneachta, cromais fiach dubh d’ a h-ól, agus mar thug Deirdre sin d’ a h-aire, adubhairt le Leabharcham, go madh mhaith lé féin fear do bheith aice ara mbeidís na trí datha ad-chonnairc, mar atá dath an fhéich ar a fholt, dath fola an laoighe ar a ghruadhaibh, agus dath an t-sneachta ar a chneas.<eng>—Rel. Celt., ii, 424.)

2148. Cf. l. 1694 ff.

2157. <gai>far na féille:<eng> like his renowned ancestor, <gai>Domhnall mac Iain mhic Sheumais,<eng> who is reputed to have been the first to ferry cattle between Uist and Skye, Allan MacDonald was a great drover. In that connexion it is told how his continued success in this occupation aroused the jealousy of other drovers in Skye. They picked a quarrel with him in a certain inn when their numbers were sufficient—Allan was a remarkably powerful man—and handled him severely. A young man, who is said in Uist to have left MacDonald’s employ after having had a difference with him, heard the news in Edinburgh, and regretted that he had not been at Allan’s side to help him when he was attacked. Taking the air and chorus of an older song, <gai>Ailein Duinn nach till thu ’n taobh-sa,<eng> he composed a song which is still to be heard in the Isles, beginning:

<gai>’S truagh nach robh mi ’s an tigh-òsda
’N am nan dòrn a bhith ’g an dùnadh.<eng>

The names of some of the offending drovers are mentioned. One of them was the famous <gai>Màrtuinn Mór a’ Bhealaich,<eng> who is said to have been Allan MacDonald’s chief rival in Skye as a wrestler, and to have finally defeated him owing to his advancing years, Martin being much the younger man. It is stated that the threats contained in the song were carried out at the next fair, which the author attended for that purpose.

2158. <gai>Do chéile:<eng> Flora MacDonald; the line apparently refers to her health.

[TD 294]

2159. <gai>loinn mhic Iain:<eng> the line of <gai>Domhnall mac Iain mhic Sheumais,<eng> who was Allan’s great-great-grandfather. The founder of this branch of the MacDonalds was Donald’s grandfather, James MacDonald of Castle Camus <gai>(Seumas a’ Chaisteil),<eng> son of <gai>Domhnall Gruamach,<eng> Chief of Sleat (Clan Donald, iii, 499).

2175 ff. MacCodrum was probably thinking particularly of Domhnall mac Iain mhic Sheumais (cf. l. 2159 n), who was a famous warrior and the hero of the battle of Carinish, in which a body of MacLeods who invaded North Uist in 1601 was cut off almost to a man. One of the best known of Uist waulking-songs was composed to him by his foster-mother, <gai>Nic Còiseam.<eng> His exploits are well known in Uist and Skye, and have appeared in various publications.

2183 ff. To praise a chief’s wife was a convention of the bards with which MacCodrum was no doubt familiar. In this case some reference to the celebrated Flora was almost inevitable, and MacCodrum’s tribute to her, though short, is well expressed. It may be compared with one in a different vein by a bard who was present at her wedding:

<gai>Fhionnghail chaomh choibhneil
’S tu sgàthan gach maighdinn
’S an reul iuil tha toirt soillse dhaibh
Dh’oidhche ’s a lò;
De Chlann Domhnaill da-rìribh
An rìbhinn ghlan òg,
’S gur àilleagan ciatach
An rìbhinn ghlan òg.<eng>
(Celtic Magazine, i, 308.)

2195. <gai>Aonghus Og:<eng> Angus MacDonald, first of the family of Milton, and Flora’s grandfather. He received the tack of Milton from John, twelfth Chief of Clan Ranald. His wife was Mary, daughter of Maclean of Boreray (Clan Donald, iii, 279-280).

2196. <gai>Raghnall Mór:<eng> Ranald MacDonald, first of Benbecula, and Flora’s great-grandfather. He was the fourth son of Allan, ninth Chief of Clan Ranald, and was known in his day as <gai>Raghnall mac Ailein mhic Iain.<eng> He died in Canna in 1636 and was buried at Howmore (Clan Donald, iii, 277-278).

[TD 295]

<gai>MARBHRANN DO SHIR SEUMAS MACDHOMHNAILL

Do (’n) Shir S(h)eumus (cheadna) an Diaidh a Bhais,<eng> M.

It is supposed that Sir James never fully recovered from the effects of the accident with which he met in 1761 (v. notes on Oran do Shir Seumas). In any case his constitution was never very robust, and ill-health obliged him to go to Italy in the winter of 1765. Despite the change of climate his illness grew worse and he died in Rome on 26th July 1766, in his twenty-fifth year. MacCodrum’s tribute to his memory was one of many, for his death caused a deep sense of loss wherever he was known in this country and abroad (Clan Donald, iii, 97 ff.).

2211 ff. None of Sir James’s predecessors, for a number of generations, had attained old age.

2217. <gai>dà fhichead:<eng> between 1695 and 1746 the Sleat family lost five chiefs; and the same number between 1718 and 1766. MacCodrum’s figure seems to be an approximate reference to the former period, for Sir James’s death is mentioned as an added misfortune in l. 2219.

2235. <gai>an uiridh:<eng> it was probably the beginning of 1767 before the news of Sir James’s death reached North Uist.

2249. <gai>nighean Ionnsa:<eng> one of the persons whose incredulity and contempt Ossian had to suffer in his old age. There is a poem called <gai>“Ninghin Iunnsa”<eng> in M. (No. 109) of which a version entitled <gai>“Suireadh Oisein air Eamhair aluinn”<eng> appears in G. (11). The reminiscences of the aged bard are addressed to her:

<gai>“Is Cuth duine far nach Fionduin
Deirimse riutsa nighean Iunnsai,
Gu’n raibh mi ’m dhea’ laoch air bheirt eile,
Ge to mi ’m sheann laoch san latha-s’.”<eng>

2252. According to tradition Ossian lived on long after the rest of the Fiann and survived to see the advent of St Patrick. Some of the Ossianic ballads take the form of dialogues between the two. <gai>Oisean an déidh na Féinne<eng> is a well-known proverb. It is also current in Ireland <gai>(Oisín d’ éis na Féine).<eng> References to the tradition are common in the literature of both countries. MacCodrum expresses the parallel metaphorically in M. Editors of the later texts rendered it as a simile. The metaphor is more

[TD 296]

characteristic of Gaelic (Rel. Celt., i, 258, 381, 412, etc.; Stokes and Windisch, Acallam na Senórach, Irische Texte, Fourth Series, i; O’Rahilly, A Miscellany of Irish Proverbs, 142, 145; Gregorson Campbell, The Fians, 78, 82 ff.).

2261. Absalom: see 2 Samuel, xiii-xix.

2270. <gai>Rìgh Dàïdh:<eng> v. l. 2261 n.

2279. MacCodrum probably heard Gaelic renderings of incidents from the Iliad and Odyssey from friends versed in the classics, such as Hugh MacDonald of Baleshare and Donald Roy his brother. It is probable, also, that Sir James talked about the heroes of Greece and Rome when he foregathered with the bard to listen to his recitals of Ossianic poetry.

2305. <gai>rìgh Seòras:<eng> George III.

2315. <gai>Lùdhais na Frainge:<eng> Louis XV (1715-1774). Sir James was a prominent figure at his court when he stayed in Paris during his grand tour.

<gai>am Pàpa:<eng> the pope at the time was Clement XIII (1758-1769). Though Sir James was a Protestant, the Pope sent to enquire for him every day during his last illness in Rome.

2318. <gai>Fionn: Fionn mac Cumhaill,<eng> leader of the Fiann, and hero of the later cycle of heroic tales and ballads. Cf. l. 1005 n. For modern Island tradition regarding him see Gregorson Campbell, The Fians, 16-28.

2319. <gai>Osgar:<eng> son of Ossian and grandson of Fionn. Regarded as the best warrior of the Fiann <gai>(ceud làmh fheum na Féinne).<eng> For modern Island tradition see Gregorson Campbell, loc. cit., 29-48. <gai>Laoidh Osgair<eng> is one of the best known of Ossianic pieces.

Diarmaid: v. l. 1007 n.

2320. Goll: v. l. 1007 n.

2322-2323. Cf. l. 1899 ff. n., l. 1903 n.

2324. <gai>ùghdar:<eng> this word seems to connote something more than oral tradition. If MacCodrum could not read written histories like the Books of Clan Ranald for himself, it seems likely that he frequented the society of those, like Neil MacVurich, who could and did. This Neil MacVurich (mentioned in l. 2560) learnt to read and write history and poetry from his uncle, Donald MacVurich. His son was illiterate (Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141).

2326. <gai>Maol-Ciarain:<eng> the author of a poem lamenting his son, a young bard named Fearchar, who visited Ireland to make a

[TD 297]

<gai>cuairt<eng> and was slain there. A Scottish version of the poem is printed in Reliquiæ Celticæ (ii, 332). It was probably known to MacCodrum (cf. l. 629a n). Maol-Ciarain is often mentioned in a similar context by the bards, e.g. <gai>Iain mac Thearlaich Oig:

Och ’s e dh’fhàg mi mar Oisean,
Is mar choltas Maol-Ciarain.<eng>
(GB., ii, 195.)

Cf. Knott, Introduction to Irish Syllabic Poetry, 96-97. For further references see BGh., 296-297.

A poem by Fearchar Ó Maoilchiaráin appears in O’Rahilly’s Dánta Grádha, No. 14; and also (according to one source) in his Measgra Dánta, No. 75.

2333. <gai>na bràithrean:<eng> Alexander, who succeeded Sir James, and who was raised to the peerage in 1776, and Archibald, who became Lord Chief Baron of the Exchequer, and from whom is descended the family of East Sheen (Clan Donald, iii, 475).


<gai>ORAN DO ’N TEASAICH

Oran a roinnidh don Teasich, air fonn Dàbhi groisgach crom ciar,<eng> E. <gai>Oran Teasaich le Ioin M‘Codrum,<eng> CC.

When MacCodrum was laid low with fever cannot be determined. The corrupt and perhaps curtailed form in which the song has been preserved would tend to indicate that it is one of his earlier compositions. The classical allusions, on the other hand, seem to place it in the same period as the lament for Sir James MacDonald.

2341. The true reading seems doubtful.

2344-2345. Cf. l. 2279 n.
