2372. The bard is thinking of an oar, of which the blade is <gai>liagh<eng> and the rounded part <gai>lunn.<eng> His emaciated legs would resemble the former rather than the latter.

2383. How he was wearing both a <gai>boineid<eng> and a <gai>currac<eng> at the same time seems difficult to understand unless the currac was in the nature of a biretta to cover his baldness. Rob Donn has a song about a bald man with the chorus:

<gai>Gaol currac, gràdh currac,
Gaol currac, currac mhaol.<eng>

[TD 298]

One of the verses is:

<gai>Thog na nigheanan collection
A chur deis’ air Iain maol;
’S gur h-e na rinn an t-airgead uile
Gruag is currac agus cìr.<eng>
(Morrison, Songs and Poems of Rob Donn, 329.)


<gai>ORAN DO MHAC ’IC AILEIN

Oran do Mhac Mhic Ailein, ann an Eiric orain Iain Ghrudair,<eng> S. <gai>Oran do Mhac-ic-Ailein no Cuideachadh Iain Ghrudair,<eng> UB. The latter is a shorter independent version, and that MacCodrum was the author is quite certain.

The tradition is that an individual of somewhat unbalanced mind known as <gai>Iain Grùdair,<eng> who harboured among other delusions an unshakable belief in his own poetic ability, strung together some miserable rambling verses in praise of Clanranald, and repeated them in a certain house in Uist when the bard, lying half-asleep on a bench, was among the company present. When Iain Grùdair’s effusion came to an end, MacCodrum roused himself and sang this song.

2400. <gai>Aisling caillich mar a dùrachd<eng> is a proverb. It is also known in Ireland, one form being <gai>Aisling na caillídhe mar budh sáimh le féin,<eng> and the wish has been expressed in verse as follows:

<gai>Iarraim mo bheith óg arís,
iarraim ar Chríost mo bheith deas,
iarraim na hiomairí do-chíd
óigfhir im aghaidh do dhul as.

Im chosaibh go dtige lùth,
m’fhiacla go rabhaid go dlúth ró-gheal,
go ndírighe mo dhrom arís,
agus trí trír d’oigfhearaibh ag tnúth lem ghean.<eng>
(O’Rahilly, Dánfhocail, no. 91 n.)

2414. <gai>an tighearna òg:<eng> the subject of the song was Ranald MacDonald, eighteenth Chief of Clan Ranald, who had shortly before the date of the song succeeded to the dignity, on the death of his father, which took place on 6th March 1766 (Clan Donald, iii, 235). He was the father of <gai>Iain Mùideartach<eng> to whom the <gai>Tàladh<eng> was composed.

[TD 299]

2429. <gai>o Eirinn:<eng> the Clan Donald was numerous in Ireland, the best known family being that of Antrim (Clan Donald, iii, 199, 202, 408).

2434 ff. References to the badge and arms of a chief or clan are common with the bards.

2438. <gai>do chinneadh féin:<eng> the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald.

2446. <gai>Clann Domhnaill:<eng> here apparently used to denote the MacDonalds of Sleat.

2454. <gai>fir Chnòideart:<eng> cf. l. 1729 n.

2462. <gai>Clann Iain:<eng> cf. l. 1883 n.

2470. <gai>na Ceapaich:<eng> cf. l. 1879 n.

2478. <gai>Clann Donnchaidh:<eng> the Robertsons. Agnes, daughter of Ranald Bane, fifth Chief of Clan Ranald (died 1509), married Robert Robertson of Struan (Clan Donald, iii, 229). This (and ll. 2486 ff., 2494, 2502) gives an indication of MacCodrum’s knowledge of Clan Ranald genealogy.

2480. The families of Struan and Lude were the oldest and most important in the Robertson clan.

2486 ff. The Clan Ranald family formed ties of kinship with the Stewarts at an early period. Reginald, the founder (died 1386), married a daughter of Walter Stewart, Earl of Atholl. Allan, his son and successor, married according to MacVurich a daughter of Stewart of Appin. John of Moidart, eighth Chief, is said to have had a daughter from Penelope Erskine who married John Stewart of Appin (Clan Donald, iii, 226, 227, 231).

2494. <gai>na Leòdaich:<eng> Ranald Bane, fifth Chief of Clan Ranald, is said to have married a daughter of Roderick MacLeod of Lewis, probably as his second wife. John, twelfth Chief, married (1613) Marion, daughter of Sir Rory Mór MacLeod of Dunvegan. The mother of the chief celebrated in the song was Margaret, daughter of William MacLeod of Bernera, son of Sir Norman MacLeod of Bernera (Clan Donald, iii, 229, 232, 234).

2502. <gai>na h-Ailpeinich:<eng> several clans—MacGregors, Grants, Mackinnons, MacAulays of Ardincaple, MacQuarries, Macnabs—claimed descent from Kenneth MacAlpin. In this case the reference is to the fact that the subject of the song married in June 1759, as his second wife, Flora, daughter of John Mackinnon, younger of Mackinnon (Clan Donald, iii, 235).

[TD 300]

<gai>na Catanaich,<eng> the reading in UB., could be defended on genealogical grounds. Roderick, third Chief of Clan Ranald, married as his second wife Marion, daughter of William Mackintosh, Captain of Clan Chattan. Ranald Bane, fifth Chief, married Catherine, daughter of Lachlan Mackintosh of Gellovie (Clan Donald, iii, 227, 229).


<gai>MARBHRANN DO CHAIPTEAN FEARGHUSTAN<eng>

Captain John Ferguson, the subject of this mock elegy, was the second son of William, son of George, fourth son of William Ferguson of Baddifurrow, who represented Inverurie in the Restoration Parliament (1660). His father lived at the Mill of Inch in Aberdeenshire. In 1746 he was in command of the ship Furnace, and scoured the Western Isles for Prince Charles and any who had supported him; no one was too insignificant to be beneath his notice. In the same year, at the recommendation of the Duke of Cumberland, he was promoted to the command of a new frigate called the Nightingale. He again appeared in the Isles in 1753 in command of the sloop Porcupine, actively engaged in patrolling the coasts and preventing the return of the rebel chiefs, who were rumoured to be preparing for a fresh insurrection. Soon afterwards he was appointed regulating officer in the same station. In 1758 he was in command of the Prince of Orange and took part in the expedition against Louisburg under Admiral Boscawen. In 1762 he commanded the same ship in the Channel fleet under Sir Edward Hawke and the Duke of York. At the conclusion of the war he was appointed to the command of the Firme and later to that of the Prince of Orange. He died on 13th June 1767. His exploits against the French indicate that he was a seaman of considerable ability.

MacCodrum may have seen him in June 1746, when he and General Campbell crossed to North Uist from Bernera, accompanied by a body of soldiers, and marched round the west of the island and on to Carinish. The bard perhaps saw him when he passed through Hougharry. It is said that Ned Burke, then in hiding in Eaval, was one of the persons he was looking for. Burke had a narrow escape. He went to buy a pair of shoes at Cladach Carinish, and while he was there the Government force arrived in the village with General Campbell and Captain Ferguson at their head. The fugitive had barely time to hide his Highland clothes under a chest and don an old black coat and a pair of old breeches; whereupon he boldly left the house

[TD 301]

and mixed with the soldiers, afterwards unobtrusively making his escape to the cave in Eaval where he was hiding. His clothes were subsequently restored to him by his brother Jacob and a shoemaker’s wife who had been bringing him food. Captain Ferguson’s only arrest during his progress through North Uist on this occasion was Neil MacAulay, an itinerant tailor, who, he said, “was to be employed by Barrisdale to undertake the murder of him” (Lyon in Mourning, i, 115, 123-124, 197, ii, 99, iii, 84; Royal Celtic Society, Spanish John, 52 ff.; Scottish History Society, The Prisoners of the Forty-five, iii, 18-19; New Stat. Acc., xiv, 316; Records of the Clan and Name of Fergusson or Ferguson, 243-245, 278-281, 499; MC., lxiii-lxiv).

Numerous instances of Captain Ferguson’s unnecessary severity and violence in the Isles could be cited from the above sources. The sentiments expressed by MacCodrum are interesting as showing the extraordinary hatred which his actions incurred. It seems, indeed, that the “Black Captain,” as he was called, was more heartily disliked by the Jacobites than anyone else except the Duke of Cumberland.

2511. <gai>An Cuan Sgì<eng> is the Little Minch between Skye and Uist. See Watson, Celtic Place-Names of Scotland, 39.

2517. Cf. Joshua xxiii, 13, <gai>“… bithidh iad dhuibh … ’n am bioraibh ’n ur sùilibh.”<eng>

2518. <gai>Ruaidheabhal:<eng> there are two hills of this name in Uist. One (406 ft.) is the highest point in Benbecula; the other (286 ft.) is situated east of Drimore in South Uist. The former is probably intended here.

2521. <gai>Iònah:<eng> see Jonah i, 17 - ii, 10.

2522-2523. It is recorded that when the Prince arrived at Morar and Borodale he found the houses “burned by Captain Ferguson” (Records of the Clan and Name of Fergusson or Ferguson, 279).

2534. <gai>an tìr ud thall:<eng> possibly Eigg and Canna, where Ferguson’s outrages were seen at their worst. Alexander MacDonald, the bard, sent an account of the matter to Bishop Forbes (Lyon in Mourning, iii, 84).

2536. <gai>Cruachan Beann:<eng> the famous mountain (3611 ft.) in Argyll, near the north end of Loch Awe. Literally, “Cruachan of Peaks”; it has five peaks. Cf. Watson, Scottish Verse from the Book of the Dean of Lismore, xxvii.

[TD 302]

2538. <gai>Eubhal:<eng> the highest peak (1138 ft.) in North Uist, situated in the south-east corner of the island. In its shadow MacCodrum spent his latter days.

2540. Evidently an implied comparison with flesh at the bottom of a pot.

2542. <gai>Teach an Triubhais:<eng> now known as <gai>Beinn Chorghadail<eng> (1723 ft.). Its older name is accounted for by the ridges which jut out in a south-easterly direction, called <gai>Cas bho Thuath<eng> and <gai>Cas bho Dheas.<eng> An old Uist rime says:

<gai>Géideabhal is Teach an Triubhais
Dà bheinn as àirde ’n Uibhist.
Càit’ an d’fhàg thu Heacla bhuidhe?
Fàgaidh mi i cuide riutha.

Géideabhal<eng> also has gone out of use; the mountain in question now has the much less interesting name <gai>A’ Bheinn Mhór.<eng> It is the highest peak (2034 ft.) in Uist, lying on the south side of Teach an Triubhais. Hecla, on the north side, is the second highest (1988 ft.).

2546. <gai>Lì bho Dheas:<eng> cf. l. 758 n. There is a very deep loch on the summit of this hill in what looks like the crater of an extinct volcano. A local rime, quoted by Dr Carmichael (Carmina Gadelica, ii, 299) says:

<gai>Loch Foghail sin ’s Loch Foghail
Loch as doimhne fo ’n domhain:
Ach tha seachd doimhne Loch Foghail
An lochan dubh domhain Lì.<eng>

2550. <gai>Beinn MhicMhuirich:<eng> its position is doubtful. It is most probably another name, whether older or not, for <gai>Beinn an Tairbeart<eng> (549 ft.) near the head of Loch Skiport. This at all events is the view of well-informed residents in the vicinity, who say that the hill was part of the out-run of Stilligarry when the lands were occupied by the MacVurichs.

2554. <gai>shìos ud:<eng> i.e. in Stilligarry.

2555. <gai>Luchd dhèanamh nan dàn:<eng> i.e. the MacVurichs, who had occupied the position of historians to the family of Clan Ranald since the fall of the Lordship of the Isles. They claimed descent from <gai>Muireadhach Albanach O Dálaigh,<eng> a bard who flourished in the thirteenth century, and whose more immediate descendants were bards to the Lords of the Isles. After coming

[TD 303]

to Uist they held Stilligarry and the four pennies of Drimsdale in virtue of their office. Tradition says that the grant was a verbal one, and that its terms (which are of a similar nature to those quoted for the MacQueens in l. 716 n.) were binding until one of the family was tricked into declaring at the Mercat Cross of Edinburgh, <gai>“Is mise MacMhuirich a mach a Staghlaigearraidh.”<eng> They lost their office and lands in MacCodrum’s time (cf. l. 2560 n.). The family possessed a large number of manuscripts, of which the Black Book and the so-called Red Book of Clan Ranald and some fragments in the National Library of Scotland are still extant. Tradition refers to one member of the family as <gai>MacMhuirich Mór<eng> and attributes to him supernatural powers, several instances of which are related (Rel. Celt., ii, 138 ff.; Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141).

2558. <gai>Niall Mór:<eng> son of Lachlan and grandson of Donald MacVurich. He was bard to the Clan Ranald family at the beginning of the seventeenth century. There are two poems by him in the Red Book of Clan Ranald, one of them being in praise of the hospitality of Sir Rory Mór MacLeod. It is told in the Isles how, while being lavishly entertained at Dunvegan, MacLeod’s bard (O Muirgheasa?), leading a leash of dogs, found MacVurich in a sorry state overcome by his potations and said:

<gai>Tha ’m bàrd Uibhisteach gu tinn,
’S e air a dhruim an tigh an òil,
’S ge b’e a thug dha an deoch
Thug e lòn do choin Mhic Leoid.<eng>

To which MacVurich, raising himself on his elbow, made reply:

<gai>Thug thu alladh air Mac Leoid,
’S dhomhsa cha bu chòir a chleith,
Nach fhaigh a chuid chon de lòn
Ach na nì luchd an òil a sgeith.<eng>

G. (291) attributes to him a well-known mock genealogy of the pipes, but it seems possible that in this instance he is confused with Neil MacVurich who was an old man in 1715 (Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141; Rel. Celt., ii, 284, 290).

2559. <gai>Domhnall Geàrr:<eng> son of Lachlan MacVurich and grandson of Niall Mór just mentioned. In 1633 he received a wadset of the lands of Balmeanach and Gerihorornish in South Uist from

[TD 304]

John MacDonald of Clan Ranald (Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141; Clan Donald, iii, 123).

<gai>Niall:<eng> son of the foregoing. He was the author of the part of the Red Book of Clan Ranald which deals with the Montrose wars and subsequent events. His early recollections went back to the reign of Charles I. He was a very old man at the time of the rising of 1715 and composed two songs in the vernacular on the wounding and death of Allan MacDonald of Clan Ranald in that year. There are several poems by him in the bardic style in the Red Book of Clan Ranald (Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141; E., 73, 75; Rel. Celt., ii, 244, 248, 264, 286, ? 295, ? 297).

2560. <gai>Domhnall eile:<eng> son of the foregoing. He seems to have taken over the lands of the family during his father’s lifetime, and it was evidently in his time that they ceased to be held entirely rent free, for in 1707 in a tack of Stilligarry and Drimsdale from Allan MacDonald of Clan Ranald to Donald MacVurich, “indoweller in Stelligarry,” a rent of £10 Scots was exacted. He was living in 1722 and probably considerably later. He may be regarded as the last of the MacVurich bards. There are two poems in the Red Book of Clan Ranald which may be by him (Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141; Rel. Celt., ii, 274, 281).

<gai>Niall:<eng> son of Lachlan, brother of the foregoing. He learned to read and write the Irish character from his uncle, but is described as not a “man of any note.” It was he who in 1760 gave to James MacPherson the book the identity of which has caused so much controversy. He saw the total abandonment of the office of his ancestors after 1745, but received a small life pension of £33, 6s. 8d. Scots from Clanranald (the Clan Donald evidently confuses him with his son Lachlan). It is very probable that MacCodrum knew him and derived some of his literary and historical knowledge from him, if not also from his uncle. The next line (2561) is apparently an indirect allusion to MacCodrum’s own appreciation of their lore (Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Rosg Gàidhlig, 139-141; Rel. Celt., ii, 140; Clan Donald, iii, 123).


<gai>BANAIS MHICASGAILL<eng>

This song the bard composed on his experiences during and after a wedding which he attended in the Benmore district of South Uist. The name MacAskill is rare in Uist, where it is

[TD 305]

regarded as pertaining to the regions of Harris and Skye. It would seem probable, in view of this fact, that it was the same wedding which was attended without invitation by Angus Smith <gai>(Aonghus mac Caluim),<eng> nicknamed <gai>Am Fuamhair,<eng> the butt of Archibald MacDonald <gai>(Gille na Ciotaig).<eng> Angus suffered the customary penalty for his breach of convention, and Gille na Ciotaig, who may or may not have been at the wedding himself, composed a song upon his discomfiture in which he makes him say:

<gai>’S e tobhta MhicAsgaill
Is cleasachd a’ cheàird
A chuir saighead mo bhàis
Air mo ghiùlan,
Bho ’n thachair an tuiteamas
Tubaisteach dhomhsa
Mo chur ann am ònar
Am punnd O!<eng>

If this identification is correct, then it is possible that MacCodrum attended a wedding so far from home in virtue of a relationship between his wife, Mary Robertson, and the bride, whose mother apparently it is who is referred to in the song just quoted as <gai>Nic Rob.<eng> Robertson is another name uncommon in Uist and the families who bear it are related to each other. MacCodrum’s song was composed upon his return home after an absence more prolonged than his wife thought strictly necessary (UB., 147-149; MC., xxii-xxiii).

The song was a favourite of the late Alexander MacDonald of Balranald.

2568. <gai>an Gleann:<eng> i.e. Glen Ushinish. At the head of the Glen, between Hecla and Teach an Triubhais, is Loch Corodale. The unbidden guest at the wedding, <gai>Aonghus mac Caluim,<eng> who lived according to MC. at <gai>Beul na h-Uamha (? Maoladh na h-Uamha)<eng> in the Benmore district, is referred to in the song by <gai>Gille na Ciotaig as Fuamhair a’ Ghlinne.<eng> At that time ninety families dwelt in Glen Ushinish. When South Uist was sold to an alien proprietor they were all swept away to make room for sheep. The only habitation now is a shepherd’s cottage.

2579. <gai>Buntàta:<eng> South Uist is said to have been the first part of the Highlands in which potatoes were grown. Ranald MacDonald of Clan Ranald saw the root in Ireland while visiting his kinsman, Alexander, fifth Earl of Antrim, and brought back a shipload to Uist, which he ordered his tenants to plant. Only after being

[TD 306]

imprisoned did they consent to do so, and when the crop was raised they deposited it at the chief’s gate, saying that though he could make them plant the strange roots he could not make them eat them. That was in 1743, and within ten years, despite this unpromising reception, potatoes became the staple article of diet (Walker, An Economical History of the Hebrides, i, 251).

2586-2587. It will be noticed that Jacobitism has already fallen into the background.

2592. <gai>Mac ’ic Ailein:<eng> either Ranald, seventeenth Chief of Clan Ranald, or Ranald, eighteenth Chief; probably the latter. Clanranald’s health would no doubt be drunk after the King’s as he was the local proprietor.

2592-2593. <gai>an t-àrmunn Dubhghlasach:<eng> Archibald James Edward Douglas (formerly Stewart), son of Colonel (later Sir) John Stewart of Grandtully, Bart., and Lady Jane Douglas. Lady Jane had married unknown to her brother, the first Duke of Douglas, and her son was born in Paris on 10th July 1748, while she and her husband were travelling on the continent. On the death of the Duke in 1761 the tutors appointed by him immediately had his nephew served heir to the estates. The services were unsuccessfully disputed by the Duke of Hamilton, the heir-male, who alleged that Douglas was a supposititious child. The result was the famous “Douglas Cause,” which began in the Court of Session in 1762 and lasted until 15th July 1767, when the Duke of Hamilton won by the casting vote of the Lord President, Robert Dundas of Arniston. The judgment was so unpopular that the Lord President’s life was for a time in danger. Douglas appealed to the House of Lords and obtained a reversal of the decision in February 1769. This was the signal for wild jubilation and tumultuous uproar all over Scotland, for the case had from the first attracted widespread interest, which grew in intensity as the legal struggle was drawn out to such inordinate length. In Edinburgh a mob collected and clamoured for a special illumination in honour of Douglas’s victory. Shouting “Douglas for ever,” they broke the windows of Dundas, and of Miller, the Lord Justice Clerk, and attempted to break into their houses. Similar attentions were paid to the houses of the Duke of Hamilton’s friends and of those who refused to illuminate their windows. This lasted for two nights, and the military had to be called out before order was restored. From MacCodrum’s allusion it would appear that enthusiasm for Douglas’s cause spread even as far as

[TD 307]

the Western Isles. The song is probably not earlier than the period between the decision of the Court of Session and the rejoicings at its overthrow by the House of Lords, for the allusion could hardly be topical in Uist until the later stages of the case. Douglas died in 1827 (Dict. Nat. Biog., xv, 287).

2604-2605. <gai>an ruidhle dhùbailte:<eng> the eightsome reel.

2615. <gai>an Gleann:<eng> cf. l. 2568 n.

2622. <gai>Coire na h-Eitigh:<eng> this corrie is situated on the north side of Hecla. MacCodrum apparently had an acquaintance there with whom he tarried on the way home. The name is now usually pronounced <gai>Coire na h-Eitich (éiteach<eng> = the roots of burnt heather). The earlier form of the genitive appears in the song.

2635. <gai>fear a’ chinn léith:<eng> at the time of the song MacCodrum must have been about seventy-five years old.

2636 ff. The nature of the bard’s alleged misdemeanour would no doubt be indicated by the two missing lines. It was apparently regarded by his wife as deserving of the ecclesiastical penalties.


<gai>BEANNACHADH-TIGHE FIR BHAGHASDAIL<eng>

This song was composed during one of MacCodrum’s apparently frequent visits to South Uist. The house which evoked the bard’s blessing was Kilbride House, the residence at the time of the song of Colin MacDonald of Boisdale, who built it (l. 2686). It was situated not far from Poll a’ Charra, overlooking the Sound of Barra, and was demolished about thirty years ago.

2642. <gai>an t-àrmunn:<eng> Alexander MacDonald of Boisdale, father of Colin. Alexander was the son, by his second marriage, of Donald MacDonald of Benbecula, afterwards sixteenth Chief of Clan Ranald (cf. l. 2648), and was born in 1698. In 1745 he refused to join the Prince, but helped him later on when he was a fugitive in the Isles. He fell under suspicion, and was arrested. The Presbytery of Uist drew up a declaration testifying to his loyalty, and that effected his release. Boisdale was a man of great physical strength, and was, according to Hugh MacDonald of Baleshare, “as able a boulman as any in Scotland.” He amassed considerable wealth, chiefly in the cattle trade. To tradition he is known as <gai>Alasdair Mór nam Mart,<eng> and many stories are told in which he figures. He is said, for example, once to have sought the hand of the daughter of MacLeod of Dunvegan in marriage.

[TD 308]

The lady’s two maids, so the story goes, seeing Boisdale’s boat approaching, and fearing that if his mission were successful their services would no longer be required, went to meet the voyagers in the guise of crows (cf. the stories of Iain Garbh Mac Gille-Chaluim of Raasay’s death) with the intention of drowning them. It transpired, however, that Boisdale’s helmsman was Lachlan MacVurich, and the MacVurichs are credited in story with the power of counteracting the <gai>sgoil dubh<eng> at every turn. And so, to the continual croak of the one witch, <gai>“Bàdh Alasdair nam Mart! Bàdh Alasdair nam Mart!”<eng> the other could only say:

<gai>“Cia mar as urrainn mise
Alasdair nam Mart a bhàdhadh,
’S Lachlann Dubh mac Dhomhnaill MhicMhuirich
’S e ’n a ghurraig air an stiuir?”<eng>

The story goes on to tell how Lachlan wounded one of the birds with silver shot and later healed her in human form in Dunvegan. In such strange ways are the well-known names of the past sometimes perpetuated in the Isles. The historical basis of the story, if any, is hard to find, unless it be that the suitor was not successful. At all events Alexander, though married three times, did not contract an alliance with the MacLeods: he married (1) Mary, daughter of Donald MacDonald of Castleton; (2) Margaret, daughter of Hector Maclean, thirteenth of Coll; (3) Anne, daughter of MacNeil of Barra. No doubt a romantic voyage may have preceded one or more of those marriages. Alexander MacDonald of Boisdale died in 1768. The present song may be dated not long after that (Clan Donald, iii, 291-294, 664; Lyon in Mourning, ii, 95 ff).

2654. The first four quatrains dealt with Alexander MacDonald. The next six, beginning here, are in praise of Kilbride House.

2679. <gai>Cailean:<eng> Colin MacDonald of Boisdale, the builder of the house, whose praise begins here. Like his father, he was a man of great business acumen, and enlarged the family fortune considerably. His prosperity is indicated by the fact that he purchased the estate of Ulva and other lands in Mull. He was married twice, first, to Margaret, daughter of Donald Campbell of Airds, and, secondly, to Isabella, daughter of Lieut. Robert Campbell, of the 99th Regiment, afterwards of Glenfalloch, and sister of John, sixth Earl of Breadalbane. Colin MacDonald of Boisdale died on 31st July 1800 (Clan Donald, iii, 294-297).

2683. This evidently refers to some proverbial saying.

[TD 309]

<gai>MARBHRANN DO UISDEAN A’ BHAILE SHEAR

Marbhrainn Uisdein Bhaile-shear,<eng> M.

Hugh MacDonald of Baleshare, the subject of this elegy, was one of the leading men of North Uist in his day. He was the eldest son of Ranald MacDonald, who became tacksman of Baleshare early in the eighteenth century, and who was factor for North Uist before Ewen MacDonald of Vallay. Hugh succeeded to Baleshare on his father’s death in 1742. Rev. Allan MacQueen, in the Old Statistical Account, credits him with the introduction of kelp-making in North Uist. According to this account one Rory MacDonald came to Uist from Ireland at Hugh’s invitation. Because this individual’s first experiments were unsuccessful and only resulted in the reduction of the seaware to ashes, the local inhabitants, always inclined to take any opportunity of ridiculing new ideas, conferred upon him the sobriquet of <gai>Ruairidh na Luatha.<eng> This occurred in 1735, and it seems possible, therefore, that Rory’s sponsor was not Hugh but his father. (It may be noted that the Clan Donald puts the beginning of kelp manufacture in North Uist as early as 1726.) Though he was of Jacobite sympathies, Hugh MacDonald did not join the Prince in 1745. He was actually in command of a company of Militia, stationed at Kyleakin, while the rising was in progress. After Culloden he secretly took up the Prince’s cause, and was able to assist him in various ways, acting as an intermediary between him and Lady Margaret MacDonald. On 10th June 1746, he and Alexander MacDonald of Boisdale were among those who visited Charles Edward in his but at Corodale to enjoy the society of the Prince over the flowing bowl for three days and three nights. Hugh MacDonald was later one of the correspondents of Bishop Forbes, and sent him an account of the Prince’s sojourn in Uist. His death, at the age of sixty-three, took place in 1769, as MacCodrum mentions in the dating verse of his elegy (ll. 2759-2762). He was buried at Kilmuir. Over his grave is a table-stone, measuring nearly 7 feet long by 4 feet wide, supported upon pillars and bearing “various devices in relief, the most important of these being a shield charged with the arms of MacDonald of Sleat—quarterly: 1, a lion rampant; 2, a hand in armour, holding a cross-crosslet fitchée; 3, a lymphad (or galley) sails unfurled and oars in action; 4, a salmon naiant;—while lower down is a group of conventional emblems including a coffin and skull, an hour-glass, cross-bones, shovels, and scythes. Round the edge of this table-stone, and almost immediately below

[TD 310]

the shield, are traces of an incised inscription, now undecipherable …” (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 305; Clan Donald, iii, 142, 538; Lyon in Mourning, ii, 94; MC., xxi-xxii; Beveridge, North Uist, 294).

2691. <gai>Sìol Chuinn:<eng> the MacDonalds claim descent from <gai>Conn Ceudchathach<eng> (Conn of the Hundred Battles) whose name was handed down in the early Irish poetry and traditions, but of whom nothing certain is known. According to the Annals of the Four Masters (seventeenth century) his period was the early part of the second century. The term <gai>Sìol Chuinn<eng> is common in bardic verse.

2704. <gai>Fear Bhàlaidh ’s a mhac:<eng> Ewen MacDonald of Vallay (v. l. 869 n.) and his son, William, who was married to Mary, daughter of Alexander MacDonald of Boisdale (v. l. 2642 n.), with issue, including Alexander, his successor, and Mary, who married Rev. Allan MacQueen (v. l. 2794 n.) (Clan Donald, iii, 543).

2705. <gai>Fear an Uird:<eng> his name, according to the next line, was Archibald. This does not seem to fit any tenant of Ord in Skye who died at the same time as the two MacDonalds of Vallay; and it is difficult to see in any case why MacCodrum should refer to a member of the clan from Skye in this context. It seems highly probable that the reading in M. is here at fault and that what MacCodrum actually said was <gai>Fear an Dùin,<eng> which fits the case exactly. Archibald MacDonald—eldest son of Rev. Angus MacDonald <gai>(Am Ministeir Làidir),<eng> third son of John MacDonald <gai>(Iain mac Uisdein),<eng> fifth in descent from Domhnall Hearach—lived at Dunskellar <gai>(An Dùn),<eng> and died without issue in 1767. He had been for some years factor for North Uist. The date of his death fits MacCodrum’s verse, which speaks of deaths which took place within six months of that of Sir James MacDonald, or possibly within six months of the receiving of the news in Uist (Clan Donald, iii, 484).

2708. <gai>Iain:<eng> probably John MacDonald of Kirkibost (cf. l. 1220 n.).

<gai>am Bàillidh:<eng> Alexander MacDonald of Balranald (see notes on <gai>Marbhrann do Alasdair mac Dhomhnaill<eng>).

2714. Cf. Archibald Grant:

<gai>O bhroilleach Chloinn Domhnaill
D’ am bu chòir an làmh dhearg.<eng>
(Songs and Poems, 70.)

[TD 311]

2722. <gai>Iarla Dhùn Tuilm:<eng> Hugh MacDonald’s father was a natural son of Sir James Mór, second baronet of Sleat. The title <gai>Iarla<eng> is no doubt an echo of the title of Earl of Ross which the Lords of the Isles once held. Rev. Allan MacQueen mentions in the Old Statistical Account (xiii, 309) that the family of Sleat were regarded as the representatives of the old Earls of Ross. The style <gai>Iarla Ile<eng> was also applied to them by the bards. In a song to Domhnall Gorm Og Iain Lom has the verse:

<gai>Aig ogh’ Iarl’ Il’
Agus Chinn-tìre
Rois is Innse Gall.<eng>
(E., 172.)

2738. Cf. the proverb, <gai>An teanga leam-leat na earb aisd’.<eng>

2771. An address to the Deity or the Saviour was a convention at the end of bardic poems of this nature. MacCodrum follows that tradition here.


<gai>MARBHRANN DO NIALL MACGILL-EATHAIN<eng>

MacCodrum composed an elegy to Neil Maclean, merchant at Kersivay, Lochmaddy, but only these few lines appear to be extant. It has been stated that Neil Maclean was factor for Sir Alexander (later Lord) MacDonald in North Uist, apparently after the death of Archibald MacDonald of Dunskellar. (Beveridge thinks he was more probably only a ground officer.) But the Neil Maclean who was factor in North Uist was still alive in 1773, and cannot therefore be identified with the subject of this elegy. Neil Maclean was of the Boreray family. He died, according to MacCodrum, in 1769 (Clan Donald, iii, 539; MC., lx; Beveridge, North Uist, 84; Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, Second Series, 302, 304).

2779. <gai>Maighstir:<eng> this designation of a layman seems unusual; perhaps it was in virtue of his being a merchant. It was generally applied to ministers who were as a rule strictly entitled to it as holders of the degree Magister Actium. At the present time it seems to be out of use among Protestant denominations except in the case of the parish minister of North Uist. (Cf. Naidheachd XV, n.)

2784. Cf. l. 2738.

[TD 312]

<gai>FÀILTE DO ’N CHLÉIR<eng>

This welcome to the Presbytery by MacCodrum appears, without his name and apart from his other compositions, in M. (No. 49). It is still repeated in the Isles, and said to be by MacCodrum. The internal evidence of the lines discloses a combination of circumstances which shows the tradition as to their authorship to be correct.

The Presbytery was thus greeted by the bard upon the occasion of the admission of the Rev. Allan MacQueen as minister of the parish of North Uist at Kilmuir on (Friday) 28th September 1770. At that time the usual amicable relations between Protestants and Roman Catholics in Uist were disturbed by differences between Colin MacDonald of Boisdale, who was a Protestant, and his Roman Catholic tenants. Before the meeting of Presbytery was constituted (cf. ll. 2791-2792, 2801) the situation was no doubt being discussed in conversation by the ministers and others assembled. At all events, one of the ministers, according to tradition, said to the bard, <gai>“Siuthad, Iain, thoir dhuinn rann ag càineadh nan sagart.”<eng> John responded with this address to the Presbytery; it will be observed, however, that he wisely avoided involving himself in religious controversy (Uist Presbytery Records; Clan Donald, iii, 266).

2793. <gai>an t-athair:<eng> Rev. Donald MacQueen. See l. 863 n.

2794. <gai>a mhac:<eng> Rev. Allan MacQueen, son of the foregoing, who was admitted as minister of the parish of North Uist on the day on which the lines were composed. He was born in 1742, and was educated at King’s College, Aberdeen, where he graduated M.A. on 2nd April 1764. He was licensed by the Presbytery of Uist on 9th December 1768, and ordained missionary in Benbecula on 29th March 1769. Next year he became minister of the parish of North Uist, as already mentioned, having been presented by George III in August. He married Mary, daughter of William MacDonald of Vallay, with issue, a daughter, Mary, born 19th December 1783 (subject of the song <gai>Màiri Bhòidheach<eng> by Alexander Stewart), and a son, Donald, captain in the Royal Scots, born 23rd December 1784. Rev. John Lanne Buchanan describes Mr MacQueen as follows: “Another valuable farm in North Uist is possessed by the reverend gentleman of Ty-Geary <gai>[Tigh a’ Gheàrraidh];<eng> who of all the tacksmen, clergymen, and gentlemen of the Western Isles of Scotland, is the largest and jolliest as well as one of the most hospitable and best natured.

[TD 313]

Never was the minister and tacksman of Ty-Gheary known to kick, beat, or scourge, or, in any shape, to lift his hand against his scallags in the whole course of his life. Were he not so well tempered a man, this moderation, not a little unusual in the Western Hebrides, might be ascribed to motives of self-interest; for a few blows, even with his naked fist, would break their bones to pieces, and render them for ever useless to himself and to others.” Elsewhere, when commenting unfavourably upon members of the Hebridean Kirk-Sessions of the time, he says: “But from these general remarks on the character and condition of the elders in the West Hebridean Islands, I must except those of North Uist, among whom are several respectable gentlemen, ornaments to the church. It is observed by a prophet, ‘As is the people, so are the priests.’ The converse of this, equally true, may, perhaps, be applied to ministers and elders. As is the minister, so is his Kirk-Session’:—for this is of his own modifying and chusing.” Mr MacQueen contributed a very good description of North Uist to Sinclair’s Statistical Account (1794), in which he displays an extensive knowledge of the various aspects of life in his parish at the time. He was also a competent writer of Gaelic, as may be seen from the testimony of Lachlan MacVurich, taken down by him and sent to the Highland Society of London, who published it in their Report on the authenticity of MacPherson’s Ossian. Rev. Allan MacQueen died at Tigharry on 8th December 1801 (Fasti Ecclesiæ, new ed., vii, 191; John Lanne Buchanan, Travels in the Western Hebrides, 41, 232; Highland Society Report, Appendix, 276; Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, Second Series, 304).

2795. <gai>trioblaid:<eng> Mr Edmund MacQueen, minister of Barra, and brother of the foregoing, complains of the difficulty of attending Presbytery meetings (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 339).

2801. <gai>mòisean:<eng> perhaps the motion to constitute the meeting.

2802. <gai>a’ bheinn:<eng> so written, the meaning is simply that he had to betake himself to the moor; the expression is common in Uist. Tradition, however, glosses this line: <gai>Aig an am so bha MacCodrum a’ fuireach ann an àite ris an abradh iad A’ Bheinn,<eng> apparently Eaval, where MacCodrum was living in 1770.

[TD 314]

<gai>ORAN DO NA FÒGARRAICH

Oran do Mhuinntir a chaidh do dh’America as na h-Eileanan Gaeleach. Air fonn: “Air tuiteam a’m’ chadal,” le Mac Codrum,<eng> S.

The above is printed in such a way as to give the impression that MacCodrum is (merely) the author of the song to the air of which this is sung, as is the case. The song is ascribed to MacCodrum in UB., and also by present tradition. The emigration which the bard deplores took place during the few years which preceded the outbreak of the American War. About 1770 there was a marked rise in rents all over the Highlands. The rents of North Uist, for example, were £1200 in 1763; in 1771 they amounted to £1800, and the rise was not entirely compensated by the rising price of kelp and farm produce. The change took place in 1769, in which year Sir Alexander MacDonald renewed the tacksmen’s leases for nineteen years. The result of higher rents was emigration on an extensive scale. Sir Alexander’s tacksmen formed themselves into a kind of company, and bought 100,000 acres of land in South Carolina, to which they emigrated en masse, taking many of their sub-tenants with them. Altogether, over two hundred people left North Uist between 1771 and 1775. Great numbers also left the MacDonald estates in Skye, including Flora MacDonald and her husband, Allan MacDonald of Kingsburgh. There was a similar exodus from the Clan Ranald lands of Benbecula and South Uist. The total number who left various estates in the West Highlands at the time ran into thousands. The following may be cited as an example: “In the year 1769 when Lord MacDonald made a general sett of his estate in Uist and imposed considerable augmentations on his tenants, the farm of Hosta was sett in tack for 19 years from Whit. 1771, to Hugh Macdonald and John Maclean, the then possessors, at the yearly rent of £24 sterling; but soon finding themselves unable to continue their possession, they sold off their whole stock, abandoned their farm, and emigrated to America, with many more of his Lordship’s tenants who were reduced to similar circumstances, at Whitsunday, 1773. In this situation Mr Neill Maclean, factor on the Island of Uist, communicated the proceedings of the tenants to Lord Macdonald. …” This emigration was a voluntary one on the part of comparatively prosperous farmers who had become discontented with their conditions of tenure, but it led to the forced emigration of poorer classes of the community who were much less capable of fending for them-

[TD 315]

selves abroad, for the old tenants were replaced by strangers whose chief consideration was how to put their farms to the most profitable use and how to work them most economically; the old patriarchal system, abolished in theory by act of Parliament in 1747, now came to an end in actual practice. The idea of stocking farms with sheep instead of cattle was first canvassed after the cattle plague of 1771, the motive being a desire for increased profits, partly on account of the greatly reduced number of workers that would be required (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 309, 317 ff., 324; Canon R. C. MacLeod, The Island Clans, 115-116, 128 ff.; Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, Second Series, 302).

2807 ff. Cf. ll. 1955-1958.

2810. <gai>ur càirdean:<eng> Rev. Allan MacQueen mentions as one of the reasons for the emigrations from North Uist between 1771 and 1775 the fact that the people received letters from friends who had emigrated several years before giving flattering accounts of North Carolina (Old Stat. Acc., xiii, 317).

2835. <gai>Mac Dhomhnaill:<eng> Sir Alexander MacDonald, ninth baronet of Sleat. He lacked his predecessor’s Gaelic outlook and could therefore be hardly as popular, but he was an efficient landlord from a business point of view. John Lanne Buchanan describes his policy towards his tenants as follows: “His Lordship has reversed the œconomy of his kinsman, Clanronald; for instead of dismissing the actual cultivators of the land, he has taken them under his own immediate protection, and settled them by dozens, in the room of one over-grown land-broker, or tacksman. Yet it is justice to mention, that Lord Macdonald did not expel the tacksmen, but only reduced their immoderate farms. His Lordship has been subjected to much unmerited obloquy. His tenants, according to their station, and in comparison of the sub-tenants of tacksmen, lived in a state of affluence. It is also to be observed, that although, on the whole, his Lordship chuses to multiply industrious and contented husbandmen, rather than to support idle gentlemen, he has been known, in the choice of tenants, to give a preference to gentlemen of active turns already settled on his estate, before others, who made larger and more tempting offers for their farms. He is very attentive to the equal and prompt distribution of justice among his tenants.” A less favourable impression of his policy is to be derived from other sources.

In 1776 Sir Alexander MacDonald was raised to the peerage

[TD 316]

of Ireland with the style and title of Lord MacDonald of Sleat. Shortly afterwards he raised a regiment on his estates known as the 76th, or MacDonald’s Highlanders, which served with distinction in the American War. It was disbanded in 1784. In 1782 he stood unsuccessfully for Parliament as a candidate for the Inverness-shire constituency. On the outbreak of war with France he raised (1794) three volunteer companies in Skye and Uist for purposes of defence, and for relieving the regular army. Lord MacDonald was one of the best amateur violinists of his day and composed several pieces which have since become popular in the Highlands. He died on 12th September 1795 (Clan Donald, iii, 99-101; John Lanne Buchanan, Travels in the Western Hebrides, 29).

2855 ff. A North Uist bard of the present time expressed himself similarly at the time of the Great War:

<gai>Cha dèan cearcan fraoich no naoisg e
Tha ’n an laigh’ air aodann fàsaich,
Cha dèan féidh le ’n cabair chaola,
Cha dèan caoirich mhaola bhàn’ e:
Cuiribh iadsan fo ’n cuid fhaobhar,
Leigibh iad gu aodann nàmhaid,
Feuch an toir iad buaidh ’s a’ Ghearmailt,
Feuch an dearbh iad an làmh-làidir.<eng>

2929. MacCodrum’s forecast has proved correct with regard to the MacDonald lands in North and South Uist, which he no doubt had particularly in mind.


<gai>NAIDHEACHDAN<eng>

A large number of stories in which MacCodrum figures are in circulation in Uist. His name appears even in proverbs such as <gai>’S duilighe dhomhsa me throtan na do MhacCodrum a theann réis.<eng> The authenticity of many of the stories is open to grave doubts; some sayings are attributed to him now which can be shown to have had no connexion with him in the traditions of previous generations. (1) The well-known address to the drowned fly which is widely attributed to MacCodrum and commemorated in the proverb <gai>Cho cearbach ris a’ chuileig a chaidh am bainne MhicCodrum,<eng> seems to belong to this class. The older shennachies


(1) Since going to press Naidheachd VI has come under suspicion for this reason.

[TD 317]

ascribed this witticism to a person known to Uist tradition as <gai>An Gille Glas,<eng> who is identified in Skye—whether rightly or wrongly—with Donald Glas, an ancestor of the MacLeods of Drynoch. A number of other stories current in Uist have been omitted from the text because they come under suspicion on similar grounds. (Cf. Celtic Monthly, v, 38.)


III

Other accounts state that the encounter was with the Skye bard Mac-a-Leoir. It is possible that meetings of a similar nature both with MacDonald and with Mac-a-Leoir took place and became confused in tradition. (Cf. Introduction.)


IV
According to some accounts the voyage was to Arisaig. Further questions—<gai>Có as fear riaghlaidh air a’ bhàta?<eng> etc.—are sometimes added, but these and the retorts are also ascribed to others. According to some the exchanges took place between Alexander MacDonald and Duncan Bàn Macintyre when the latter was on one occasion coming off a boat at Tigh a’ Chreagain, Loch Aweside. Another and better-known version says that the replies were given to Macintyre by a young lad who ferried him across Kyle Rea during a tour made by him for the purpose of collecting names for his book (MT., xii, No. 22; Celtic Monthly, ii, 222).


V

<gai>Uachdar<eng> is a township in Benbecula beside the North Ford and opposite Carinish. Perhaps this should be told as two separate incidents.


VII

Some verses by Alexander MacDonald, the catechist, appear in UB. John Lanne Buchanan describes the order of catechists (whom he calls “questars”). The following apparently refers to Alexander MacDonald: “There is a blind bully of this order [of questars] in Uist, who, in order to escape contempt, and secure respectful attention both to his person and his doctrines, carries about with him, wherever he goes, loaded pistols. As he is remarkably strong, as well as full of courage, though blind, few people are fond of grappling with him” (John Lanne Buchanan, Travels in the Western Hebrides, 244).

[TD 318]

2942. <gai>Ma-ruibhe:<eng> Máel Ruba (640-722), who is invoked here, was abbot of Bangor in Ulster, but spent the latter part of his life in Scotland, founding the monastery of Applecross in 673. His name is frequently used for purposes of asseveration in Uist (Celtic Place-Names of Scotland, 287).


VIII

This is the best remembered of all MacCodrum’s sayings. Loch Hasten is a small loch—much larger before it was drained—near the rock of the same name <gai>(Creag Hàstan)<eng> to the west of Loch Sandary. Unlike Loch Sandary, which was drained by the <gai>Abhainn Mhór<eng> into <gai>Ceann a’ Bhàigh,<eng> Loch Hasten was drained towards the west.


IX

In this story MacCodrum appears as a practical joker rather than as a wit. Whether this was a frequent rôle with him is hard to say. Another story would seem to show that it was, but it is not well authenticated, and has, in view of its improbability (reminiscent rather of the exploits of <gai>MacCrùislig<eng> or <gai>Gill-easbuig Aotrom<eng> than of MacCodrum), been excluded from the text. It represents him as going to a cemetery situated on an island (? Vallay) to which a funeral was about to take place, putting off his clothes, and lying among the graves before anyone else arrived. The first to appear on the scene were two men who had charge of the whisky. On seeing them, he who lay among the graves suddenly stood up. The two men, after recovering somewhat from their fright, decided to offer a dram to the apparition. This was accepted, and so was a second, but the third was refused with the ambiguous remark, <gai>“Tha gu leòr eile a’ tighinn a so as mo dheaghaidh, agus fàgaidh mi an còrr acasan.”<eng>


XI

The name of one of the persons in this story should perhaps be written with a surname—<gai>Iain MacGille-Chrìosd.<eng> There are <gai>Clann Mhic Gille-Chrìosd<eng> still in North Uist, but they now pass officially as Macleans.


XIII

The name of the priest in this instance is not known. There was a priest in South Uist in MacCodrum’s time named O’Henley

[TD 319]

to whom he composed some lines. Efforts to trace these have so far been unsuccessful.


XIV

Cf. notes on Oran do Ailean Chinnseborgh.


XV

Patrick Nicolson was a native of Skye who came to Uist about the middle of the eighteenth century. He was a merchant in Benbecula, and in course of time (probably after MacCodrum’s death) became factor for Clanranald and tacksman of Torlum and Griminish, for which he paid, according to the Clan Ranald Rental of 1798, an annual rent of £100 Scots. John Lanne Buchanan has the following notice of him: “… Mr Patrick Nicholson, an industrious farmer and enterprizing merchant. Mr Nicholson, in his commerce with mankind, is as just and upright as any man in his line of life, and in a quarter so distant from the seats of law and government, can well be supposed to be. He is a great encourager of the industrious poor; and, though not a native of the place, is highly and justly esteemed by all ranks of people” (MC., xlix; Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, Second Series, 312-313; John Lanne Buchanan, Travels in the Western Hebrides, 40).

2968. The reference is to the fact that the Nicolsons were known as <gai>Clann Mhic Neacail a’ bhrochain.<eng> Cf. the following sayings:

<gai>Brochan Chlann Mhic Neacail
Tog ’n ad uchd e;
Lite Chlann Mhic Neacail
Tog air sop e.<eng>

and:

<gai>Clann Mhic Neacail a’ bhrochain
’S an droch aran eòrna,
Nam potagan mine
’S nan craigeanan feòla.<eng>
(Cf. Trans. Gael. Soc. Invss., xxix, 337.)
